<!-- pp. 1-20 -->

## Chapter 1: Introduction: The Theoretical Background

### 1.1 Development of Ideas about Demographic Cycles

**Malthusian Origins**

- Modern population dynamics begins with **Thomas Robert Malthus** (*An Essay on the Principle of Population*, 1798)
- Core mechanism: population growth beyond subsistence means → food prices rise, real wages decline, per capita consumption drops (especially among poor)
- Economic distress (famine, plague, war) → lower reproduction, higher mortality → slower population growth allows subsistence means to "catch up"
- Extended by **David Ricardo** (1817): theories of diminishing returns and rent
- Result: population numbers oscillate as they conflict with food availability

**Wilhelm Abel and Price History**

- **Wilhelm Abel's** *Agrarkrisen und Agrarkonjunktur* (1935) provided rich time-series data on prices, wages, rents, population movements in Western and Central Europe (13th-20th centuries)
- Grain prices (in grams of silver) showed wavelike movement: **three secular waves** identified
  1. Upward movement during 13th-early 14th century, followed by decline in late Middle Ages
  2. Upsurge in 16th century, followed by decline or equilibrium in 17th century
  3. Increase during 18th century, followed by irregular fluctuations in 19th century
- 20th century saw a fourth period of price inflation (**Fischer** 1996)
- Abel argued fluctuations in money circulation could not adequately explain long-term price trends
- Population moved in same direction as food prices, inverse ratio to wages (Abel 1980:292–93)
- Malthusian-Ricardian theory better explained data than monetarist theory
- Predicted progression: rents rise first, grain prices lag behind rents, industrial goods prices lag behind grain prices, workers' wages bring up rear

**The Dobb-Sweezy Debate**

- **Maurice Dobb** (1946): crisis of 1350–1450 caused by inefficiency of feudalism plus growing revenue needs of ruling class
- "Feudal lust for expanded revenue" driven by: growth of parasitic class size + increasing extravagance of noble consumption → intensification of feudal pressure → peasantry destroyed (killed goose that laid golden eggs)
- **Paul Sweezy**: root cause was rapid expansion of trade from 11th century onward, bringing ever-increasing variety of goods to ruling class (Sweezy et al. 1976:38–39)
- **Robert Brenner** (1974) critiqued both: Malthusian-Ricardian theories couldn't adequately explain long-term economic growth and income distribution trends
- Brenner argued theory ignored ("abstracted away") social structure — most important part being surplus-extraction relationship between direct producers and ruling class

**Brenner's Challenge to Malthusianism**

- Different European societies starting from similar conditions after **Black Death** followed divergent trajectories
- Serfdom disappeared from Western Europe (England, France) but made strong comeback in Central Europe (Poland, Prussia)
- Different property structures (landholding system) and balances of power (cohesiveness/organization of ruling class) → different paths after demographic catastrophe
- More damaging argument: observation of continued stagnation in most traditional European economies post-Black Death (Brenner 1985a:18)
- **England example**: Black Death removed one-third of population mid-14th century; by end of century population reduced to one-half of 1300 peak
- Malthusian logic predicted: drastic population decrease → higher agrarian productivity, low food prices, high real wages, resumption of vigorous population growth
- Reality: prices and wages largely in line with Malthusian predictions, but population stagnated for more than a century (growth resumed only late 15th century)
- Brenner: episodes of long-term stagnation only understood as product of established structures of class relations (1985a:18)
- Decline in direct producers reduced lords' income; to maintain income, lords extracted more from each peasant and dispossessed one another (brigandage, internal warfare) → disruption of production → further demographic decline, not return to equilibrium

**Post-Malthusian Synthesis Needed**

- Neither purely demographic nor purely class conflict explanations work well with data
- **Guy Bois** (1984): Malthusian model neglects important explanatory variable
- Need: synthetic theory encompassing both demographic mechanisms (with economic consequences) and power relations (surplus-extraction mechanisms)
- In dynamic systems framework, doesn't make sense to speak of "the primary factor" — factors interact dynamically, each affecting and being affected by the other

> "It is in the economy, in social relations and, even more fundamentally, in biological facts, rather than in the class struggle, that we must seek the motive force of history" — **Le Roy Ladurie** (1973), quoted in Hilton 1985:4

### Role of the State

- Brenner debate almost entirely ignored role of the state
- Marxists: state merely vehicle for conveying interests of ruling class
- Malthusians: focus on economic variables
- But significant movement among historical sociologists "to bring the state back in" (**Skocpol** 1979)
- States not simply created and manipulated by dominant classes; they are agents in their own right, competing with elites in appropriating resources from economy

**State Breakdown Cycles**

- Recurrent waves of state breakdown and political crises in European history: "calamitous" 14th century (**Tuchman** 1978), "iron century" 1550–1660 (**Kamen** 1971), "age of revolutions" 1789–1849 (**Hobsbawm** 1962)
- Each period preceded by sustained substantial population growth
- **Jack Goldstone** (1991): causal connection between population growth and state breakdown
- **Demographic-structural theory**: *demographic* because underlying driving force is population growth; *structural* because it's not demographic trend itself that directly causes state crisis but its impact on economic, political, social structures (Goldstone 1991:xxvi)
- Goldstone's work received highly positive verdict from historical sociologists (**Collins** 1993, **Wickham-Crowley** 1997, **Li** 2002)

**Are Secular Cycles Universal?**

- Increasingly clear that European societies subjected to recurrent long-term oscillations during second millennium CE (Braudel 1988, Cameron 1989, **Fischer** 1996)
- Concept not discovered by Europeans: **Plato**, **Aristotle**, **Han Fei-Tzu** connected overpopulation to land scarcity, insufficient food, poverty, starvation, peasant rebellions (**Parsons** 2005)
- Chinese traditionally interpreted history as series of dynastic cycles (**Reischauer** 1960, **Meskill** 1965, **Usher** 1989, **Chu and Lee** 1994)
- 14th-century Arab sociologist **Ibn Khaldun** developed original theory of political cycles explaining history of the Maghreb (**Inayatullah** 1997)
- Book examines hypothesis: **secular cycles** — demographicsocial-political oscillations of very long period (centuries long) — are the rule rather than exception in large agrarian states and empires

### 1.2 A Synthetic Theory of Secular Cycles

**Need for Integration**

- Brief review focused mainly on controversies between advocates of various processes as dominant influences
- In heat of debate, opposing sides simplify and caricature each other's views
- Clear that neither purely demographic nor purely class conflict explanations work well with data
- Synthetic theory incorporating both these (and other) processes may provide viable hypothesis testable with data
- Secular cycles only understood as result of interaction between several interlinked variables: **economic** (including demography), **social structure** (particularly elite-producing population-state interactions), and **political** (state stability or collapse)
- Explicit focus: **agrarian societies** (more than 50% of population, typically above 80–90%, involved in agriculture)

### The Demographic Component

**Carrying Capacity**

- Based on original insights of **Malthus** and **Ricardo**, further developed by neo-Malthusians like **Le Roy Ladurie** and **Postan**
- Key variable: **population density** in relation to **carrying capacity** of local region
- Concept developed by ecologists in context of logistic model (invented by **Paul Verhulst**, popularized by **Raymond Pearl**, Pearl and Reed 1920)
- **Carrying capacity** defined as: population density that resources of habitat can support in long term (for excellent discussion see **Cohen** 1995)
- *Resources* usually refer to food, though in some environments limiting resource may be water or fuel
- Carrying capacity = upper ceiling on population growth (from economics viewpoint: limit arises because labor inputs into production suffer from diminishing marginal returns)

**Boserup's Contribution**

- Carrying capacity affected by: physiographic features, year-to-year temperature/rainfall fluctuations, gradual climate changes
- Most important: affected by existing level of agricultural technology and by how technology is employed
- **Ester Boserup** (1966, 1981): population growth can have positive effect on economic innovation
- Boserup widely regarded as anti-Malthusian, but her insights and Malthus's can be comfortably combined within same general theoretical framework (**Lee** 1986, **Wood** 1998)
- Adverse effects of population growth on standard of living provide strong inducements for adoption of new means of production
- In agrarian societies, economic change can win only temporary respite from marginal immiseration (Wood 1998, **Clark** 2007a)
- Example: society approaching current limits can invest in clearing forests, draining swamps, irrigation, flood control → increase in carrying capacity
- But at some point no more forests to cut or swamps to drain; if population continues to grow, eventually will again press against Malthusian limits

**Dynamics of Population Approaching Carrying Capacity**

- As population density approaches carrying capacity: shortages of land and food, oversupply of labor
- Food prices increase, real wages decline, per capita consumption (especially among poorer strata) drops
- Economic distress → lower reproduction + higher mortality rates → slower population growth
- Should population density reach carrying capacity: just enough food to sustain and replace one individual; birth and death rates equalize, population density at equilibrium
- In actuality, other factors not taken into account by purely demographic model would preclude emergence of stable equilibrium

**Structural Effects of Population Growth**

- Population growth in excess of productivity gains has fundamental effect on society's structures
- Typical changes: high rents and land prices, increasing fragmentation of peasant holdings or high numbers of landless peasants, increased migration of landless peasants to cities
- **Urbanization** increases (proportion of population inhabiting towns and cities)
- Cheap labor → flowering of trades and crafts
- Demand for manufactures increases (elites profit from high rents on land and lower labor costs)
- Increased urbanization + conspicuous consumption by elites → promote regional and international trade
- Gap between well-to-do and poor grows
- In rural areas: overpopulation means no food reserves available in case of crop failure
- Years of poor harvest: hardly noticed in better times, now result in significant mortality and, at worst, catastrophic famines
- Chronic undernourishment → conditions conducive to spread of epidemics

**Urban Unrest and Elite Unity**

- Cities accumulate landless peasants and jobless artisans (growing ranks of paupers and vagrants)
- Food riots and wage protests become frequent
- Eventually: deepening economic misery → peasant and urban uprisings
- However: as long as elites united and state maintains control of military, such popular uprisings have small chance of success
- **Jack Goldstone** fundamental point:

> "It is a profound and repeated finding that the mere facts of poverty and inequality or even increases in these conditions, do *not* lead to political or ethnic violence (Gurr 1980, Goldstone 1998, 2002b). In order for popular discontent or distress to create large-scale conflicts, there must be some elite leadership to mobilize popular groups and to create linkages between them. There must also be some vulnerability of the state in the form of internal divisions and economic or political reverses. Otherwise, popular discontent is unvoiced, and popular opposition is simply suppressed." (Goldstone 2002a)

### Social Structure: Commoners, Elites, and Social Mobility

**The Surplus Curve**

- Important consequence of law of diminishing returns: amount of surplus produced by cultivators nonlinearly related to their numbers
- **Surplus** = difference between total production and what is needed for subsistence (minimum amount of resources needed to support and reproduce each peasant household multiplied by number of households)
- Resources needed for subsistence increases linearly with population; total product grows slower than linearly due to law of diminishing returns
- At certain critical population density (which we have defined as carrying capacity), two curves intersect
- This is point where surplus becomes zero (should population increase beyond carrying capacity, surplus becomes negative → peasant households don't get enough resources to reproduce themselves, population density must decline)
- Curve relating surplus produced to population density crosses zero both where population equals zero and where it equals carrying capacity; **hump** somewhere between these two critical points
- When population increases from low level, initially surplus increases (more peasants means more surplus)
- At some intermediate density, surplus reaches maximum (where effects of diminishing returns on labor inputs into agriculture begin to be felt)
- After that point, surplus begins to decline

**Elite Extraction and Landholding Systems**

- Surplus produced by peasants not made available to elites (and state) automatically
- Left alone, peasants would happily consume it themselves (or simply work less, "consuming" it as extra leisure time)
- How much of production ends up in hands of elites depends on many economic and political factors
- Important dynamic: elites usually able to extract larger amount of surplus during late stages of population growth
- Specific mechanisms depend on landholding system
- Oversupply of rural labor elevates rents → increases landowner's profits
- In **serfdom-based system**: lords can set level of extraction almost arbitrarily high
- Oppressed serfs have nowhere to flee (whole surrounding landscape at saturation level)
- Only alternative: life of vagabond or bandit (always been brutish and short)
- Most serfs have no realistic alternative to submission

**Elite Expansion During Stagflation**

- Free but landless laborers must secure employment to support themselves and families
- Oversupply of labor → depressed wages, chronic unemployment or underemployment for substantial part of population
- Employers (both rural and urban) profit greatly from this economic situation
- During late stages of population growth, when commoners suffering from economic difficulties, **elites enjoying golden age**
- Both reproduction of existing elites and recruitment of new elites from commoners fastest when amount of extractable surplus greatest
- Expansion of elite numbers should take place during **"stagflation" phase** (see below for definitions of phases of secular cycle)
- Fast-rising prices and land rents offer greatest opportunities for rapid accumulation of wealth by current and aspiring elites
- State fiscal problems lead rulers to increase sale of privilege and rank → accelerate social mobility into elite ranks
- Result: peak of elite numbers often lags behind that of general population (important exception: societies with widespread **polygyny** discussed in section 1.3)

**Declining Elite Incomes**

- Such happy state (for elites) cannot continue for long
- First: expansion of elite numbers means amount of resources per elite capita begins to decline
- Would occur even if total amount of surplus stayed constant
- Second: as general population grows closer to carrying capacity, surplus production gradually declines
- Combination of these two trends results in accelerating fall of average elite incomes

**Elite Psychology and Consumption Expectations**

- Dynamic processes also have sociopsychological aspect
- During good times: elites become accustomed to, and learn to expect, high level of consumption (the "growing extravagance of noble households" of **Dobb** and **Sweezy**)
- Additional element (pointed out by Sweezy): ever-increasing quantity and variety of goods available to elites as result of urbanization, growth of crafts, expansion of trade (factors themselves consequence of population growth)
- Modern studies of consumption level expectations: people generally aim at matching (and if possible exceeding) consumption levels of their parents (**Easterlin** 1980, 1996)
- What's important: not absolute level of consumption but level in relation to previous generation
- In other words, expected **"living standard"** is culturally determined **inertial** variable (inertial because it changes slowly, on generational time scale)
- If we can extrapolate results from studying modern consumers to preindustrial elites (at least, may be reasonable working hypothesis): during good times elites would easily become accustomed to elevated levels of consumption, expansion would occasion little social comment
- By contrast: should their level of consumption decrease in relation to previous generation's, elites expected to react vehemently to this development
- This argument suggests no contradiction between bitter critique of elites for their luxurious and wasteful way of life by contemporary social commentators and equally bitter complaining of elites themselves about their poverty and indebtedness

<!-- pp. 21-40 -->

### Economic Inequality and the Matthew Effect

- Elite incomes deteriorate unevenly during late stagflation — while most aristocratic lineages lose ground, a few grow richer
- **Matthew effect** (**Merton 1968**): poor aristocrats attempt to maintain elite status on inadequate economic basis, forcing them into debt and eventual asset sales (often land)
- Wealthier lineages maintain necessary consumption and acquire land from impoverished neighbors — "the poor get poorer while the rich get richer"
- Same dynamic operates among peasants: during hardship, most sell land or starve, while a small percentage of thrifty or lucky peasants concentrate land holdings
- Successful peasants translate wealth into upward social mobility demands — contributing to elite overproduction at end of demographic expansion
- Growing inequality creates pressure for social mobility (upward and downward), destabilizes society, and fuels radical ideologies of social justice and redistribution

### Dynamics of Surplus Extraction

- Declining aristocratic incomes lead to: (1) intensified peasant oppression, (2) increased intraelite competition for scarce resources
- Elites attempt to increase extraction through economic and **extraeconomic (coercive)** means — success depends on structural characteristics (military strength relative to producers, legal/cultural limits on extraction)
- Excessive extraction can cut into subsistence resources, causing negative population growth rates
- **Bifurcation point**: stage where secular cycle may follow alternative trajectories — example: serfdom disappeared in post-medieval England and France, but rose in Prussia and Poland simultaneously
- Structural characteristics or chance events determine which trajectory the system follows — essentially rephrasing in dynamical systems terms **Brenner**'s critique of **Malthusian theory**

### Stuart Borsch Case Study: England vs. Egypt After Black Death

- **Stuart Borsch (2005)** compared Black Death effects in England and Egypt
- **Post-Black Death England**: wages rose, rents and grain prices dropped, unemployment decreased, per capita incomes grew — economic recovery by year 1500 in full swing
- **Post-Black Death Egypt**: wages dropped, land rents and grain prices rose, unemployment increased — no economic recovery by 1500, agricultural output declined 68% between 1350–1500
- **Borsch** argues persistent stagnation in Egypt explained by structural factors:
  - After 1250, Egypt ruled by cohesive militarily capable **Mamluks** — specialized slave-warriors who repelled Mongol invasions (second half of 13th century)
  - English peasants could resist elites by hiding in hills and forests (abundant in depopulated England); longbow negated elite military advantage
  - Egypt's narrow strip of arable land between desert left no room for evasive tactics
  - Mamluks used tremendous coercive power to maintain preplague levels of resource extraction from greatly diminished rural population
  - Extremely high exploitation prevented demographic revival — system caught in "vicious equilibrium" stable with respect to internal perturbations, finally destroyed by external conquest (Ottomans 1517)

### Increased Intraelite Competition

- Second consequence of plunging elite incomes: increased intraelite competition depending on structural characteristics (state's capability to suppress overt violence)
- Elites facing declining agricultural incomes seek employment with church or state bureaucracy — training improves chances, leading to **"credentialing crisis" (Collins 1979)**: rapid expansion of educational enrollments
- Trends in higher education serve as index of intraelite competition (**Goldstone 1991:123**)
- Civil litigation levels also index intraelite conflict (**Goldstone 1991:120**)

### Bastard Feudalism and Factional Conflict

- Impoverished elites attach to powerful magnates' retinues — in 15th-century England known as **"bastard feudalism" (Dyer 1989:35)**
- Large retinue necessary to advance lord's interests in government, litigation, civil war
- Limits on available land, offices, royal patronage lead to polarized factional battles for spoils (**Goldstone 1991:119**)
- Elites lose unity, split along fission lines: new vs. old elites, religious factions, regional vs. center
- Not enough resources for everybody — certain segments become losers
- **Counterelites** or **dissident elites**: segments aspiring to elite status who end up as losers — usually don't constitute true sociological group (unified only by hatred for existing regime and desire to bring it down)
- Motivations not purely economical — late stagflation phase offers ideological justification for revolutionary action through harsh oppression and extreme inequality

### State Breakdown

- Demographic growth leads to: (1) expansion of armies and bureaucracies, (2) rising state expenditures, (3) increased aspirants for elite positions — further fiscal strain
- States must expand taxation despite resistance, but surplus production declines (previous section) and state competes with desperate elites for shrinking surplus
- Even with rapidly rising taxes, state headed for fiscal crisis
- Declining **real** revenues may be masked by persistent price inflation — important to express fiscal flows in real terms
- Population growth leads to: rural misery, urban migration, falling real wages, increased food riots and wage protests
- After lag time, negative population effects hit elites (increasing rivalry and factionalism)
- Rapid population growth expands youth cohorts — particularly impacted by lack of employment opportunities
- Growing economic inequality, elite competition, popular discontent fuel ideological conflicts — example: early modern Europe, dissident elites and dissatisfied artisans recruited into heterodox religious movements

### High Sociopolitical Instability

- End result: state bankruptcy, loss of military control, elite movements of regional/national rebellion, combination of elite-mobilized and popular uprisings after central authority breakdown (**Goldstone 1991:25**)
- Internal war among political factions is one aspect of increased interpersonal violence
- Breakdown of social order accompanied by: increased banditry, homicides, violent crimes
- Ideological level: social pessimism pervasive, state legitimacy at lowest point
- Society approaches condition appropriately called **"Hobbesian"** (**Hobbes** himself lived during such period)
- Collectively referred to as **high sociopolitical instability**

### The Effect of Sociopolitical Instability on Population Dynamics

- Two general (interrelated) ways instability affects population: (1) demographic rates, (2) productive ability of society (**Turchin 2003b:120–21**)
- Weak or absent state increases populace mortality due to: crime, banditry, internal warfare (civil war)
- External war effects change with secular cycle phase: strong state directs warfare outward (state frontiers, areas outside state suffer); state collapse reduces resistance to external invasion
- Internal warfare and external invasions blur (e.g., **Hundred Years' War** in France)
- Warfare has indirect effect on mortality: rebel or invading armies spread epidemics

### Migration and Disease During Times of Trouble

- Times of trouble cause increased migration: refugees flee war-afflicted or destroyed productive areas
- Migration effects: (1) adds to mortality, (2) birth rates decline (people on move can't afford children), (3) leads to epidemics
- Increased vagrancy spreads disease by connecting isolated areas
- Example: **Ireland 1810–44** (period before Great Famine): harvests failed/partially failed in 15 of 35 years — did not lead to starvation but followed by outbreaks of "famine fevers": typhus, dysentery, scurvy, cholera spread by beggars/vagrants seeking charity and employment (**Grigg 1980:138**)
- Army movements and expansion of international trade facilitate disease spread
- International trade expands in precrisis (stagflation) phase, then gradually declines after society descends into anarchy
- Rise of widespread epidemics most probable during late stagflation phase
- **Pandemic arrival is one of most frequent triggers of demographicstructural collapse**

### Local Population Density and Epidemics

- Vagabonds and beggars aggregate in towns/cities, increasing population size
- May tip local population density over **epidemiological threshold** (critical density above which disease spreads, below which it dies out)

### Political Instability Effects on Reproduction

- Political instability lowers reproduction rates due to lowered production capacity
- Peasants unable to weather short-term subsistence crises without organized surplus storage — accumulated stores easy prey for marauding armies and predators
- During uncertainty, people marry later, have fewer children — reflected in birth rates and infanticide rates (family limitation disguised as increased infant mortality)

### Effects on Productive Capacity (Carrying Capacity)

- Second and more important effect: sociopolitical instability affects **productive capacity** (carrying capacity)
- Vigorous states invest in increasing agricultural productivity: irrigation canals, roads, flood control, clearing forests, organizing colonization of underpopulated regions — increases cultivated area and land productivity
- State offers protection — in stateless society, people live only in natural strongholds or defensible places (e.g., walled cities)

### Examples of State Protection and Productive Land Use

- **Roman Empire**: overwhelming majority of Italian population in lowlands where most productive land concentrated; after Rome's collapse, settlements moved to hilltops (**Wickham 1981**)
- **Wanka hill fort chiefdoms** in Mantaro Valley of Peru (**Earle 1997**): prior to Inka pacification, Wanka lived in crowded hilltop fortresses; after conquest, population moved to lower elevations where best agricultural land located — diet and life span of both elite and commoner dramatically improved (**Johnson and Earle 2000:327**)
- **Thomas Basin**, Norman bishop, described northwestern France in 1420s after virulent **Hundred Years' War** outbreak:

> "a state of devastation such that from the Loire to the Seine, and from there to the Somme, the peasants having been killed or run off, almost all fields were left for a number of years not only uncultivated, but without people…. All that could be cultivated at that time in that region was only around and inside towns or castles, close enough so that, from the top of the tower or watchtower of the lookout could perceive the attacking brigands. Then, with the sound of a bell, or horn, or some other instrument, he gave all those working in the fields or vineyards the signal to withdraw to the fortified place." (quoted in **Dupâquier et al. 1988a:368**)

- Lack of effective suppression of internal violence imposes **"landscape of fear"** — large proportion of agriculturally suitable lands abandoned (too far from security)
- Strong state protects productive population from external and internal (banditry, civil war) threats, allowing whole cultivable area to be put into production

### Elite Dynamics during the Depression Phase

- Sociopolitical instability affects elite numbers similarly to commoners (specific mechanisms differ)
- Elites little affected by subsistence crises; escape epidemics more lightly (better nutrition, likelihood of better care during disease, higher mobility)
- Urban elites could withdraw to country estates at first sign of incipient epidemic (e.g., Boccaccio's *Decameron*)
- Higher nobility with estates in multiple provinces could avoid regional epidemic
- Elites ran higher risk of violent death due to active political participation

### Elite Death Toll Examples

- Battle of Poitiers (1356): **Dupâquier et al. (1988a:342)** quote **Philippe Contamine** estimate that ~40% of French elite slaughtered
- Battle of Agincourt (1415): same proportion killed
- Wars of Religion (late 16th century): 20,000 Huguenots killed in one day (**St. Bartholomew's Day massacre, 1571:39**)
- State purges: first Ming emperor purged 100,000 Chinese officials (**Tignor et al. 2002:62**)
- **Sulla**'s proscriptions eliminated 1/3 of Roman ruling class, senators; another 1/3 eliminated by proscriptions following Caesar's death

### Downward Mobility and Elite Decline

- Less spectacular but ultimately more important: downward mobility reducing elite numbers
- Plunge in elite incomes (begins in precrisis period, exacerbated by general population decline) affects most strongly lowest noble stratum
- Example from **Christopher Dyer** for late medieval England: esquire or gentlemen living on £10–20/year, employing only three servants, living in one house, meals devoid of luxury (wine/spices) had little room to maneuver when income plunged 50% mid-15th century:

> "They must have cut back, or even cut out completely, their occasional wine-bibbings, and avoided travel whenever possible, but too many economies of this kind might force them to drop out of the aristocracy and accept yeoman status" (**Dyer 1989:108**)

### Mechanisms Reducing Elite Surpluses

Six mechanisms reducing elite surpluses:
1. Deaths from civil war
2. Deliberate purges by new rulers
3. Limitations imposed on elite production (celibacy, primogeniture)
4. Downward social mobility (voluntary or state-forced)
5. Increased material resources from conquest or agricultural productivity improvements
6. Development of new political order directing greater share of resources to elites

- Several mechanisms usually operate in combination; specific mix depends on cultural peculiarities and historical accidents

### End of Instability and the Beginning of the New Cycle

- Three main factors driving sociopolitical instability rise: general overpopulation, elite overproduction, state insolvency
- All must be reversed before disintegrative phase can end
- Trend reversal occurs in variety of ways depending on: society characteristics, geopolitical environment, exogenous factors
- Last stages of secular cycle rife with **bifurcation points** — sociopolitical trajectory behaves nondeterministically

### Mechanisms of Population Decline

- Overpopulation problem usually "dealt with" during crisis phase
- Common proximate mechanisms: disease, prolonged civil war, external conquest of disunited society
- Not all population declines accomplished by catastrophic epidemics
- Finally, external conquest often results in demographic catastrophe

### Alternative: Carrying Capacity Increase

- Alternative to population collapse: increase in carrying capacity
- Overpopulation results not from absolute numbers being too large but from too high population density relative to carrying capacity
- Carrying capacity can increase from technological progress — probably happened in early modern England
- During 17th-century crisis, English population hardly declined while average grain yield per acre probably doubled — end result: twofold decline in population pressure on resources

### Conquest and Climate Change

- Carrying capacity may increase from conquest of new underpopulated territories
- Example: conquest of **Kazan and Astrakhan khanates** by Muscovy (16th century) opened vast areas along Volga for Russian colonization in succeeding centuries
- Theoretically, carrying capacity can increase from substantial climate amelioration (authors cannot point to well-documented, convincing example at this point)

### Processes Leading to Elite Reduction

- Manner of elite overproduction abatement depends on military strength of aristocracy
- Nonmilitarized ruling class can be expropriated en masse by warlords, rebel generals, peasant bandits
- Rapid, comprehensive elite turnover results in relatively short sociopolitical instability period following state collapse
- Apparently happened on several occasions during Chinese imperial period (ruling class dominated by literate administrative elites rather than military specialists)
- Rapid elite turnover can result when ready external source of potential elites exists (e.g., **Ibn Khaldun** cycles in Maghreb, discussed next section)

### Military Aristocracy Reduction

- Ruling class with preponderance of military power over internal and external rivals reduced only by internecine fighting between elite factions
- Can result in very prolonged periods of sociopolitical instability or "depression" phases

### Conditions for New Secular Cycle

- For new secular cycle to begin, pressures of general population on resources and elites on commoners must be substantially reduced from precrisis levels
- Third condition: not all societies capable of broad-scale cooperation required to construct functioning state
- Some societies with previous imperial history can lose this ability over time (**Turchin 2003b, 2006**)
- Entirely possible for civil warfare to gradually die out but centralizing, integrative trend failing to take hold
- Area may persist indefinitely (or until conquered) in fragmented state as collection of small-scale polities
- Potential explanations of this failure beyond book scope — simply indicate it's another possible bifurcation point

### Phases of the Secular Cycle

- Oscillatory dynamics don't go through truly discrete phases with clearly marked breakpoints
- For convenience, need to divide each secular cycle into phases
- Classificatory scheme understanding: transitions between phases rarely abrupt (dates usually rounded to nearest decade)

### Integrative and Disintegrative Trends

- Cycle broadly divided into two opposite trends: **integrative** and **disintegrative**
- Politically, integrative phase characterized by: centralizing tendency, unified elites, strong state maintaining order and stability
- Internal cohesion often results in vigorous prosecution of external conquest wars (may result in state territory extension if weaker neighbors available)
- Disintegrative phase characterized by: decentralizing tendency, divided elites, weak state, internal instability and political disorder (periodically flaring in civil war)
- External conquest wars much more difficult during disintegrative phase (if they happen, usually during intervals between civil wars, at expense of equally weak opponents)
- More frequently, external enemies profit from internal weakness, resulting in increased raids, invasions, loss of territory

### Population Trends

- Population tends to increase during integrative phase, decline or stagnate during disintegrative phase
- Climatic fluctuations, epidemics, or external enemy overrun can cause short-term population losses
- Vigorous population growth resumes as soon as exogenous forces stop acting
- During disintegrative phase, population losses due to epidemics, famines, wars not made up by sustained population growth
- Even when proximate Malthusian forces (epidemics, famines, wars) in abeyance, population often fails to increase despite being below carrying capacity

### Subphases: Expansion and Stagflation

- Useful to divide integrative and disintegrative periods into subphases
- Population growth particularly vigorous during first **expansion** phase of integrative trend
- Time of relatively stable prices and modest real wage declines (if any)
- As population density approaches carrying capacity limits, price increases or wage declines accelerate — **"stagflation"** or **"compression"** or **stagflation** (stagnation plus inflation) phase
- Although majority of commoners experience increasing economic difficulties during stagflation, elites enjoy golden age, numbers and appetites continue to expand

### Crisis and Depression Phases

- Stagflation phase (and overall integrative trend) succeeded by general crisis
- Expansion grades smoothly into stagnation; stagflation-crisis transition often (not always) abrupt
- Discrete events signaling crisis arrival: pandemics, extreme famine episodes, state collapse followed by intense civil war (or combinations)
- Crisis phase not discrete, brief event (one meaning of *crisis*) but extended period lasting one or more human generations
- Population decline during crisis results in plentiful per capita resources
- Doesn't necessarily end disintegrative trend: too many elites/elite aspirants, intraelite conflict continues generating internal instability
- Crisis grades smoothly into **depression** phase, characterized by endemic civil warfare
- Population may grow during intervals between intense civil wars, but increases typically don't last, followed by declines (not as catastrophic as crisis phase)
- Depression phase ends when elite ranks pruned by internal conflict to point where disintegrative trend can reverse, new secular cycle begins
- Alternatively, if no functioning state can get going, depression phase grades smoothly into **intercycle** of indeterminate length

### Classificatory Scheme as Ideal Type

- Scheme proposed is ideal type — helpful to indicate rough state of dynamical system with single word
- Considerable variation in trajectories followed by actual societies
- Boundary between phases should be taken as "fuzzy" rather than "hard"
- Early book reader suggested allowing phases to overlap (some merit, as different phases dominated by different social processes often overlapping in time)
- Example: onset of political crisis doesn't always coincide with shift from population growth to decline
- Dating stagflation-crisis transition may be problematic
- Authors chose to stay with nonoverlapping phases (otherwise too confusing to readers)
- Do not impose discrete phases on each case study in procrustean manner

## 1.3 Variations and Extensions

### Factors Affecting Characteristic Lengths of Secular Cycles

- General theory exposition (section 1.2) Western European-centric, but theory should be applicable to any agrarian society
- Focus on how structural and cultural characteristics affect demographic-structural dynamics, particularly average period of cycle

### Why Secular Cycles Are Not Strictly Periodic

- Secular cycles not periodic in strictly mathematical sense (each succeeding cycle doesn't exactly repeat preceding one)
- Although secular rises and falls are endogenously generated by interactions between agrarian state subsystems, macrosocial dynamics cannot be strictly periodic
- Three reasons:
  1. Nonlinear dynamic feedbacks can generate not only strictly periodic (cyclic in mathematical sense) dynamics but also **aperiodic chaos** — erratic-looking behavior produced entirely by internal, endogenous reasons
  2. Dynamics affected by exogenous forces (changes in geopolitical and ecological environment) — exogenous factors affect societal dynamics but not themselves influenced by societal dynamics
  3. Individuals possess free will, can act unpredictably — even single person's act, if in right place at right time, may influence whole society trajectory

### Stochastic Process and Brownian Motion

- Can model individual actions at microlevel as stochastic process (kind of **Brownian motion**) resulting in erratic, unpredictable changes in macrosocial trajectory
- Authors don't expect strict periodicity in secular dynamics
- Instead, dynamics should have average period (characteristic time scale) with substantial variation around this average

### Factors Determining Cycle Length

- Mean period of single boom-and-bust secular cycle determined by characteristic lengths of phases, which depend on various social, economic, political parameters
- Typical expansion phase length primarily determined by: (1) per capita rate of population increase, (2) population density relative to carrying capacity at cycle beginning
- Example: if population grows at 1% per year, takes 70 years to double (not bad estimate of typical expansion phase)

### Geopolitical Effects on Expansion

- Expansion phases affected by geopolitical environment
- States enjoy greatest ability to mobilize society for external conquest war during middle parts of integrative secular trends
- Abnormally long expansion phases result from successful territorial conquest (especially when accompanied by colonization of conquered territories, serving to reduce population pressure in metropole)

### Crisis Phase Length Unpredictability

- Crisis phase length much less predictable
- Definite biological limit on how fast human population can grow; no comparable limit on how fast it can decline
- Population can decline very rapidly (pandemic) or more slowly (incessant civil warfare)
- Pathogens afflicting historical populations varied in lethality
- Relatively mild pathogen could drive population down slowly (recurrent epidemics, resulting in long decline phase)
- Severe epidemic leads to very short period of drastic population decline and deeper degree of social disintegration and longer depression phase (e.g., post-Black Death Europe)

### Stagflation and Depression Phase Lengths

- Characteristic lengths of stagflation and depression phases depend more on state and elite dynamics than general population
- Military strength of elites has large effect on depression phase length (or even if such phase exists)
- Models tailored to Western European societies characteristics (largely monogamous elites enjoying military power preponderance over internal and external enemies) suggest typical secular cycle periods in range of 2–3 centuries (**Turchin 2003b:138**)

### Ibn Khaldun Cycles

- Very different situation in certain Islamic societies
- Paradigmatic example: sociopolitical dynamics in medieval **Maghreb**, brilliantly described by **Ibn Khaldun (1958)**
- From demographic-structural theory perspective, Maghrebin states differ from Western European states in two respects:
  1. Islamic societies permitted **polygyny**
  2. Ready source of militarily powerful counterelites nearby

### Polygyny and Elite Reproduction

- **Polygyny** important because number of wives is most significant predictor of male reproductive success in humans (**Betzig 1986**)
- Aristocratic males could afford to support several wives and concubines — rate of elite population growth in Islamic societies was (and is today) much greater than for elites in Christian societies
- True that some elite polygyny practiced in Western Europe (aristocrats increased biological fitness by having multiple mistresses, acknowledging bastards)
- Biological reproduction rate of Islamic elites was several times higher than Christian elites

### Desert Threat and Rapid Elite Turnover

- Second factor: location of Maghrebin societies in thin strip of arable land between Mediterranean Sea and desert
- "Desert" (dry steppe and semi-desert zone between Mediterranean littoral agrarian societies and extremely arid central Sahara regions) occupied by nomadic pastoralists, primarily **Berbers**
- Desert chiefdoms not significant military threat as long as agrarian states maintained internal cohesiveness
- As soon as Maghrebin society experienced state collapse, became extremely vulnerable to conquest from desert

### Ibn Khaldun Cycle Dynamics

- When demographic-structural models modified to account for these factors, exhibit very different dynamics (**Turchin 2003b**)
- High reproductive rate of elites means they increase much faster than general population
- Elite numbers increase so rapidly that commoner overpopulation plays much lesser role or no role in bringing about state collapse
- Result: integrative trend of secular cycle over much faster than in standard model (developed for Western European situation)
- Once collapse occurs, usually no lengthy depression phase (doesn't take much time to organize coalition of desert tribes to pick up pieces and establish new dynasty)
- Result of shortened integrative trend and missing depression phase: models predict much faster secular cycle for Maghrebin-type societies (order of one century rather than 2–3 centuries for Western European states)
- Prediction agrees with **Ibn Khaldun** observation that dynastic cycle in Maghreb extends, on average, over four generations (generation time in humans typically 20–30 years)
- Note: this is pure theoretical prediction; models not fitted in any way to Maghrebin data
- Shorter cycle period follows directly from structural assumptions of models of faster elite reproductive rate and rapid elite turnover after state collapse

### Ottoman and Mamluk Examples

- Not all Islamic polities predicted to exhibit Ibn Khaldun cycles
- Key parameter identified by theory: rate of growth of elite numbers
- Islamic societies that controlled elite growth rates in one way or another predicted to exhibit slower cycles, with periods similar to Western Europe
- Example: **Ottoman Empire** — sultans had access to essentially unlimited supply of wives and concubines; however, when old ruler died, only one son allowed to replace him, all others killed
- Furthermore, top levels of bureaucracy and army leadership recruited not from native elites but by means of **devshirme** (state, not biology, controlled size of high-ranking elite stratum)
- Only lower-rank landed elites permitted to increase "biologically," being not very wealthy, couldn't afford too many wives
- Result: secular cycles in Ottoman polity should be much longer than in Maghreb

### Mamluk Polity

- Even more extreme case: **Mamluk** polity in medieval Egypt
- Ruling class recruited entirely from slave markets
- Children of Mamluks could not be Mamluks, thus automatically dropped out of ruling class
- In principle, this arrangement should have stopped dead **Ibn Khaldun's** dynamic and, barring exogenous perturbations, led to stable equilibrium

### The Fractal Nature of Historical Dynamics

- Different social processes operate at variety of temporal scales
- Shorter scales include: daily, weekly, monthly, annual cycles
- Beyond that: human generations, processes occurring on time scale of centuries (including secular cycles), longer-term phenomena such as social and biological evolution

### Example: Stock Market (DJIA)

- Example: stock market as measured by **Dow-Jones Industrial Average (DJIA)**
- DJIA fluctuates on variety of scales: daily (exchange shuts down at night), weekly (no activity on weekends), annual (fiscal year accounting affects investor behavior), multi-annual (business cycles), multidecadal (**Kondratieff cycle**, though not all economists accept reality of such long cycles)
- DJIA trajectory looks "fractal" because amount of fluctuation depends on time scale at which trajectory viewed

### Time Scales and Smoothing

- If interested in understanding business cycle effect on stock prices, should ignore short-term fluctuations within single day, probably within week
- Time series for investigating multi-annual oscillations would probably use DJIA values averaged for each week
- **Averaging** is simplest kind of smoothing — smooths away all "uninteresting" short-term fluctuations from point of view of main question
- If we want to know how holiday periods affect stock price movements, would certainly retain within-week fluctuations, perhaps go down to hourly movements
- Point: different questions require approaching analysis at different time scales

### Population Dynamics Time Scales

- Turning to population dynamics: population changes occur on variety of scales
- Monthly (female menstrual periods)
- Yearly (subsistence and epidemic cycles)
- Generational (somewhere between 2–3 decades)
- Secular (1, 2, or 3 centuries, according to theory of secular cycles)
- If interested in dynamics of childhood diseases, appropriate time scale would be weeks or months (capture within-year course of each epidemic)
- Example: measles incidence begins to increase after children brought together at beginning of school year, gradually building up to peak in winter

### Appropriate Time Step for Secular Cycles

- If we want to understand how secular cycles unfold, certainly don't need to know weekly or monthly mortality fluctuates, or that there may be deficit of births nine months after Lent (devout Christians avoiding sexual intercourse)
- All such within-year or year-to-year fluctuations irrelevant for our investigation purposes
- Appropriate time step is one human generation
- Need to average over smaller-scale fluctuations
- Also need to do something about very long trends driven by social evolution
- Requires some kind of removal of millennial trends (**Turchin 2005:153**), as done for early-modern English population (see appendix to chapter 3)
- By smoothing within-decade fluctuations and removing millennial trends, retain two temporal scales of interest:
  1. Longer one is average period of secular cycle (what needs to be explained)
  2. Shorter one is human generation time (time step of dynamical process postulated to be explanatory mechanism of secular cycles)

### Population Number Inertia

- Important to remember: population numbers are dynamic variable with lot of inertia on temporal scales shorter than human generation
- Particularly true with respect to population increase: can occur only slowly as babies born and raised to enter adult population
- Even under ideal conditions, human population needs at least one generation to double
- On other side of demographic balance, mortality can theoretically collapse population to very low level (or extinction) in very short time
- However, most typically annual variation in death rates (due to crop failures) can be quite substantial but largely smoothed out — buffered — at level of total population numbers

### Climate Variability and Temporal Scales

- Buffering ability of total population numbers important in understanding how climate variability affects population dynamics
- Annual variation, even if quite extreme, may have little effect on population change
- If population well below carrying capacity, peasants may have sufficient stores to weather year or two of bad crops without demographic effect
- In contrast, long-term cooling (even by less than one degree centigrade) may have much more substantial effect on population dynamics by lowering carrying capacity
- Argument just illustration of why temporal scales important; in real world, climate change effect depends on: cycle phase, alternative crops peasants can switch to, many other factors

## Generation Cycles

- Preceding discussion should make clear: authors far from adopting monocausal view of human history
- Main hypothesis: demographic-structural processes very important in historical dynamics
- Would not argue they're only thing going on
- Not good research strategy to include everything one can think of in model
- **Albert Einstein** said: theory should be as simple as possible, but no simpler than that

### Fathers-and-Sons Dynamic

- One particular process not part of demographic-structural theory but must be taken into account when studying secular cycles: **"fathers-and-sons" dynamic** (**Turchin 2003b, 2006**)
- Mechanism operates during prolonged disintegrative secular trends characteristic of secular cycles in Europe
- Empirical observation: disintegrative trends not periods of continuous civil war; there are periods when sociopolitical instability particularly high, interspersed with periods of relative pacification

### Example: Late Medieval France (Hundred Years of Hostility)

- To illustrate: during disintegrative trend of late medieval France ("Hundred Years of Hostility"), good reigns alternated with bad ones
- Reign of **John II (1350–64)**: period of social dissolution and state collapse
- Reign of his son **Charles V (1364–80)**: time of national consolidation and territorial reconquest
- Next reign: **Charles VI (1380–1422)**: another period of social disintegration and collapse
- Followed by period of internal consolidation and national resurgence under **Charles VII (1422–61)**, which finally lifted France out of late medieval depression
- General dynamical pattern of alternation between very turbulent and relatively peaceful spells observed again and again during secular disintegrative phases

### Social Psychology Explanation

- Possible explanation of such swings in collective mood lies in social psychology
- Episodes of internal warfare often develop in ways similar to epidemics or forest fires
- At beginning of conflict, each act of violence triggers chains of revenge and counter-revenge
- With time, participants lose all restraint, atrocities become common, conflict escalates in accelerating, explosive fashion
- After initial explosion, violence drags on and on, sometimes for decades
- Sooner or later most people begin to yearn for return of stability and end to fighting
- Most psychopathic and violent leaders killed off or lose supporters
- Violence, like epidemic or forest fire, "burns out"
- Even though fundamental causes that brought conflict on in first place may still be operating, prevailing social mood swings in favor of cessation of conflict at all costs, uneasy truce gradually takes hold
- Those people, like generation of **Charles the Wise**, who directly experienced civil war become "immunized" against it; while they're in charge, keep things stable
- Peaceful period lasts for human generation (20–30 years)
- Eventually, conflict-scarred generation dies off or retires, new cohort arises (people who did not experience horrors of civil war and not immunized against it)
- If long-term social forces that brought about first outbreak of internal hostilities still operating, society will slide into second civil war
- Result: periods of intense conflict tend to recur with period of roughly two generations (40–60 years)

### Generation Cycles Definition

- Swings in social mood may be termed **"generation cycles"** because they involve alternating generations either prone to conflict
<!-- pp. 41-60 -->

### Other Long Cycles (continued)

- **Schlesinger cycles** (generation cycles) also noted by **Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.** (1986)
- **Birth rate oscillations**: 50-year periods in 20th-century America (**Easterlin** 1980, **Macunovich** 2002)
- **Kondratieff cycle**: economic indicators oscillating with ~50-year period (**Kondratieff** 1984)
- Kondratieff and Schlesinger cycles may be correlated and oscillate in synchrony (**Berry** 1991, **Alexander** 2002)
- Possible correlation with war cycles (**Goldstein** 1988)
- **Note**: Understanding of these cycles is deficient; many doubt their reality
- Authors focus only on **sociopolitical instability dynamics** — forced by need to understand multiple peaks in disintegrative phases

### Exogenous Forces

The demographic-structural model focuses on **endogenous forces** (internal feedbacks), but real systems also affected by **exogenous factors**:

- **Geopolitical environment**
  - Strong, aggressive neighbors exploit state weakness during disintegrative phases
  - Can deepen societal collapse
  - Worst case: state conquered/annexed (e.g., **Chinggis Khan's** empire rolling over studied states)
  - Alternatively: weak neighbors allow external conquests, relieving population pressure and providing outlet for surplus elites, lengthening integrative phase

- **Disease environment**
  - Pandemics like **Black Death** originate externally, spread across continents
  - Repeatedly arise within Eurasia (**Turchin** 2008)
  - Effect depends on population growth phase
  - Early-stage population: epidemic = minor interruption
  - Dense population: highly vulnerable, severe drop can trigger longer/deeper disintegration cycle

- **Social evolution**
  - Growth of agricultural technology affects carrying capacity
  - Significant crop yield increases elevate carrying capacity
  - Same effect on food prices/consumption as substantial population declines

- **Global climate**
  - Long-term temperature/rainfall fluctuations affect crop productivity and carrying capacity
  - Society already pressing on resources can be tipped into crisis by climate worsening

- **Singular historical events/accidents**
  - Example: **Discovery and colonization of Americas** by Western Europeans
  - Torrents of precious metals (especially **American silver**) flowing into Europe from 16th century
  - Acted as amplifier creating stronger, more inflationary growth cycle in 16th-17th centuries
  - **"Price revolution of the sixteenth century"** had two causes: monetary and demographic (**Fischer** 1996:74)

## 1.4 Empirical Approaches

### Four Fundamental Variables

Theory has four variables (in dynamical systems: **endogenous variables**):
1. **Population numbers** (relative to carrying capacity)
2. **Social structure** (elite numbers and consumption levels)
3. **State strength** (fiscal health)
4. **Sociopolitical instability**

Reciprocal interactions among these generate secular cycles. Goal: collect data on how each changed during study periods.

### Data Collection Challenges

**Ideal**: time-series data at regular intervals (decade or human generation of 20-30 years)

**Reality**: rarely achieved in historical applications

- **First problem**: substantial measurement noise
  - Not fatal — statistical methods estimate information content
  - Even worst case (directional trends only) can test model predictions

- **Second problem**: reasonable quantitative measures, but irregularly spaced points
  - Can be informative with qualitative indications between "anchor points"
  - Example: early modern England population estimates confirmed by later formal reconstruction
  - Statistical methods exist for irregularly-spaced time-series (not employed in this book)

### Proxy Variables

Often lack direct measurements — use **proxy variables**:

- **Climatologists** use tree rings, lake deposit varves, ice core isotopes for past climate
- **Archaeological records** valuable (e.g., **Morris** 2005)
  - Dwelling counts can indicate population dynamics
  - Example: **Novgorod** population revealed by leather shoe remains in cultural layers (**Nefedov** 2002)

> "Having quantitative data on the relative fluctuations of a variable is almost as good for testing theory. In fact, it is much better to have a time series on relative fluctuations than an excellent absolute estimate limited to one point in time."

- **Demographic-structural theory is about dynamics** — change with time
- Cannot measure change with single time slice

### Exploitable Data Sources

**Public building intensity** (temples, churches):
- Shows remarkable fluctuations over time
- Reflects resources available to state, elites, or both

**Coin hoard temporal distribution**:
- **Michael Crawford** (1960): close correlation between coin concentrations and internal war periods in Roman Republic (**Crawford** 1993:162)
- **Armstrong** (1998): similar pattern in late Carolingian period

**Cautions with proxy variables**:
- May not perfectly correlate with variable of main interest
- Example: people per building varies with time
- Coin hoards affected by monetization degree and time elapsed since burial
- Need careful treatment — but would be madness to ignore them

**Best practice**: Build explicit model of factors affecting proxy, estimate parameters, impute values of interest
- Statistical methods developed for natural sciences
- Example: inferring temperature from mercury expansion in glass tube
- Complex geophysical applications (e.g., locating underground oil)
- Not attempted in this book but possible for future

### Endogenous vs. Exogenous Variables

**Endogenous variables**:
- Part of feedback loops postulated by theory
- Dynamics determined by other endogenous variables (plus noise element)
- Influence how other endogenous variables change

**Exogenous variables**:
- Affect state of system but not affected by it
- Example: **real wage**
  - **Malthusian-Ricardian theory**: determined by population relative to productive capacity
  - Can be influenced by other variables (e.g., internal war disrupting grain production)
  - In turn influences demographic rates, then population change

**Table 1.1**: Empirical predictions of demographic-structural theory
- Lists endogenous variables and predictions for how they change across cycle phases
- Goal in following chapters: document dynamics of as many variables as possible

## Chapter 2: Medieval England — The Plantagenet Cycle (1150-1485)

### 2.1 Overview of the Cycle

**Secular cycle boundaries**:
- Bracketed by two periods of intense internal conflict:
  - **Reign of Stephen** (1138-53)
  - **Wars of the Roses** (1455-85)
- Period (1150-1485) spanned by **Plantagenet dynasty** (Lancastrian and Yorkist branches)
- End date (1485) probably uncontroversial
- Population regime changed from stagnation to growth at end of 15th century
- **Controversial**: sustained population growth may not have started until end of 12th century

**Case study presentation scheme**:
1. Demographics and economic variables
2. Social structure and elite dynamics
3. Political aspects
4. Examine variable interactions during each phase (expansion, stagflation, crisis, depression)

### Trends in Population and Economy

**Major population movements** (**Hatcher** 1977, **Hallam** 1988b, **Hatcher and Bailey** 2001, **Dyer** 2002):
- General growth to late 13th century
- Peak around 1300
- Slow decline during early 14th century, accelerating to collapse with **Black Death of 1348** and aftershocks
- Depression phase during most of 15th century
- First signs of recovery around 1480
- Sustained increase during 16th century (next cycle)

**Uncertainties**:
- Less known about precise peak magnitude (1300)
- More importantly: when sustained growth began

**Population reconstruction starting point**: **Domesday Book census of 1086**
- Lists ~275,000 persons (**Hatcher** 1977:68) — working-age males or household heads
- Must convert to total population
- Allow for omitted northern counties, major cities, unrecorded subtenants/landless men
- **Multiplier uncertainty**: how many people per household?
  - **Russell** (1948): 3.5 multiplier → 1.1 million England population (1086)
  - **Postan** (1966): argued for 2.5 million
  - Current: 4.5-5 multiplier more plausible (**Harvey** 1988:48)
  - Most quoted: 1.75-2.25 million range (**Hatcher** 1977:68)

**Second anchor**: **1377 poll tax**
- 2.5-3 million people in England (**Hatcher** 1977:68)
- 40-50% population drop between 1348-1377
- Some decrease from 1300 to 1348
- Peak population estimate: 4.5-6 million, "with balance pointing to higher reaches" (**Hatcher** 1977:68)

**Hallam's estimates** (1988b):
- Working forward from 1086 using manor holding records
- Assumed 4.7 people per household, 2 million 1086 population
- Generated Table 2.1 estimates
- Population/settlement expanded unevenly 1086-1300
- **Michael Postan** (1956): expansion most rapid to 1130, then stagnation
- **Langdon and Masschaele** (2006:63): same conclusion

**Evidence for mid-12th century population decline**:
- Data fragmentary but suggestive
- Possible decline during mid-12th century from Stephen-Matilda civil war
- **Gesta Stephani**: villages "standing lonely and almost empty," unharvested fields, perished/fled peasantry (**Miller and Hatcher** 1978:x)
- Tax revenues during early Henry II shrank 25% vs. 1130
- Not just revenue disruption — officials reported productive lands "waste"
- Fiscal machinery fully recovered by 1165, but revenues only matched Henry I at end of Henry II's reign (1154-89)
- **Likely**: general population declined during Stephen's reign
- Rapid expansion resumed end of 12th century, continued through most of 13th
- Inflation appearance 1180-1220 (**Harvey** 1973): indirect evidence of changed population regime

**Peak population**:
- **Hatcher and Hallam**: ~6 million estimate
- **Campbell** (2005): argues for 4.25 million
- Authors accept higher estimate
- Main argument relies on relative population changes (noncontroversial)

**Final signpost**: **Tax returns and muster certificates of 1520s**
- 2.25-2.75 million England population (
<!-- pp. 61-80 -->

## Chapter 2: The Plantagenet Cycle (continued)

### Coin Hoards and Instability Evidence

- **Coin hoards** track sociopolitical instability patterns across medieval England
- After mid-12th century peak, hoards dropped to early 13th century minimum
- 13th-14th centuries saw gradual, uneven rise, peaking around **1370**
- Second major peak in second half of 15th century tracks **Wars of the Roses** instability
- General correspondence between instability index (counting years of civil war/rebellion per 20 years) and hoard distribution
- Only significant mismatch: 1370 hoard peak vs. 1400 instability peak
- Data caveat: relies on **Thompson (1956)** compilation, now fifty years outdated; many more hoards discovered but not compiled

### 2.2 The Expansion Phase (1150–1260)

**Population Growth Indicators**

- No direct estimates, but lasting growth must have begun after **Stephen's anarchy** ended, stability under **Henry II**
- Persistent **wheat price inflation** from 1160 onward signals population pressure
- Other overpopulation signs: fragmentation of peasant holdings
- **J. Z. Titow (1961)** study of Somerset manor of Taunton: land-to-peasant ratio fell from **3.3 acres (1.33 ha)** in 1248 to **2.5 acres (1 ha)** in 1311

**Political Stability**

- Lowest instability level of entire medieval period
- Not conflict-free: three major crises
  - **1173–74 rebellion** against Henry II
  - Civil wars at end of John's reign
  - **1258 troubles** leading to "baronial reform" of 1263–67 (Mortimer 1994:77)
- **1173–74**: started in Normandy, spread to England; disgruntled earls revolted (centered in Midlands); kings of France and Scotland invaded, defeated by royal loyalists
- **1215–17**: politico-constitutional struggle between feudal barons and **John Lackland**, leading to **Magna Carta**
- Both 1173–74 and 1215–17 wars were "fairly mild conflicts (by medieval standards), mostly conducted by maneuvering and sieges"
- Civil war of **1263–66** and conflicts after it discussed in section 2.4

**Chronology of Internal Conflicts (Table 2.5 excerpt)**

- 1138–53: Anarchy (Stephen vs. Matilda/Henry)
- 1173–74: Widespread rebellion against Henry II
- 1215–17: Civil war between John and barons (Magna Carta), then royalists vs. rebels
- 1263–67: "Reformer" vs. "conservative" barons; **Simon de Montfort** defeated and captured Henry; de Montfort defeated and killed at **Evesham**
- 1315: Civil disorders during Lancaster supremacy (1314–22)
- 1321–22: Civil war; baron uprising in western counties; Edward II defeated Lancaster at **Boroughbridge**, beheaded him
- 1326–27: **Isabella and Mortimer** rebellion; abdication of Edward II, murdered eight months later
- 1330: Coup of Edward III against Mortimer (hanged 1330)
- 1381: **Peasants' Revolt**
- 1387–88: Insurrection of the "Lords Appellant"
- 1391: Coup d'état of Richard II
- 1397–99: Deposition of Richard II (1399) after parliamentary demand for accounting; Richard forced to abdicate, imprisoned in Tower, later died (murdered?) in 1400
- 1400–1408: **Glyn Dwr rebellion**
- 1414: Lollard plot against king's life
- 1448–51: Domestic disorders; Henry VI unfit to rule; council in power; factions encouraged private armed retainers who terrorized neighbors, paralyzed courts, dominated government
- 1450: **Jack Cade's rebellion**
- 1455–56: **Wars of the Roses, 1st phase**; Battle of St. Albans (1455): Somerset defeated and killed
- 1460–65: Wars of the Roses, 2nd phase; multiple battles, Henry VI imprisoned, Edward IV acclaimed king (1461)
- 1467–71: Wars of the Roses, 3rd phase; Edward's victory at Barnet (1471) where Warwick killed; Henry VI died (probably murdered) in Tower
- 1483–85: Wars of the Roses, 4th phase; Richard III defeated rebels, Henry defeated Richard III at Bosworth Field
- 1489: Rebellion in Northumberland
- 1495: **Rebellion of Perkin Warbeck**
- 1497: Insurrection in Cornwall over parliament-imposed tax; suppressed at Blackheath (June 22, 1497), leaders executed

### 2.3 Stagflation (1260–1315)

**Rural Population**

- Growth continued to 1300 but at slowing rate
- England and Wales approached **six million mark**
- In some regions growth ceased altogether
- Classic **Malthusian-Ricardian** overpopulation signs developed:
  - Prices reached secular peak in **1310s**
  - Real wages declined
  - Land rents increased
  - **Entry fines** (paid on taking up tenancy) increased even more steeply than rents

**Rent and Entry Fine Data**

- Rents ranged widely by location, period, and land type
- Evidence shows rents increased even more steeply than inflation
- Entry fines prior to mid-13th century scarce, but suggest steeper increase than rents
- Rents in 1000: 0.3–0.6 d./acre (England); by early 14th C: 8–28 in Huntingdonshire, 33 in Yorkshire, 30–36 in Northumberland
- **Bishopric of Worcester** rents: 12.5 shillings in 1299
- Norfolk rents 1370–90: 10.75 shillings; Warwickshire 1437: 10.5
- Hundred Rolls of 1279–80: rent per acre depended on holding size, tenant freedom status, landlord type (church vs. lay), land fertility, access to markets, nearness to employment
- Entry fines also varied; some lords used them to increase returns where they couldn't raise rents
- Overall trend unmistakable: **landlords' ability to extract surplus from peasants increased during stagflation**

**Peasant Holding Fragmentation**

- Holdings became increasingly fragmented
- **Grigg (1980:68)** examples:
  - Bishop of Worcester's estate: peasants holding a **yardland (30 acres/12 ha)** declined from 33% in 1250 to 25% in 1300
  - Kempsey: number of smallholders tripled between 1182 and 1299
  - Taunton manor: average holding 1.3 ha in 1248, declining to 1 ha in 1311
- By end 13th century: on sample of estates owned by ecclesiastical lords, **33% of population had less than 1 ha**
- Minimum farm size for family subsistence estimated at **4.5–6 ha**
- By 1300 majority of English peasants had less than this and couldn't survive without alternative income sources
- Distribution of landholdings (late 13th century): holdings of **half virgate** were both median and mean

**Peasant Budget Analysis (Table 2.9)**

- Detailed calculation for peasant holding **half virgate (15 acres/6 ha)**
- Assumptions: 3-field system, 10 acres crop acreage, 0.25 q sowing rate
- Yield ratio: **1:4**
- Production: 10 q harvested, minus 2.5 q seed = 7.5 q net
- Production in money (1 q = 6 s.): **45 s.**

**Extractions from peasant production:**
- Tithe (10% harvest): 1 q = 6 s.
- Rent (1 s./acre × 15 acres): 15 s.
- Other feudal exactions: 1 s.
- Taxes: 1 s.
- Death duties (heriot, mortuary, entry fee): 60 s. total, amortized over 20 years = 3 s./year
- **Total extraction: 26 s. = 4.3 q**

**Peasant consumption:**
- Minimum consumption: **4 q**
- Remaining to peasant: 7.5 q – 4.3 q = **3.2 q**
- Deficit: 3.2 q – 4 q = **–0.8 q** (in money: 5 s.)

**Proportions of total production:**
- Consumption: 48%
- Church tithes: 12%
- Landowner: 38%
- State: 2%

- Peasants did not grow just wheat; more realistic mix (wheat, barley, peas, oats) yields similar result
- **Dyer (1989:113)**: similar calculation shows ~10% difference from simpler wheat-only model
- Cash income from animals not included in Table 2.9; peasant with virgate might derive additional 33 s. from this source
- Half-virgaters kept fewer animals, would yield little cash surplus after tithes and consumption
- Garden, poultry, wife's spinning, sons hiring out as agricultural laborers critical to meeting obligations
- "How he paid for clothing, cooking pots, or furnishings is not at all clear" (Dyer)
- During mild crop failure, half-virgater would go into debt to survive

**Elite-to-Commoner Resource Extraction**

- Church: 12% (underestimate; excludes tithe on animals and garden produce)
- Lord: 38% (underestimate; excludes labor services)
- Feudal exactions: ~1 s./year
- State: 2% (tiny proportion)
- Peasant left with less than half product (barely enough for subsistence)

- While typical half-virgaters balanced on edge of survival, few well-to-do peasants with **virgate** did much better
- **Dyer (1989:117)**: average yardlander in normal year "in a good position to make a cash surplus"
- By contrast, smallholder had to find employment for 130 days/year to make ends meet
- Large numbers of such smallholders, so only small minority could secure full employment
- Around 1300 at Halesowen (Worcestershire): wealthier peasants had average **5.1 children**, vs. cottagers' **1.8 offspring** (Dyer 2002:158)

**State Taxation**

- Remarkably, only **2%** of peasant-generated product went to state
- Estimate follows **Dyer (2002:258)**: peasant born 1270, acquiring holding of 20 acres in 1293, would pay direct taxes in 1294–97, then additional nine years 1301–1322
- His son (succeeding mid-1320s) would pay in 1327, 1332, 1334, 1336, and contribute to three subsidies 1337–40
- Two generations paid **60 s. over forty-six years, or 1.3 s./year**
- Half-virgater holding fifteen acres: probably **1 s./year**
- Crown's taxes only 2% of gross domestic product (Dyer 2002:257)
- In sum, **England of 1300 was very undertaxed country**; surplus extraction heavily lopsided in favor of elites
- By 1300, elite extraction had started cutting into bare subsistence minimum, tendency worsened during most of 14th century

**Root Cause of Late 13th Century Immiseration**

- Not just surplus extraction by feudal lords, but **massive population growth** during preceding century
- Population growth decreased average peasant-to-land ratios AND increased inequality of landholdings
- One-third of rural freeholders held **acre or less**; another third held **1–10 acres** (Kanzaka 2002:599) — not enough to break even
- By contrast, 10% of freeholders very well off with **forty or more acres**

### Urbanization

**London Growth**

- **Keene (2001:196)**: 1100 population ~20,000 (0.8% of total country population)
- By 1300: **80,000** (1.3% of total)
- Poll taxes 1377: 1.7% of assessed England population in London, suggesting slightly under 50,000 Londoners
- Summary: between 1100 and 1400, **urbanization index doubled**

**Large Towns**

- Proportion of population living in large towns (over 10,000 people) increased
- 11th century: only one such town (London)
- End 13th century: **14–16 towns** with 10,000+ population, containing at least 5% of England's population (Britnell 1995:10)
- Urbanization continued during 14th century

**New Town Foundations**

- New towns founded during 12th-13th centuries
- Planned towns in England and Wales peaked during second half of 13th century (Beresford 1967:366)

**Urban Demographics**

- 14th century urban population increase clearly NOT result of better demographic rates vs. rural locations
- Medieval cities were "death traps"; most English towns decimated by plague outbreaks (Dobson 2001:276)
- Towns made up losses through immigration from rural areas
- Direct evidence: spectacular increase in recruitment of new citizens in **York's freemen's register** (Dobson 2001:276)
- Some cities (e.g., **Coventry**) expanded during second half of 14th century despite Black Death ravages (Phythian-Adams 1979:33)

### The Elites

**Elite Prosperity During Stagflation**

- While general population growth slowed and peaked around 1300, **elite numbers continued expanding** throughout stagflation and even beyond (to 1350)
- Elites did well economically during 13th century

**Magnate Families**

- At top, number of magnate families expanded only slightly (perhaps 160 to 200 families)
- Average income grew from **£200 to £670/year** — more than twofold increase in real terms

**Middle Ranks Expansion**

- **Belted knights** (substantial landowners holding land worth at least £10/year): 1,000 in 1200
- By 1300: **3,000 knights and esquires** with incomes over £20/year
- Rough equivalent in real terms of £10 in 1200

**Lesser Landholders**

- Numbers also grew, though precise estimate difficult
- **Given-Wilson**: 9,000–10,000 lords with income over £5 in 14th century
- **Denholm-Young**: proposed number twice that
- **Dyer**: 20,000 households enjoying incomes £10–£100/year in 1300 (includes beneficed clergy, excludes lesser landowners £5–£10)
- Truth probably lies between extremes
- Likely that landholders kept pace with general populace (with lag), tripling from ~5,000 in 12th century to 15,000 at peak in 14th century

**Inquisitions Post Mortem Evidence**

- Elite replacement rate well above one during whole period 1250–1340
- If landed elites were closed class changing only by births/deaths, numbers would have expanded by factor of **2.67** during century after 1250
- Reality: elite numbers affected by upward and downward social mobility
- No evidence for massive downward mobility after 1250 comparable to earlier period (around 1200, substantial numbers of lesser nobility slipped down social scale; Given-Wilson 1987:16)
- Regional studies suggest those losing ground in minority
- **Oxfordshire sample**: five families lost land, nine gained it, seventeen remained in same position (Dyer 2002:152)

**The Clergy**

- Nonproductive class greatly bloated by huge numbers of clergy
- 13th century England "swarming with clerics" (Jessopp 1892)
- Estimated **25,000 monks and nuns** (Moorman 1946:258)
- Parish clergy estimates: 40,000 (Moorman 1946:53) to 2% of population (Coulton 1907) — implying over 100,000
- **9,000–10,000 parishes** in 13th century England (Moorman 1946:5)
- Around **five ordained men per parish** (Moorman 1946:55)
- **Hilton (1966:62)**: diocese of Worcester had 2,000 ordained clerics with 445 parishes
- These numbers imply **50,000 secular clergy** in England in 1300, or **75,000 counting monks and nuns**
