- Does not include "clerical or semi-clerical underworld" (Hilton 1966:62)

**Foundations of Elite Prosperity**

- Provided by plentiful labor supply → increasing rents, declining wages
- Elite incomes from land kept pace with or grew faster than inflation
- **Dyer (2002)**: "main benefit for lords came from additions to numbers of customary tenants who owed heavy burdens of labor service and cash payments"

**Growing Inequality**

- Gap between economic well-being of commoners and elites increased
- While peasant incomes plummeted (lack of land, increased rents, decreased wages), elite incomes increased in absolute and relative terms
- Trend visible in military wages: elite soldiers (knights) pay grew faster than inflation; real wages of commoners (foot soldiers) declined

**Magnate Income Examples**

- Overall pattern to 1300: increasing inequality
- Standard of living of commoners declined; gentry incomes generally outpaced inflation; magnates did best
- Henry II's reign: few lords had income exceeding **£500/year** (Bartlett 2000:80)
- Highest income in **Sidney Painter's** list of 54 barons around 1200: **£800** enjoyed by **Roger de Lacy**, constable of Chester (died 1210)
- Ratio of maximum to average income among barons: only **4:1**
- One hundred years later: largest income in Painter's list of 27 landholders: **Edmund, Earl of Cornwall** — **£3,800** annual income at death in 1301 (Painter 1943:174)
- Accounting for inflation: represents increase of **2.8 in real terms**
- Even greater: **Thomas, Earl of Lancaster**: **£11,000 in 1311** (Dyer 1989:29)
- Maximum to average income ratio: **16:1**, compared to 4:1 one hundred years earlier
- Six earls (including Earl of Cornwall) enjoyed income of more than **£3,000/annum** (Dyer 1989:29)
- Largest known fortune in cash in late medieval England: **Richard, Earl of Arundel**: **£72,250** at death in 1376 (Bean 1991:565)

**Elite Luxury Consumption**

- Wine consumption reached medieval peak in early 14th century
- English imported **20,000 tuns** from Gascony (Dyer 1989)
- Worth wholesale **£60,000** (Miller and Hatcher 1978:81)
- Assuming 20,000 elite households: represents **2–3 liters of wine per household per day**

### 2.4 Crisis (1315–1400)

**Population Decline**

- Economic misery of commoner population grew steadily during stagflation, peaking in early 14th century
- Peasants squeezed from below (insufficient land from too many people competing for limited land) and from above (expanding, rapacious class of noble landowners maintaining consumption levels)
- Important regional variations in how social structure responded to population pressure
- **East Anglia**: freeholder stratum (sokemen) progressively subdivided land among heirs → proliferation of smallholders with tiny plots
- South: manorial lords exerted better control over land distribution → substantial minority of peasants held 1/4 to whole virgate (3–12 ha), leaving surplus population with cottage and garden plots
- Both cases: most population didn't have enough land to feed itself, relied on additional income sources
- Increasing misery affected grain storage: in Colchester 1295–1301, median store of wheat per taxpayer slipped below one quarter (Hallam 1988a:822)
- Peasants increasingly leading precarious hand-to-mouth existence, most having no protection against fluctuations in grain brought in as harvest
- String of very poor harvests in England beginning **1315** resulted in mortality rates "nothing short of catastrophic"

**The Agrarian Crisis of 1315–21**

- Classic study: **Postan and Titow (Postan 1973: Chapter 9)** on heriots paid on five Winchester manors
- Heriot = payment when tenant died and another replaced him
- Average number of heriots more than doubled: from 47/year during second half 13th century to **106 during decade 1310–19**
- Most revealing: dynamics of **money heriots** (paid by poorer villages with few/no beasts, few/no acres of land)
- Money heriots fluctuated around 10/year until 1290, then grew rapidly to peak of almost **60 in 1310–19**, another peak in decade prior to Black Death
- Clear that first to suffer from dearth were **poorer segments of rural society**
- Further evidence: high wheat prices correlated with numbers of money heriots but not animal heriots (analysis by J. Longden in Postan 1973:179–185)

**The 1315–22 Disaster**

- Period of severe harvest failures and livestock epidemics = "dividing line in the history of the medieval English countryside" (Miller and Hatcher 1978:60)
- Poor harvest of 1314 succeeded by two disastrous harvests **1315 and 1316**
- Harvests improved after 1317, but series of deadly epidemics affected cattle herds **1319–1321**
- Agrarian crisis of **1315–21** resulted in noticeable population decline
- Direct evidence fragmentary, but tithing penny data from Essex parishes: between 1300 and 1340, number of tithingmen declined by **30 percent** (Poos 1985)
- Indirect evidence: significant increase in unwanted holdings, signaling slackening competition for land (Miller and Hatcher 1978:59)
- Wheat prices during 1330s and 1340s declined to levels not seen since 1270
- Secular trend definitely down, with significant fluctuations
- By 1340s amount of uncultivated land reached noticeable dimension in some counties (Sussex, Cambridgeshire) (Miller and Hatcher 1978:61)

<!-- pp. 81-100 -->

## Continuation: Crisis Phase (1340-1485)

### Urbanization Paradox

- London population grew from 80,000 (1.3% of English total) in 1377 to higher proportion, despite overall national decline to ~50,000 rural population
- Several towns expanded: **Colchester** (4,000 → 6,000), **Coventry** (5,000 → 9,000)
- Larger towns like **Bristol**, **Norwich**, **Southampton**, **York** prospered in late 14th century
- **Push-pull dynamic**: rural population pushed by increased seigneurial oppression, pulled by urban employment opportunities serving elite consumption

### Elites in Crisis

- Elites sought non-land income as peasant extraction reached limits
- **Military employment** became most common avenue for aristocratic advancement
- **Literacy spread** among aristocracy — the **miles literatus** (literate knight) ceased to be rare by 14th century
- By 15th century, intraelite competition slackened (e.g., Oxford student population declined)

#### Hundred Years' War as Elite Enterprise

- Extraordinary aristocratic military participation 1338-61 during English successes in France
- 900+ knights served at **Crécy** and **Calais** (870 total, 680 English) in 1359-60 expedition
- **Denholm-Young** estimated ~1,250 knights in England at start of 13th century
- Knights paid 2-4 shillings/day; 2-3 months campaigning = substantial sum (£6-18), given £20/year needed to support a knight
- War spoils highly lucrative: plunder, ransoms from French noblemen, indemnities (**rachâts**) from occupied fortresses
- **Edward III** received £250,000+ in ransoms for **King John of France** and **King David of Scotland**, plus similar amount from rachâts
- Later conquests brought land income: **Henry V** conferred lands worth £30,000 on followers after Normandy/Maine/Anjou conquests

#### War Profitability Debate

- Medievalists debate if Hundred Years' War was net profitable for England
- Clear that **elites profited enormously** — rewards went primarily to them, while commoners bore costs (bulk taxation, purveyances)
- Per **McFarlane**: of £8 million in war taxes over 120 years, half came from wool taxation
- After **Edward III's** victories at **Crécy** and **Neville's Cross** (1346), parliament thanked God, saying "all the money they had given him had been well spent"

#### Military Reverses and Elite Crisis

- Anglo-French warfare resumed 1369; by 1370s French reconquered most of Aquitaine
- After **Black Death**, poll tax of 1381 precipitated **Great Revolt of 1381**
- Peasant revolts suppressed but terrified landowners — parliaments refused further direct taxes temporarily
- Great Revolt marked turning point, undermining ability to profit from internal French turmoil during **Charles VI's** minority
- Accelerated transition from **direct domanial exploitation** to **leasing of demesnes** (started late 1360s)

### Declining Land Revenues

- 15th century land incomes fell 20% (southern/midland England), one-third (northeast)
- Elites squeezed by diminishing land returns and lost overseas conquest opportunities
- State finances under pressure — greater proportion of Crown income diverted to elites
- **Edward III's** annuity bill (1360s): £13,000; **Richard II's** (1399): £25,000 (threefold real increase)
- Small proportion of aristocracy benefited from funds; similar pressure from lesser gentry
- Magnates' retinue sizes increased: average 50 in first half 14th century → 150+ in second half 15th century

### Rise of "Bastard Feudalism"

- **Bastard feudalism**: large baronial retinues fueled intense intraelite competition, increasing factionalization, privatization of coercive power
- Major factor contributing to later civil wars, particularly **Wars of the Roses**

### The Rise and Fall of State Finances

#### 13th Century: Mild Taxation

- 13th century taxes mild, stayed approximately constant or declined in real terms before 1290
- Proportion of GDP to state **plunged** (both GDP and population expanded greatly)
- Even after taxes doubled (~1300), still <2% of GDP
- England undertaxed — needed worthy cause to unify Crown, aristocracy, commoners
- **War with France** provided this common cause

#### 14th Century: Revenue Surge

- Crown revenues **doubled** during early Hundred Years' War (see figure 2.6)
- 1370s-1380s revenues stayed roughly same level, but represented another doubling of tax burden (population halved by plague)
- New poll taxes (1377) aimed to shift taxation burden toward peasantry
- 1369-80 government spent £1.1+ million on extraordinary expenditures

#### Tax System Expansion

- 1376 "Good Parliament" rejected direct taxation requests
- 1377 parliament granted direct subsidy via **poll tax** — brought lower classes into tax system previously exempt
- Unprecedented taxes imposed 1377, 1379, 1381:
  - 1377: everybody over 14 paid 4 d.
  - 1381: everybody over 15 paid 12 d.

#### Revenue Collapse

- Per **W.M. Ormrod** and **Patrick O'Brien**: maximum tax yield (real terms) achieved 1336-45
- General trend next century: **down**
- Shorter-term fluctuations (peaks 1300, 1340, 1380, 1420) correlated with intensified Scotland/France warfare
- Between 1340 and lowest point (1460), revenue from taxes declined **almost threefold**

### Rising Sociopolitical Instability

- Popular immiseration, intraelite conflict, state financial difficulties drove social unraveling
- Rise of sociopolitical instability (early 13th to late 14th century) not simple/unilineal — overlaid by **generational waves** (figure 2.7)
- Most significant 13th century unrest: 1258 crisis → **barons' wars** of 1263-67

#### 1320s Wave of Internal Conflict

- Next wave occurred 1320s during last half of **Edward II's** reign
- Unsuccessful rebellion: **Welsh Marches barons**, **Battle of Boroughbridge** (1322) — royal forces won, rebel leaders executed
- Successful rebellion: **Mortimer** and **Isabella** (1326)
- **Edward II** deposition and murder (1327)
- Rebellion led by **Henry of Lancaster** suppressed by Mortimer (1329)
- Execution of **Edmund, Earl of Kent** for plotting against regime (1330)
- Coup by **Edward III** against Mortimer/Isabella regime, Mortimer's execution (1330)
- **Regicide of Edward II** significantly elevated intraelite conflict intensity

#### Edward III and Richard II Reigns

- **Edward III** relatively free of internal strife (except at end) — elite energy directed toward successful France war
- By end of reign, French reconquered most lands lost to English → chain of events culminating in **Peasants' Revolt of 1381**
- **Richard II** (1377-98): period of enhanced sociopolitical instability with elite faction strife + peasant uprisings
- 1381-1405: at least five more popular revolts (beyond 1381) broke out or barely averted
- Most regional except **Cheshire rising of 1403** evolving into major civil war
- Serious elite infighting: **Lords Appellant** uprising (1387-88), **coup d'état** where Richard II regained control (1391)
- Civil war peak 1397-99: Richard II convicted three lords appellant of treason/executed; following year **Henry of Bolingbroke** deposed Richard II, crowned himself **Henry IV**
- **Richard** died or was murdered in prison (1400)
- 1400: great uprising in **Wales** led by **Glyn Dwr**, lasted eight years

### The Late Medieval Crime Wave

- 14th-15th centuries: heightened criminal activity — **"late medieval crime wave"**
- **Barbara Hanawalt** (1976, 1979): best explanations for crime pattern changes = **economic changes** and **war**
- Economic crimes increased during scarcity periods
- Burglaries increased enormously 1315-19 during **Great Famine** (figure 2.9)
- Annual economic crime statistics closely followed wheat price fluctuations

#### Homicide Patterns

- Homicides primarily affected by **political strife** and **war**
- County-level analysis supports this (Hanawalt): **Herefordshire** highest crime peak not during 1315-17 famine but during civil war leading to Edward II deposition
- Scottish wars (1314-19, 1322-23, 1332-37) coincided with Yorkshire crime rises
- War contributed to nobles' household problems, gang activity, increased murder
- Commons complaints about "increased horrors of gangs and pardoned felons who were king's veterans" were correct
- Homicide rates greatly increased 1300-1348 (figure 2.9)
- Murder rate probably persisted during second half 14th century (rural **Northamptonshire** data, table 2.14)
- Murder rate 14-18 during 1360-79 vs. average 11 during 1300-29
- Homicide incidence per capita **nearly tripled** during 14th century (accounting for post-Black Death population decrease)

> "War also contributed to the problems of the nobles' households and gang activity in general and correlated with increased murder. The Commons were undoubtedly correct in their complaints about the increased horrors of gangs and pardoned felons who were king's veterans" (Hanawalt)

#### Intraelite Conflict Forms

- Ranged from full-scale civil war to persistent noble faction infighting to small-scale feuding and individual violence
- 14th century **Gloucestershire**: more than half resident knights/esquires committed at least one felony or trespass

## 2.5 Depression (1400-1485)

- Year 1400 somewhat arbitrary breakpoint — no abrupt transition
- Civil war outbreaks continued arriving in recurrent fashion

### General Population and Peasant Economy

- 15th century population stayed relatively constant: 2-2.5 million range
- Some authorities depict trend as essentially flat (Dyer); others suggest continued decline toward 1450 at slower rate than second half 14th century
- Replacement rates for commoners and landowners both below one before 1450 — second view probably closer to truth

#### Improved Living Standards

- Low population densities → greatly improved living standards
- Real wages continued increasing first half 15th century (though slower; see figure 2.3)
- Peasants ate less bread, more meat/fish/dairy
- Bread: 50% of harvest worker diet (Norfolk, 1300) → 15% (15th century)
- Meat: 8% → 30-40%
- Land-peasant ratios increased greatly: by 1450, one-eighth of rural householders in England held 50+ acres, compared with tiny proportion before epidemics

#### The Medieval Depression Puzzle

- Great puzzle: why didn't excellent real wages, consumption patterns, land-peasant ratios, low rents translate into renewed population growth?
- All favorable conditions in place by 1400, yet population growth resumed only a century later
- One possible cause: **late marriage** — but doesn't explain why population growth resumed in 16th century
- What changed around 1500 that caused population growth to resume? Addressed later when discussing sociopolitical instability role

### Elite Dynamics

- While commoners enjoyed better incomes/consumption, landholding elite incomes **continued to decline**
- **Bean** (1991): 1420-70 marked fall in landed revenue for many estates (1470-1500 were recovery years)
- Significant regional variation: 20% reductions normal (southern/midland England); Cornwall lost less ground; northeast England revenues fell one-third

#### Declining Aristocratic Consumption

- Aristocratic revenue decline matched by decreasing consumption
- Around 1300: English consumed 20,000 tuns of French wine
- 1460s: <5,000 tuns imported; wine imports didn't recover until after 1490

> "To sum up, the aristocracy as a whole expanded their consumption in the thirteenth century; they drank more wine, rebuilt their monasteries, cathedrals and castles, and surrounded their houses by moats. In the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries they experienced greater or lesser degrees of financial embarrassment.... Even those skilful and fortunate families who did add to their estates still had to cope with the fact that newly acquired assets were deteriorating in value. Cash incomes declined, and real incomes declined still further because of the operation of price scissors. The problem was first felt in the twenty or thirty years before the Black Death. After that catastrophe the aristocracy felt vulnerable but did not suffer drastic drops in income. The most serious decline came after 1400, and the worst was over by the 1470s or 1480s." (Dyer 1989:108)

#### Elite Numerical Decline

- Falling income → many aristocratic families couldn't maintain status
- Balance between upward/downward mobility shifted decisively toward latter
- Affected all ranks (tables 2.9a-c):
  - Magnates: ~200 barons (1300) → 60 peers (1500)
  - Middle-rank aristocrats (knights/esquires): 3,000 (1300) → 1,300 (1500)
  - Both magnates and middle-rank elites apparently declined **two-thirds**
  - Lower-rank elite numerical decline probably same order of magnitude

#### Mechanisms of Elite Reduction

**Political extinguishment**: some lineages extinguished when backing wrong side in civil war/coup d'état
- Of 16 new earls/marquises/dukes created during **Edward II** and **Richard II** troubled reigns, at least 14 executed/exiled/demoted within 5 years of creation
- Of 13 created by **Edward III**, none suffered that fate
- Intensity fluctuated in cyclical fashion

**Economic decline**: many noble lineages couldn't maintain status due to diminishing revenues
- **George Neville, Duke of Bedford**: quietly dropped from peer list (1478)
- **Marquis of Berkeley** disappeared (1492)
- Some lost status temporarily: **lords Clinton** dropped out 1460-1514

**Negative replacement rates**: between 1350-1450 (see figure 2.4) → many male lineages failed
- Fortunes often merged with other same-rank lineages, keeping latter afloat despite declining land returns

#### Social Tension from Elite Contraction

- Forces contracting elite numbers generated enormous social tension
- Many privileged class members not content to sink quietly into yeomanry
- Important factor contributing to 15th century civil wars

#### Growth of Armed Retinues

- Political instability growth driven by armed retinues associated with magnates
- **Henry Percy, Earl of Northumberland**: spent one-third to one-half of £3,000 total income on fees/annuities to supporters
- Retainers paid £2-10/annum — very welcome supplement to £10-20/annum income

### State Fiscal Collapse and Onset of Civil War

#### Henry V and Early Successes

- **Henry V** reign: period of internal stability (apart from minor **Lollard** persecution) + successful France warfare, similar to middle **Edward III** years
- **Henry VI's** minority years also relatively peaceful
- 1429: English failed to capture **Orléans** (city relieved by **Jeanne d'Arc**); French position began unraveling
- 1442: French conquered **Gascony** (except Bordeaux/Bayonne)
- 1448-51: English almost completely expelled from France
- French reconquest of Maine (1448), Normandy (1450), Bordeaux/Bayonne (1451) left only **Calais** in English hands at Hundred Years' War end
- String of defeats triggered events remarkably resembling what followed English reverses at end of **Edward III's** reign

#### Crown Debt Crisis

- By 1433 government in increasingly dire fiscal straits: accumulated debt £160,000, income from taxation voted for next 2 years assigned
- Annual deficit on regular/domestic income/expenditure alone: £21,000+
- Defense of French possessions against resurgent French required huge outlays
- By 1449 situation infinitely worse: economic depression of mid-15th century meant Crown landed revenue declined; recession/trade embargo with Flanders halved customs/poundage income
- **Henry VI** reign saw steady alienation of royal properties, many for life or lives terms
- Royal annuities cost Exchequer ~£30,000, accounting for close to one-third all royal revenues
- Parliament grudgingly voted half subsidy (1445), none (1447)
- 1448 Crown jewels sold
- 1449 total Crown debt rose to **£372,000** (table 2.15)

#### Second Debt Crisis

- Second time state debt reached unsustainable level (first: **Edward III**)
- But Edward III saved by military successes in France + enormous ransom of **John II** captured at Poitiers
- 1450 military situation in France reversed

#### Government Collapse

- **Henry VI** intermittently insane and unfit to rule
- Government lost last vestiges of legitimacy with disastrous loss of all French possessions (except Calais)
- Now broke, couldn't maintain internal order

#### Rising Lawlessness

- General lawlessness/disorder increased during 1440s, especially 1450s (figure 2.10)
- Royal justice perverted for partisan ends, "particularly in East Anglia, Kent, and Sussex, where extortion, fraud, theft, violence, and intimidation…had not been witnessed on such a scale since the reign of Richard II"
- Private feuds/riots/armed clashes: 1448-55 at least one-sixth of peerage imprisoned for disreputable conduct at some time
- Nobility feuding gradually became private wars, merged into general civil warfare = **Wars of the Roses**

### Wars of the Roses (1455-1485)

#### Initial Phase: Elite Quarrels

- Listed counties affected by major elite quarrels around 1450:
  1. West (Cornwall/Devon → Somerset/Wiltshire): Earl of Devon vs. Lord Bonville feud (5,000-6,000 retainers/allies)
  2. North (Cumberland/Westmorland/Yorkshire): persistent guerilla warfare between Nevilles and Percies
  3. Bedfordshire: Lords Grey and Fanhope feud
  4. Norfolk/Suffolk: Duke of Suffolk vs. Sir John Falstoff and Pastons
  5. Oxfordshire/Warwickshire: Stafford vs. Harcourt families
  6. Gloucestershire: Berkeleys vs. Countess of Shrewsbury
  7. Southern Lincolnshire: Sir William Tailbois exploits
  8. Derbyshire: Longfords vs. Blounts

#### Popular Rebellion: Jack Cade (1450)

- 1450: major popular rebellion led by **Jack Cade**
- Kent pressure rising for more than a decade: disturbances 1438, 1443, 1445, 1448
- **Cade rebellion** spread widely across southern England: Wiltshire mob lynched Bishop Ayscough; risings in Salisbury, Isle of Wight, Glocester, Essex
- Later 1450 (August-September): another wave of risings in Sussex, Wiltshire, Essex, Kent
- Cade's Revolt finally suppressed 1451

#### Civil War Escalation

- Collapse of royal finances (1449) followed by state breakdown
- 1449-50 parliament impeached **Earl of Suffolk** (murdered as he tried leaving country)
- Ensuing power struggle between **Richard of York** and **Edmund Beaufort, Duke of Somerset** escalated into open warfare (1455)
- Following year Somerset defeated/killed in **Battle of St. Albans**
- 1459-71: civil war fought between factions led by **Queen Margaret** and **Richard of York**
- York killed in battle (1461), but son **Edward IV** crowned same year
- **Henry VI** captured and imprisoned in Tower, died (most likely murdered) 1471

#### 1471-1483 Lull and Renewal

- 1471-83: peaceful lull
- **Edward IV** died, succeeded by son **Edward V** (only twelve years old)
- Conflict erupted between two elite factions: one led by king's maternal uncle **Lord Rivers**, other by paternal uncle **Richard of Gloucester**
- Richard won, Rivers and some anti-Richard leaders executed without trial
- **Edward V** deposed and, in all probability, later murdered in Tower with his brother
- 1485: **Richard III** fell at **battle of Bosworth Field**; **Henry Tudor** became king of England

#### End of Wars and Internal Stability

- **Bosworth** marked end of Wars of the Roses
- Intensity of internal warfare rapidly declined after 1485
- Rising in Northumberland (1489), pretender **Perkin Warbeck** attempt (1495-97), insurrection in Cornwall (1497)
- Last aftershock: minor uprising in Yorkshire = **Pilgrimage of Grace** (1536-37)
- After which England enjoyed a century of internal stability

### Limited Direct Impact on Population

- Intensity of intraelite conflict during Wars of the Roses extremely high
- Three kings deposed/killed; numerous magnates executed, often without trial
- Many ending on losing side beheaded in mud on spot
- **Direct effect on England population insignificant**: unlikely >50,000 (out of 2-2.5 million total) ever took part in civil war battles
- Military operations affected only small proportion of kingdom

### Indirect Impact: Sociopolitical Instability

- Direct combatant losses just tip of iceberg
- Not the throne struggle itself that damaged society but **general increase in sociopolitical instability** during 1445-85 (particularly 1380-1410 and 1445-1485)
- Instability manifested in increased interpersonal crime, banditry, feuding, factional infighting
- Political struggles of great lords = manifestation of underlying social trend rather than direct cause

#### "Landscape of Fear" and Productive Capacity Damage

- High sociopolitical instability 1380-1485 (especially 1380-1410 and 1445-1485) **damaged productive capacity** (carrying capacity)
- Specific mechanism: establishment of **"landscape of fear"**
- Most clearcut case: peripheries largely left to fend for themselves
  - North: persistent Scottish raids depopulated large Borderlands swaths — essentially abandoned by Crown to Nevilles/Percies (who fought each other incessantly, taking opposite sides in York-Lancaster conflict)
  - Southern seashore suffered badly from French pirate raids; many coastal areas abandoned, population moved inland
  - Wales: **Glyn Dwr** rebellion and following reconquest caused widespread destruction
  - East Anglia: land lost from inundation due to failure of 13th century flood control measures
  - All these abandoned lands couldn't be put to productive uses

#### Central Kingdom Insecurity

- Central kingdom not as dire as periphery, but cultivator life precarious
- Breakdown of law/order in English countryside mid-15th century = rule rather than exception
- Common people very vulnerable to intraelite fighting
- Many factions targeted rivals' tenants or others caught in conflict middle for intimidation, extortion, robbery, simple murder
- Example: **Bishop Lacy of Exeter** recorded during Earl of Devon vs. Lord Bonville private war (1451), some tenants at Clyst (east of Exeter) "dared not occupy the land"
- **Thomas Percy, Lord Egremont** followers, even after his death at **battle of Northampton** (1460), continued holding **castle of Wressle** in Yorkshire against all comers, using it as raiding/harrying base nearby

> "It was impossible to cultivate land when you or your dependents could be robbed or murdered at any moment, your work horses stolen, and your house burned down around your ears."

#### Security Zones and Overall Capacity Reduction

- Some areas probably more secure because local elites maintained peace; others less so
- Strong places (walled towns) also created security zones around them
- **Landscape of fear** came into being → proportion of arable land couldn't be cultivated, lowering overall carrying capacity, number of people English soil could support

### Population Mobility in 15th Century

- 15th century: time of high population mobility
- Facile explanation: 15th century Englishmen/women particularly footloose
- Generally people need weighty reasons to abandon places where they've invested time/labor
- 14th century reasons: economic oppression by landed elites
- 15th century: breakdown of law and order

#### Village Abandonment

- Great number of English villages abandoned during 15th century
- Some probably "murdered" by landlords wanting to turn them into sheep pasture
- Others (particularly smaller ones) abandoned because too insecure

#### Migration Patterns

- Some migrants: one rural area → another
- Others: → towns
- Majority of towns continued doing well during this period
- Since premodern towns = population sinks, only way they maintained numbers = constant immigrant influx
- When **Tudor regime** pacified countryside, removed important reason for rural dwellers to move to town security
- Result: during early Tudor period most towns lost population, some simply withered on vine

## 2.6 Conclusion

### Major Predictions Confirmed

- Great mass of data reviewed suggests **Malthusian-Ricardian theory** of **Postan** and **Le Roy Ladurie** works quite well explaining demographic/economic/social dynamics of England until mid-14th century
- Most striking observation: almost perfect inverse relationship between population pressure and real wage (conversely, very good correlation between population pressure and misery index; see figure 2.1)
- Strong dynamical patterns (documented in figure 2.1) support idea that historical processes can profitably be studied using theoretical/data analytical methods of nonlinear dynamics

### The Medieval Depression Anomaly

- **"Medieval depression"** — failure of population growth to resume once Black Death aftershocks died out — significant anomaly from crude Malthusian model viewpoint
- Chapter argues: needed to understand medieval depression = **elite- and state-centered perspective** of demographicstructural theory
- Key factor preventing population growth 1380-1485: **high sociopolitical instability** manifesting as recurrent breakdown of law/order
- Law/order couldn't be established permanently until elite numbers/appetites brought in line with society productive capacity
- **Population growth couldn't resume until elite overproduction problem somehow solved**

### Solving Elite Overproduction

- Took unusually long time for this to happen
- By 1485: economic hardship and internal warfare during 14th-15th centuries pruned English ruling class size to roughly **one-third** of early 14th century levels
- Numbers of magnates declined from ~200 barons → ~60 lay peers
- Middle ranks (knights/esquires) shrank 3,000 → 1,300
- Lesser gentry numbers declined perhaps 15,000 → 5,000

### Declining Economic Inequality

- Another aspect of same process: decline of extreme economic inequality developed by 1300
- Lower social hierarchy end: population decline greatly increased land-peasant ratios, improved consumption patterns even for poor
- Higher end: huge 14th century fortunes (earls of Lancaster £11,000 in 1311; dukes of Lancaster £12,500 in 1394) gone by 15th century
- Maximum income assessed 1436: £3,230 (Richard of York), well below even earls of Cornwall early 14th century (£6,000 in 1300)
- Other £3,000+ incomes: Earl of Warwick and Duke of Buckingham
- Around 1300: six earls enjoyed £3,000+/year income
- Later 15th century: even these fortunes tended to disappear
- York inheritance merged into Crown after Richard of York's son crowned **Edward IV** (1461)
- Warwick fortune absorbed by Crown after 1471 (Richard Neville, Earl of Warwick, killed in battle fighting against Edward IV)

### Theory Qualifications

#### Exogenous Factors

**Climate**: cold/wet years after 1315 apparently triggered beginning of population decline; global cooling following medieval optimum probably depressed crop yields, decreased carrying capacity of medieval-early modern agrarian system

**Black Death**: even more obviously exogenous shock — Eurasia-wide plague pandemic arrival in England (1348). Population would likely have continued declining even absent Black Death, but probably much more slowly and not as deeply

**Geopolitical situation**: England's relationship with neighbors (Scotland/France) another exogenous factor of great importance. Deadly wars with France served to lengthen Plantagenet cycle disintegrative phase

#### Endogenous Complexities: Fathers-and-Sons Cycles

- Standard demographicstructural models predict continuous sociopolitical instability, gradual numerical decline of ruling class
- Yet actually, instability waxes/wanes in waves, interspersed with relatively peaceful periods
- General occurrence during secular cycles disintegrative phases = **"fathers-and-sons" cycle**

**Plantagenet England pattern**: three fathers-and-sons cycles interacted repeatably with changes in England's geopolitical environment

Essential dynamic of each cycle:
1. **Centripetal phase**: unified elites, increased taxation, success in external wars
2. **Centrifugal phase**: state fiscal problems, intraelite competition shading into civil war, loss of external conquests

**First cycle**: reigns of **Edward I** (1272-1307) and **Edward II** (1307-27)
- Edward I reversed century-long decline in Crown revenues (see figure 2.6), conquered Scotland, profiting from its civil war collapse
- Edward II presided over increasingly fractious nobility, declining revenues, disastrous defeat at **Bannockburn** (1314), lost Scotland, lost crown/life in 1320s civil wars

**Second cycle**: reigns of **Edward III** (1327-77) and **Richard II** (1377-99)
- Edward III unified elites, achieved highest medieval English taxation rate, conquered half of France
- Richard II alienated major nobility segment (executed some), saw declining revenues, parliament tax refusal, widespread popular uprisings
- Like Edward II: overthrown, later died (possibly murdered) in prison

**Third cycle**: combined Lancastrian and Yorkist period
- Reign of **Henry V** and infancy of **Henry VI**: England experienced national unification, relative fiscal stability, successful France conquest
- Beginning 1430s: gradually slid into state bankruptcy, intraelite conflict, territorial France loss, finally all-out civil war
- Last battle of Wars of the Roses (1485) not only end of third mini-cycle of fathers/sons, also **end of grand secular cycle of Plantagenet England**

*OceanofPDF.com*

## Chapter 3: Early Modern England: The Tudor-Stuart Cycle (1485-1730)

### 3.1 Overview of the Cycle

- Cycle start: year **Tudor dynasty** established, marking end of long instability period culminating in Wars of the Roses
- 1485: also good candidate for turning point in population history — medieval population depression succeeded by first signs of demographic growth
- Cycle end harder to pinpoint: chose **1730** (last quinquennium of negative population growth in Wrigley et al. data)
- Another possible endpoint: **1750** (sustained population growth resumed only after that date)
- Secular cycle encompasses Tudor, Stuart, and beginning of Hanoverian dynasties

### Trends in Population and Economy

- Population trajectory of early modern England dominated by two trends:
  1. Sustained population growth between early 16th century and mid-17th century
  2. Population stagnation until mid-18th century (figure 3.1a)
- Factor to account for: acceleration of scientific/technical progress eventually (after our period) [text continues beyond page 100]

<!-- pp. 101-120 -->

## Chapter 3: England (1485-1730) — continued

### Population Pressure vs. Absolute Population

The **Malthusian-Ricardian theory** focuses on **population pressure** (people relative to resources), not absolute numbers. Carrying capacity depends on technology and yields. Between late 15th and mid-18th centuries, population stagnated while **population pressure decreased substantially** due to rising agricultural productivity. This created a typical secular cycle pattern.

> "The chief enabling factor of the Industrial Revolution was the great progress in agricultural productivity that began during the seventeenth century."

- **Figure 3.1**: Between 1450-1800, population grew from ~3 million to 8+ million, but population pressure peaked in mid-17th century then declined
- After 1600, carrying capacity rose sharply due to improving yields
- The "**misery index**" (inverse real wage) parallels population pressure closely, validating the calculation method

### The Price Revolution of the Sixteenth Century

Between 1540s and 1600, wheat prices **quadrupled** (from 20 to 80+ grams of silver per quintal). By the 1640s and 1690s, wheat exceeded 120 g per quintal—a **sixfold increase** since 1540. This was England's famous **price revolution**.

- CPI (basket of consumables) rose similarly
- Real wages collapsed as nominal wages lagged far behind price increases

### Social Structure and Elite Dynamics

**Tudor-Stuart elite hierarchy** (Table 3.1):
- **Magnates (peers)**: 60-170 individuals during the cycle—barons, viscounts, earls, dukes, bishops, top administrators
- **County gentry (middle-rank elites)**: 1,300-4,400 esquires, knights, baronets
- **Parish gentry (lesser elites)**: 5,000-15,000 "armigerous gentry"
- Lawyers, urban elites (merchants, exporters, financiers), parish clergy

**Elite expansion 1540-1640**:
- Elite numbers **tripled** while general population grew only 80% (2.8 to 5.1 million)
- Society became significantly **top-heavy**
- After 1640, middle-rank and lesser elites declined by one-third; peers continued growing slowly

**Clergy dynamics**:
- 9,000-10,000 parishes in medieval England
- By end of Plantagenet cycle, many parishes lacked resident curates or churches
- 1540-60: sharp decline in ministry entrants
- Diocese of Canterbury: 107 of 274 livings had no incumbent in 1560
- Oxford archdeaconry: rectors/vicars/curates fell from 371 (1526) to 270 (1586)
- After 1600: rapid increase; by 1640 not enough livings for demand
- **Gregory King** (1688): estimated 10,000-12,000 clergy
- Numbers at least doubled since 1500, but quality/status/income did not rise proportionally

> "The late medieval parish priest was little more than a semi-literate dirt-farmer of dubious morals: the Caroline minister of a parish had a university degree, strong religious convictions, a comfortable house, some books on his shelves."

17th-century clergyman positions became a "**spillover reservoir**" for surplus elites.

### State Finances

**Real revenues** (deflated by wheat price):
- **First phase (to ~1550)**: increased more than threefold
- **Second half of 16th century**: trend inverted, purchasing power lost two-thirds of value due to price inflation
- **1600-1640**: revenues fluctuated at low levels
- Per capita tax rates on eve of Great Revolution: **half** of late 15th-century levels
- **Revolution era**: first spurt of increase, then slight Restoration decline
- **Around 1700**: great sustained growth in real revenues, finally surpassing mid-16th century peak

### Sociopolitical Stability

**1485-1640** relatively peaceful (Table 3.2), with exceptions:
- **Mid-Tudor crisis** (1540s-50s): dynastic instability, religious strife, financial difficulties—but never escalated into full crisis
- Regional rebellions 1536-1554 (Pilgrimage of Grace, Cornwall Rising, Kett's, Wyatt's) were suppressed rapidly

**1640-60**: full-scale state collapse and civil war
- Restoration brought peaceful interlude
- **1685-92**: second instability period (Glorious Revolution)
- **18th century**: very peaceful except two Jacobite risings in Scotland (rapidly suppressed)

**Coin hoards** (Figure 3.4): dominated by Great Revolution peak; secondary peaks during Glorious Revolution and mid-16th century (possibly related to English-Scottish succession war and Irish rebellion rather than internal English instability)

### 3.2 Expansion (1485-1580)

#### General Population and Economy

Population began expanding around **1500** (some say 1480s, others 1510s). Indirect signs:
- Jump in replacement rates in inquisitions post mortem around 1500 (primarily landed elite data)
- Intense peasant house rebuilding 1440-1520
- Rebuilding of thousands of parish churches 1480-1540
- **Price inflation 1500-1530** with sluggish wage response

**1500** appears to be the turnaround when the misery index began sustained ascent.

### 3.2 Expansion — Population Estimates

**1524-25 lay subsidies**: suggest 2.3 million people (same as 15th-century minimum). Later reevaluation (Campbell 1981) suggests even lower, but conflicts with firm 1541 estimate of 2.8 million. Growth from 2.3 to 2.8 million (1520s to 1540s) implies **1% annual growth**—above the solid post-1540 rate of 0.87% (Wrigley and Schofield 1981).

**Urban-rural shift**:
- With exception of London, **all English towns lost population** 1400 to mid-16th century
- **Coventry** (Midlands): expanded 1348-1450 despite Black Death; 1440 pop. 10,000+, 1500 only 8,500-9,000; 1520 only 7,500; mid-16th century collapsed to 4,000-5,000
- Other cities losing ~half their population 1377-1525: Winchester, York, Boston, Lincoln, Lynn, Leicester, Norwich, Bristol, Southampton, Salisbury, Hereford
- Many towns "stopped being towns"—inhabitants deserted or ceased urban economy
- Trend severe in west/north Britain

**Deurbanization drivers** (reverse of previous cycle's stagflation):
- Shrunken elite + reduced elite consumption = depressed demand for luxuries/manufactures
- Towns offered reduced employment for rural migrants
- Rural areas more attractive: political stability under Tudors + expanding carrying capacity via internal colonization
- Both pull and push factors reduced rural-to-urban migration
- Urban populations shrank due to high mortality and low birth rates

By **1541**: 2.8 million (Wrigley et al. 1997). After 1540, population grew at accelerating rate exceeding 1% annually in 1580. After 1580 continued growth, crossing 4 million in early 1590s, but at declining rate.

### 3.2 Expansion — The Price Revolution's Impact

Rapid population growth + torrents of New World precious metals drove prices to unprecedented levels. Nominal wages increased **much slower**, with dire consequences for commoners.

**Early 16th-century laborer's standard of living**:
- 150 days work/year at 4 d./day = 50 s. annual income (master craftsmen: 6 d./day, 50% more)
- Five quarters wheat (14.5 hl, family of five index) cost 30 s. (60% of income)
- Rent: 5 s./year
- Clothing: 4 s./person/year (but probably mostly homemade; poor wore cast-offs)
- Shoes: 1 shilling/pair for "poor people"
- Fuel: collected wood where available; burned dried haulms/beans/cow dung where scarce
- Life not easy, but wages sufficient for food and basic shelter
- Wife/children supplemented income via textile work or seasonal agriculture

**16th-century wage collapse**:
- Agricultural wage (southern England): constant at 4 d./day until 1550, then grew to 8 d./day by 1580s-1590s
- Purchasing power declined **50% by the 1590s**

**Rent dynamics**:
- Land rents rose, but initially slower than prices
- Shifted distribution of agricultural profits from landlord to tenant
- During stagflation phase, rents increased faster than prices (direction reversed)

### 3.2 Expansion — Elites

Elite expansion **lagged behind** general population growth until mid-16th century. Peer numbers fluctuated near 60 most of the century. Knight numbers increased from 375 (1490) to 600 (1560)—only 60% increase while population more than doubled.

**Mid-16th century elite fortunes transformed** by the **dissolution of monasteries**—the greatest land transfer in English history after Norman conquest:
- Church net yearly income: £400,000 (Hoskins estimate), 60%+ passed to Crown
- **1536-54**: large portion (valued £1.1 million) sold to gentry
- Created economic basis for subsequent elite class expansion

### 3.3 Stagflation (1580-1640)

#### Population and Economy

Population continued growing but **decelerating**, reaching 5.3 million in 1640s. Between 1480-1640, English population **more than doubled**.

> "The doubling of the population in the 120 years before the civil war is the critical variable of the period, an event the ramifications of which spread out into every aspect of the society." (Stone 1972:67)

**Consequences**:
- **Drastic decline in land-to-peasant ratio**: before 1560, 57% of landholdings were 1+ acres; after 1620, only 36%
- Proportion of landless peasants drastically increased: laborers owning only cottage+garden/croft rose from 11% (pre-1560) to 40% (post-1620)

**Grain price inflation**: eightfold from 1500 to 1640 (nominal; expressed in silver, sixfold increase).

**Jack Goldstone regression model** (1991):
- Logtransformed population + harvest quality index + time (technical change) explained **99% of variance** in log-transformed prices
- Most important factor driving inflation: **population growth**

**Money supply expansion** (bullion from Americas) also contributed, but:
- Even in Spain (greatest effect), sustained inflation started **1540**—before substantial precious metal arrivals
- Inflation continued after 1620 despite declining bullion imports
- Primary mover: population growth
- Evidence: **disparity between food/fuel vs. manufactures** (Table 3.3)—grain increased eightfold, manufactures only threefold

**Real wages** declined **40%+** between 1500-1640.

**Land rents** (Table 3.4):
- 16th century: rents increased in line with prices
- After 1580: rents **rapidly outpaced inflation**
- Result: real rents stagnated during expansion, so landlords' only revenue increase was acquiring more land
- Stagflation phase: real rents grew rapidly
- Landlords enjoyed substantial income increase (e.g., average gentry in Warwickshire: **400% increase** in standard of living, 1530s to 1630s)

### 3.3 Stagflation — Urbanization and Trade

16th century: **London population growth** kept pace with total England population (2-3% of total). After 1600, London mushroomed: **10%+ of total population** by mid-18th century. Early 19th century: London growth lagged (9% by 1830).

**Urbanization exhibited typical secular dynamics**: peak of urbanization lagged substantially behind population peak. Not limited to London: between 1603-1670, urban population of East Anglia grew 50% while total area population rose only 11%. Largest towns grew fastest: **Norwich** 12,000 to 30,000 during 17th century.

**Trade and industry paralleled urbanization**:
- Shipping owned in London: 12,300 tons (1582) → 35,300 tons (1629) → ~150,000 tons (1686)
- Value of food/raw material imports to London: £1 million (1622) → £3 million (1700)
- **Iron production**: 140 tons/year (1500) → 10,000 tons/year (1600) → 20,000 tons (1660)

### 3.3 Stagflation — Elites

Between 1540-1640, elite strata expanded **much faster** than general population (Table 3.1). Population grew 80% (2.8 to 5.1 million); elite numbers **tripled** (6,300 to 18,500 aristocratic families).

**Radical increase across all elite strata**:
- Peers: 60 → 160
- Baronets and knights: 500 → 1,400
- Esquires: 800 → 3,000
- Armigerous gentry: 5,000 → 15,000

**Gentry involvement in local/central government**:
- Justices of peace (four sample counties): 60 (late 15th c.) → 96 (1562) → 166 (1636) → 396 (1702)—**6.6-fold increase**
- House of Commons: 300 → 500 members
- Gentry component rose from 50% to 75%—implying **2.5-fold increase** in gentry MPs

**Professional expansion**:
- Lawyers, doctors, secretarial/administrative assistants: sustained striking increase, peaking 1640
- Attorneys (Court of Common Pleas): 342 (1578-1633) → 1,383
- Clergy also increased starting 1560, peaking 1640

**Causal factors for gentry rise**:
1. **First phase (~1540-60)**: gentry profited from massive land transfer of church property
2. **Second phase (after 1580)**: benefited from rising real rents
3. Crown financial worsening forced land sales: Crown lands sold 1589-1635 = £2.1 million, most to gentry

**Rising conspicuous consumption and inequality**:

> "In 1485 most English people, even well to do, wore similar dress. Women wore plain, loose-fitting garments and men did likewise. Fine but simple linen was as acceptable in formal costume as ornate silk.... The third and fourth decades of the sixteenth century, however, saw an explosive growth in the consumption of expensive and ornate costume. Demand rose enormously, especially among the wealthy, who purchased expensive brocades, velvets, and silks for new and splendid costumes.... During the reign of Elizabeth, men changed their fashions entirely; their clothes became more elaborate and distinctive.... Women matched male attire with exquisitely decorated farthingales and fine damask gowns.... [T]he sixteenth century closed with a 'wild orgy of extravagance,' as the provincial gentry attempted to emulate the London *haute monde* by wearing extravagant costumes and hats with twelve-inch crowns." (Berger 1993:20-21)

**Elite numbers exceeded "sustainable level"**:
- Competition for jobs and patronage intensified
- Dire consequences for political stability

**Two indicators of intraelite competition** (Goldstone 1991):

1. **University enrollments**: increased drastically during second half 16th century, peaking 1640—not simply part of millennial literacy increase, because by 1750s (when intraelite competition subsided) enrollments had declined to pre-1600 levels (Figure 3.5)

> "The universities were turning out an educated clergy and laity in excess of suitable job opportunities, and were thus creating a large and influential group of discontented 'Outs'" (Stone 1972:96)

2. **Litigation among gentry**: 1640 probably more litigation per capita at Westminster than any time before or since. By 1750, common law hit "spectacular all-time low." From 1640-1750, gentry appearing in Courts of Common Pleas (plaintiffs or defendants) dropped 65%+. Increase in litigation was not simply result of modern society rise.

**Third indicator: dueling epidemic**:
- Number of duels/challenges in newsletters/correspondence: five (1580s) → nearly twenty (1590s) → peak of thirtynine (1610s)
- Rose in tandem with (and perhaps part of) late 16th/early 17th-century crime wave
- **Homicide data** (Eisner 2003): general crime incidence increased/declined in step with population pressure and inverse real wages (Figure 3.6)

### 3.3 Stagflation — The State

**Fiscal strain intensified**:
- Crown real expenses increased proportionately to population while real income declined
- Expanded elite numbers imposed greater patronage costs
- From mid-16th century on, Crown forced to sell assets, levy forced loans, seek parliamentary grants even in peacetime
- By 1630s: Crown lands largely gone, unpaid Crown debt exceeded ordinary revenues
- Interest on debt > ordinary revenues
- Prior efforts to secure extraordinary revenues alienated elites to the point they were unlikely to acquiesce to further fiscal demands/entreaties

**State debt and pensions**:
- State debt: £400,000 (1603) → £900,000 (1618)
- Elizabeth "perks": £8,000/annum to peers
- James I: £105,000
- 1626 pensions: £140,000/year (~quarter of total cash revenues)
- Some legitimate government service demands, but increasing part fueled "parasitic court aristocracy preying upon revenues"

### 3.4 Crisis (1640-60)

#### The Onset of the Civil War

By 1640, social pressure from population expansion, elite overproduction, and state insolvency reached **breaking point**. **Jack Goldstone** (1991) used social/political indicators to quantify growing pressure in *Revolution and Rebellion in the Early Modern World*.

**Mass mobilization potential (MMP)** of general populace has three components:
1. Degree of misery (real wages)
2. Youthful age structure (increases mobilization potential)
3. Urban growth (concentrates poor young sons and discontented commoners, amplifying discontent from poverty)

Goldstone combined these into one MMP measure (Figure 3.7b). MMP increased during 1530-1750 but **not enough alone** to cause state collapse when elites were unified.

**Second trend: loss of elite unity**:
- Favorable economic conjuncture for landowners during stagflation → massive elite expansion
- But surplus extracted from peasants stagnated/declined after 1620
- Real rents peaked first quarter 17th century, then declined thereafter (Table 3.4)
- Average income per elite capita **declined on eve of Great Revolution**
- Pain not spread evenly: many impoverished, others continued doing well
- Growing segment faced downward mobility + visible rise in inequality
- One avenue for preserving status: seek employment with state/church/magnates
- But employment could not keep pace with growing elite aspirants (most with university degrees)

> "Limits on available land, civil and ecclesiastical offices, and royal patronage led to increasingly polarized factional battles between patron-client groups for available spoils" (Goldstone 1991:119)

When one faction won, it attempted to completely exclude rivals. Example: **George Villiers, Duke of Buckingham**, monopolized court patronage 1617-1628.

> "the ascendancy of Buckingham...transformed abuse into a scandal of systematic exploitation." (David Loades 1999:308)

**Third trend: fiscal difficulties**:
- State finances also under pressure from rising military costs (military revolution of 16th century)
- Revenues failed to match outlays
- Real revenues declined during second half of 16th century, stagnated 1600-1640 (Figure 3.3)
- State's revenue-raising ability could not keep up with increasing fiscal demands
- Crown used expedients for short-term relief: sale of Crown lands, offices, titles, debasement of coinage, borrowing from London and international markets
- By 1630s: Crown lands gone, state debt >£1 million
- State on brink of bankruptcy; took slight shock (Bishops' Wars) to tip over precipice

#### Economic Consequences of the Civil War

Civil wars 1642-1651 (with intervals), followed by continuing political instability until 1660 Restoration. During this period:
- **~10% of male population killed**
- Towns sacked: Birmingham (1643), Bolton (1644), Leicester (1645)
- Castles/houses of nobility/gentry sacked or destroyed to prevent future use
- Local studies document property damage extent

**Gloucester example**: remained under one side's control throughout (siege not particularly destructive), yet 1646 investigation found 241 houses destroyed, 1,250 people homeless; suburbs not rebuilt until 18th century.

**Countryside suffered more**:
- Almost every village in Inshire (area around Gloucester) plundered at least once, many repeatedly
- Tewkesbury region (Gloucestershire): plundered 1643, 1644, 1645
- Pillage + heavy taxation by both sides → land values declined so far tenants refused to pay rents and abandoned leases
- Some villages reportedly depopulated

**Land transfer**: enormous amount changed hands (though much later reversed):
- £3.5 million Crown lands
- £2.5 million ecclesiastical lands
- £1 million+ royalist lands

<!-- pp. 121-140 -->

### Civil War Impact on England

- Land confiscation caused massive dislocation; purchasers sought quick returns, tenants who couldn't produce title evidence faced eviction
- Rural poor largely neutral during 1640s-1650s; only intervention was "clubmen" assemblies protecting fields/crops/cattle from both armies' depredations
- Civil war did enormous economic damage, but slight population decline offset by rapidly increasing agricultural productivity
- Wheat yields increased 50%+ during 17th century, compensating for lost cultivated land from warfare

### Population Demographics (England)

**Demographic machinery** (mid-16th to 19th century):
- Work by **Wrigley, Schofield** provides firm grasp of population changes
- Temporal variation in growth due to changes in both birth and death rates, but with different lags
- **Nuptiality** explained large proportion of crude birth rate variance
- Proportion never marrying: 5% (mid-16th century) → 25% (1650) → 5% (next hundred years)
- Average age at first marriage increased 1550-1650, reducing average children per married woman by at least one

**Mortality trends**:
- 17th century = era of steadily worsening mortality, peaking around 1680
- Improvement around 1700, then mortality peak in 1720s (particularly infants/children)
- 18th century saw gradual improvement in life expectation
- Migration grew 1550, peaked 1650, then secular decline until 1780s

**Famine, disease, and war**:
- Worst famine: **1594-97** (longest run of bad harvests in 16th century)
- **1622-23**: grain price rise coincided with severe cloth industry depression, leaving many unable to buy food
- Clear evidence of deaths from starvation (vagrants, elderly widows, pauper children)
- By mid-17th century, widespread famine became thing of the past
- **Plague** gradually released grip by end of 15th century; recurred during 16th century
- **1570-1670**: period of recurrent plague epidemics, culminating in **Great Plague of London (1665)**
- Late 17th century: plague suddenly went extinct in England
- **Typhus epidemics** often flared when internecine fighting intensified (e.g., 1648)
- Smallpox epidemics in London often exceeded 2,000 deaths during 1670s

## 3.5 Depression (1660–1730)

### Population Stagnation

- England's population declined from peak of **5.4 million** (1650s) to **5 million** (1680s)
- Very slow, uneven growth thereafter; still 5.4 million in 1730
- Vigorous growth began only after 1750
- Main factor: mortality (fecundity showed increasing trend from 1650 on)
- Adult mortality improved during 18th century, but offset by high infant/child mortality until 1750s
- **Infanticide indictment rates** peaked in late 17th century, slowly declined during 18th century (figure 3.8)
- Suggests high infant mortality may have been conscious/unconscious birth control attempt

**Three factors explain stagnation puzzle**:

1. **Urbanization's negative effect** on population growth
   - Peak of urbanization considerably lagged population peak
   - 1700-1750: London had 10% of country's population (fourfold increase from 1580, slightly greater than 1830's 9%)
   - High urbanization during depression phase was important factor in unfavorable demographic regime
   - **E.A. Wrigley** calculated: 1690-1710 immigration to London (~30,000/year average) absorbed half the natural increase of entire country's population
   - London's plague losses made good by second year after outbreak
   - London disproportionately attracted female immigrants (demand for domestic servants)
   - Result: fewer than 90 men per 100 women, further depressing population increase ability

2. **Real rents jumped 70%** (last quarter 17th century → first quarter 18th century)
   - Data from **Robert Allen** suggests elites in better position to "turn the screws" on peasants after intraelite disunity ended
   - Increased rent partly compensated by productivity increase, but still must have substantially decreased peasants' personal consumption
   - Real wages during this period generally kept increasing (never reached 15th-century maximum)
   - But if peasants were overexploited, should have spilled into real wages (which we don't see)
   - Perhaps increased rents simply retarded rural population growth, contributing indirectly to slow population growth

3. **Climate worsening** in early 18th century
   - Associated with **Maunder Minimum** of solar activity
   - Climate cooling affected Europe from London to Moscow, causing crop failures and famines
   - France and Russia experienced declines during first decade of century
   - Usually attributed to wars of **Louis XIV** and **Peter I**, but equally possible explanation is exogenous climate factor

### The Elites

**Civil wars' impact on landed elites**:
- Particularly affected: royalists (lands confiscated and sold)
- Combined value of confiscated properties: over **£1.25 million**
- Partially reversed because royalists bought estates back before 1660
- However, most incurred heavy debt to do so
- Nearly **£1.5 million** raised from ~3,000 royalists by Committee of Compounding
- Heavy taxation on top of exactions and composition fines
- Royalists had to sell part of lands after long period receiving no rents; these lands not restored after 1660
- Although bulk of royalist landlords retained position, many greatly impoverished; some lesser elite families had stiff fight to survive

**Last quarter 17th century**:
- Almost all landlords experienced further income reduction from low agricultural prices and falling rents
- Had to bear much greater rate of taxation (after 1692: taxes absorbing 1/5th of income for many gentry)
- Many at lower fringes of gentry had to part with land
- Declining economic fortunes reflected in consumption patterns
- Early 17th century: rapid expansion in luxury imports; after civil wars: significant change of ethos among elites (reduction in conspicuous expenditure on houses, clothes, entertaining)

**Economic malaise extended into 18th century**:
- Average wealth of esquires in Norfolk and Suffolk: **£700** (1628-40) → **£330** (1700-40)
- Drop of 50%+ in real terms
- Result: increased downward mobility, almost automatic descent by younger sons into lower social strata
- Example: Cumbria 1680-1750, only one younger son of gentry able to purchase land and climb back into gentry
- Upward mobility into gentry ranks similarly restricted
- "With a few exceptions, days when man of fortune converted wealth into landed acreage already numbered by end of 17th century" — practice disappeared by mid-18th century

**Elite numbers shrank** (late 17th → mid-18th century):
- Partly result of diminished upward and enhanced downward mobility
- Partly result of elite demographics (**Hollingsworth 1964**)
- **Replacement rate** (average number of adult sons per father) among upper-rank elites declined below 1.0 after 1650
- Kept declining (to below 0.8) until first quarter of 18th century (figure 3.9)
- During next quarter century: increased to 1.1, but not until after 1750 did replacement rate achieve healthy level of 1.3-1.4 (mid-19th century)
- "Collectively, peerage, baronetage and knighthood totalled only **1075 by 1760**, fall of 30% since beginning of century; among lesser gentry in counties position appears same"
- Numbers given in table 3.5
- Overall elite numbers declined from 18,500 to 13,000 (see table 3.1)
- As number of landowning families shrank, average size of estates tended to increase

### Consequences of Civil War: Changed Social Mood

**Before 1642**: England could look back to century of internal peace
- Risings of 1549 quelled without undue difficulty
- Somerset overthrown without bloodshed
- Northumberland's succession attempt collapsed with only hint of civil war
- Mary's regime achieved objectives, never looked in danger of overthrow
- Harvests of mid-1550s and mid-1590s inflicted terrible sufferings on poor, but nowhere set off large-scale risings
- Elizabeth crushed Presbyterian movement and contained Catholic threat
- Essex's revolt collapsed into black comedy in 24 hours
- Succession crisis at Elizabeth's death solved dextrously and peacefully

> "The last peasant revolt serious enough to send gentry fleeing from their homes in terror had been in 1549…[and] by 1640 two generations of gentry had gone by since the great fear, and memories had grown dim" (Stone 1972:76)

- Few among elites could foresee depth of societal collapse from their actions
- Intraelite conflict allowed to escalate unchecked until it acquired dynamic of its own

**1640s-1660s**: most protracted and intense period of sociopolitical instability since Wars of the Roses
- Following two decades (after Stuart Restoration) were, in contrast, internally peaceful
- Alternation between civil war and internal stability provides another example of **bigenerational cycle**
- Around 1680: social pressure increasing again
- Three parliaments came and went in rapid succession (1679-81)
- Reign of "legal terror" against Whigs and dissenters began 1682 (several executed, others driven into exile)
- **Charles II** died 1685, succeeded peacefully by **James II**, but same year two risings took place
- **Argyll and Monmouth rebellions** rapidly suppressed
- When **William of Orange** landed three years later, rebellion quickly spread, James II chose to flee England
- Result: **"Glorious Revolution"** (1688-90, with aftershocks in Scotland and Ireland until 1692)
- Stuart dynasty abolished, Parliament offered crown to **William and Mary** jointly
- Remarkable feature: relative bloodlessness
- Whigs and Tories disagreed sharply on issues, but differences patched up largely as result of "men's recollection of what happened forty-five years earlier, when unity of the propertied class had been broken"
- Compromise reached by elites held up remarkably well throughout whole 18th century and beyond
- After Glorious Revolution: no major rebellions in Great Britain (union of England and Scotland after 1707), apart from two Jacobite risings
- **1715-16** and **1745-46**: these aftershocks followed Glorious Revolution at roughly generational intervals (first led by James II's son, second by his grandson)

### The Turnaround Point: The Mid-eighteenth Century

- By mid-18th century, last echoes of early modern crisis had dissipated
- Real wages increased throughout early 18th century, reaching peak around 1750
- Last serious disturbance of internal peace: Jacobite rising in 1745-46
- Numbers of elites reached minimum sometime around 1760s
- Ruling class achieved unprecedented degree of unity
- After stabilization of political system in 1721, landowners drastically reduced number of both electors and contested elections to Parliament
- Elite cohesiveness allowed them to tax themselves at unprecedented rate for early modern society
- Ability served England well during century-long conflict with France culminating in Napoleonic Wars
- By mid-18th century, stage was set for astonishing demographic, economic, and territorial expansion England was to experience during next century and a half

## 3.6 Conclusion

- Demographic, economic, political, and social trends in early modern England (roughly 1485-1730) generally moved consistent with **demographic-structural theory** predictions
- Case of English Revolution fits demographic-structural theory particularly well (as argued by **Goldstone 1991**)
- Three ingredients of revolution: financial crisis of state, acute competition and factionalism among elites, existence of large body of disaffected commoners who could be mobilized by parliamentary leaders against royalists in London
- Measures of all these processes increased during second half of 16th and early 17th centuries, peaking around 1640

**Operation of demographic-structural machinery influenced by other factors**:
- Geopolitical environment apparently played minor role during this cycle
- Although **William of Orange** invasion precipitated Glorious Revolution, success entirely due to internal factors
- Long-term fluctuations in climate probably important in contributing to population stagnation (late 17th → early 18th century)
- During this cycle: no traumatic exogenous events comparable to Black Death arrival in 1348
- Increase in epidemics incidence during 17th century apparently an endogenous process

**Most important factor outside core variables**: acceleration of social evolution eventually resulting in **Industrial Revolution**
- Because of scientific and agronomic advances, crop yields began increasing shortly after 1600
- During 17th century yields doubled
- As a result, carrying capacity also doubled
- This dramatic increase in carrying capacity explains why population numbers in England did not collapse to same degree as Spain or Germany in 17th century
- When dividing total population by estimated carrying capacity, observe that resulting variable (population pressure on resources) oscillates in very similar fashion to Plantagenet cycle (figure 3.10)

**Figure 3.10 shows**:
- (a) Raw population numbers, average yields, and estimated carrying capacity
- (b) Population pressure (population numbers in relation to carrying capacity, in percent of K) and misery index (inverse real wages)
- Population pressure on resources oscillates similarly to Plantagenet cycle

## Appendix to Chapter 3: Detrending Population Data

### England 1450–1800: Population Data

- Population numbers for 1540-1800 from Table A.9.1 in **Wrigley et al. (1997)**
- Data of Wrigley et al. resampled at decadal intervals
- For 1450-1525: data from **Hatcher (1977)**, sampled at ten-year intervals
- Value for 1530 interpolated
- Population data show increasing long-term trend
- Such nonstationarity violates assumptions of nonlinear time-series analysis; thus data need to be **detrended**

### Detrending the English Population Data

- **Agrarian revolution** in England started during 17th century
- Can trace this revolution using data on long-term changes in grain yields (**Grigg 1989, Overton 1996**)
- Average wheat yields in 13th century: ~10 bushels grain per acre
- Yields declined slightly during 14th-15th centuries to 8 bushels per acre (perhaps from worsening global climate)
- Even as late as 1580s, yields still at late medieval level
- During 17th century: yields began improving, increasing to ca. 15 in 1700 and 20-21 in early 19th century
- **Net yields** (subtracting seed corn) were lower
- Example: typical late medieval seeding rates = 2 bushels per acre; thus net yield = only 6 bushels per acre
- Net yields from Grigg and Overton plotted in figure 3.10a
- To capture rising trend: fitted data after 1580 with straight line (see figure 3.10a, note log-scale)
- Linear relationship appears adequate description of trend
- By coincidence, **1 bushel of net yield per acre translates exactly into 1 million persons of carrying capacity**

**Calculating carrying capacity**:
- Assuming total potentially arable area of 12 million acres and that one individual (averaging over adults and children) needed minimum of 1/4 (8 bushels or 2.9 hectoliters) of grain per year
- Carrying capacity of England shown in dashed line in figure 3.10a

**Detrended population**:
- Obtained by dividing observed population numbers by estimated carrying capacity
- Detrended population (also thought of as population pressure on resources) defined as N'(t) = N(t)/K(t)
- Critical assumption: K is proportional to net yield, Y
- Since Y is only quantity varying with time in formula, other components (total arable area, consumption minimum) being constant multipliers, K will wax and wane in step with Y
- Exact values of constant multiples do not matter, since interested in relative changes of population pressure
- Estimate of K based on potentially arable area (not actually cultivated area, which fluctuated with population numbers)
- Latter quantity fluctuated little across centuries (for example, as result of inundation of coastal areas during Middle Ages or more recent reclamation using modern methods)
- Can be approximated with constant without serious loss of precision

**Test of appropriateness**:
- Regressing estimated population pressure on real wages reported by **Allen (2001)**
- Very close inverse relationship between these two variables
- Not a very good one if we were to use nondetrended population numbers
- As figure 3.10b shows, population pressure and inverse real wages fluctuated virtually in perfect synchrony

---

## Chapter 4: Medieval France: The Capetian Cycle (1150–1450)

## 4.1 Overview of the Cycle

- Official start of **Capetian dynasty**: accession of **Hugh Capet** to French throne in 987
- Before 1200: Capetian kings directly controlled rather insignificant extent of territory, overshadowed by other north French polities (most important: Normans and Angevins)
- Integrative trend set in gradually during 12th century
- Important landmarks: consolidation of royal lands under **Louis VI "the Fat"** (1108-37) and activities of **Suger, abbot of St. Denis** (1122-1151)
- For this reason, date beginning of high medieval cycle in France to **1150**

**Cycle phases**:
- **1150-1250**: expansion phase (rapid population growth, enormous territorial expansion controlled by French kings)
- **Mid-13th century**: stagflation phase set in (population growth slowed, gradually ceased altogether)
- Onset of crisis signaled by famines of **1315-17**, reached peak with **Black Death** arrival
- Followed by military defeats, peasant uprisings, first civil war
- Temporary stabilization achieved during 1360s, but proved very fragile
- **Depression phase**: characterized by high sociopolitical instability and absence of sustained population growth, lasted until mid-15th century
- Around **1450**: new integrative trend became obvious (end of medieval cycle, beginning of early modern one)

**Notes on dating**:
- Dates for cycles and phases within cycles are provisional and subject to change should new evidence be forthcoming
- Dates are "fuzzy" — specific date had to be selected within broad penumbra of events occurring in ten or twenty years surrounding it
- Thus, 1450 really means 1440-60, or even 1430-70
- Division of continuous cycle into discrete stages is useful device for organizing presentation but does not mean transitions between successive phases occurred abruptly, in a single year

**Territorial and regional challenges**:
- Large and spatially heterogeneous entity of kingdom of France offers further challenges to historical analysis
- Country we know as modern France was, in medieval period, aggregation of different polities and cultural regions, which could and did move out of synchrony
- Chronology that follows primarily relevant to territorial core of French state, region north of Loire inhabited by speakers of **Langue d'Oïl**

### Population and Economy

- Perhaps **6 million** people in France around 1100
- Close to **20 million** in 1328
- Such tripling of population during 12th-13th centuries (with fastest growth concentrated around 1200) quite typical of Western European countries
- During century after Black Death, French population declined to around **10 million** (figure 4.1a)
- Numbers refer to population within modern French borders
- Peak population in area controlled by Capetians was smaller, perhaps around 16 million (Sumption 1991:10)
- In 1328: officials of royal treasury counted nearly **2,470,000 households** (great fiefs and princely appanages, not taxed by king, were excluded)

**Food prices** (expressed in silver equivalents):
- Exhibited very similar pattern to population dynamics: 13th-century increase, followed by decline to minimum toward mid-15th century (figure 4.1b)
- Also shorter-term oscillations (of about fifty years) superimposed on secular cycles

### Social Structure

**Power hierarchy**:
- Top: great territorial magnates (lay lords: king, dukes, counts, barons; prelates: abbots, bishops, archbishops)
- Below them: middle and lower rank elites (from more substantially knights to relatively poor country squires)

**Income perspective** from **Edouard Perroy's (1962)** study of nobility in county of Forez (south-central France, late 13th century):
- Top: **Count of Forez** with **12,000 *livres viennois*** (l.v.) annual income
- Below him: 2-3 barons with incomes of 1,000-2,000 l.v. per year
- Twenty or so substantial knights, each with castle: incomes between 100-500 l.v.
- Holders of fortified houses: worse off, with incomes of 50-100 l.v. per year
- Bottom: about 100 lesser gentry whose incomes varied between 25-50 l.v. per year
- Basic minimum on which single person could live in modest comfort at time: **5 l.v.** (typical allowance to young nobleman attending university or pension to widow of lesser gentry)
- In other words, 25 l.v. per year was enough to maintain family of 4-5, but left no surplus for status-seeking
- Sum of 25 l.v. at that time equivalent to **£5**; thus minimum incomes of lesser gentry in France and England essentially the same
- Substantial knight in Forez with minimum income of 100 l.v. had counterpart in English knight or esquire with **£20 per annum**

<!-- pp. 141-160 -->

### Land Fragmentation and Peasant Impoverishment

- Population pressure drove progressive fragmentation of peasant landholdings
- In **Dauphiné**, 75% of peasants had less than 2 ha; in **Hainault**, 60% held 1 ha or less
- Minimum viable peasant holding: ~6 ha (in three-field system at 10 hl/ha yield)
- From 6 ha, after paying **tithe** (1/9) and **terrage** (1/7), peasants had 22 hl (16 quintals) for family consumption
- After reserving 8 hl for seed, family of 4-5 left with enough grain to survive
- Less than 20% of peasants had 6+ ha holdings—the vast majority could not feed themselves
- Result: vast rural proletariat dependent on outside employment
- Many migrated to towns, depressing urban wages and fueling urban growth

### Land Prices and Rents

- Land scarcity drove prices up dramatically during 13th century
- In **Normandy**: acre cost 2 livres early 13th century, 20 livres a century later
- In **Picardy**: *journal* (~0.4 ha) varied 1-4 livres first half of 13th century, rose above 4 livres and reached 6-10 livres by 1300
- Regional variations: **Beauce** (near Chartres) saw prices decline, but these were exceptions
- **Rents increased faster than prices**: around **Lille**, rents increased fivefold 1276-1316

### Urban Development During Stagflation

- Rural population growth slowed and stagnated during stagflation phase
- Urban population grew rapidly—over 70% of planted towns in France outside **Gascony** appeared 1250-1315
- Rural land clearance (assarts) concentrated in expansion phase; urban expansion in stagflation phase
- **Paris** population doubled during 13th century, continued growing into 14th
- Cities 10,000-20,000 increased to twenty+; dozen in 20,000-50,000 range
- Cities grew from migrant influx, trade/crafts expansion, and as refuges during instability
- Rural dwellers abandoned exposed *plat pays* for fortified towns as violence increased

### Elite Overproduction

- **Jonathan Sumption**: minor noblemen suffered from "smallness of holdings, result of generations of pious bequests and family partitions"
- **Primogeniture** never rigidly applied in western/northern France—not even accepted in principle
- By early 14th century, holdings of lesser nobility "reduced to barely workable parcels"
- In **Ile-de-France**: at least 1/4 of noble vassals of Parisian abbeys and Crown had income under 10 livres
- Many petty nobles mortgaged land or sold to rich peasants
- Survivors would "turn to war for their living and finally to brigandage"

### The State: Revenue Crisis

- Royal revenues increased nominally but could not keep pace with inflation
- **Louis IX** (~1250): ordinary revenues ~0.25 million l.t.
- Late 13th century (**Philip IV**): annual income 0.4-0.6 million l.t.
- **Charles IV** (1322-28): 0.28 million l.t. ordinary revenues = only 53% of father's purchasing power
- **Philip VI** (1328-50): despite vast Valois appanage, ordinary revenues only 80% of Philip IV's in real terms

## 4.4 Crisis (1315-65)

### Population Collapse

- By early 14th century, France "literally crammed with people"—ecosystem strained to breaking point
- Collapse result of concatenation: famine, pestilence, war

**Famine Returns:**
- Agricultural production barely sufficient by early 14th century
- Famines rare in 13th century, returned repeatedly in 14th
- First major famine: **1302** (Carpentier and Le Mené 1996:353)
