- Sustained population decline began after multi-annual famine of **1315-17**
- **1315-30**: series of terrible winters brought famine—"alarming warning sign"

**The Black Death:**
- First wave of bubonic plague swept France **1348-49**
- Mortality: 1/4 to 1/2 (80-90% in some cases), depending on region
- Recurrent plague epidemics hit France at ~decade intervals rest of century
- Example: Lyon's second highest mortality peak after 1348-49 was in **1361**

**Social Pyramid Distortion:**
- Plague imposed disproportionate mortality on the poor
- At **Albi** (1343-1357): very poor (owning possessions under 50 livres) fell from 76% to 62%
- Middle stratum (50-200 livres) increased 21% to 28.5%
- Rich (over 200 livres) doubled from 4.2% to 8.1%
- Plague made social pyramid even more top-heavy (same pattern as England)

**Overall Decline:**
- French population began declining after 1300, precipitous drop after Black Death 1348
- Further losses from recurrent epidemics (1361) and **Hundred Years' War**
- Population declined ~50%: from 20 million to 10 million
- Low-level fluctuations continued for century, increase only after second half of 15th century

### Improved Living Standards for Survivors

- Population collapse brought economic relief to peasants and workers
- Real wages and consumption levels improved dramatically toward end of 14th century
- **1338**: Provençal drovers ate crude barley bread
- After Black Death: barley bread "good enough only for sheepdogs"—laborers entitled to wheat bread

**Wage Data (Table 4.3):**
- Building worker at Rouen: 1.5 s.t. (1320) → 4.5 s.t. (1500)
- Infantryman: 1.0 s.t. (1320) → 3.4 s.t. (1500)
- Dramatic improvement 1320-1380, especially peak at 1380

### Lords and Peasants: Intensifying Contradiction

- Elite overproduction at 14th century start: shrinking peasant surplus supporting more elites
- **Guy Bois**: lord compelled to draw more from peasant production; additional drain endangered peasant self-sufficiency
- This contradiction "insoluble and disruptive"
- Post-1315 famines affected poorest strata; plague disproportionately killed poor
- Result: proportion of elites to general population increased beyond already high levels

**Elite Income Crisis:**
- Elites forced to seek "other means" to preserve caste
- End result: intensification of intraelite conflict for scarce resources
- Poorer nobles particularly hard hit
- In 13th century Forez: many lesser gentry lived on 25 *livres viennois* (£5) or less
- As food prices doubled/tripled (1310s), noble households "slipped below minimum level of consumption consistent with noble status"
- Some noble households starved during early 14th century famines
- Mid-century: literally tens of thousands of destitute noblemen of fighting age
- **Philippe Contamine**: 25,000-30,000 nobles flocked to army of **Philip VI** in 1340 hoping for military pay
- King and lieutenants "surprised, even disconcerted" by huge numbers responding to call to arms
- This disorganized, undisciplined crowd contributed to French defeats at **Crécy** and **Poitiers**

**Administrative Expansion:**
- Elite overproduction extended beyond military class
- **1314-1343**: principal judicial officers of royal courts in Paris increased fourfold
- Number of notaries increased similarly
- 'Sergeants' (enforcement officials) increased sevenfold

### State Collapse

- Intensity of intraelite competition rising while state losing ability to keep peace and protect from external invasion
- Main problem: **financial**

**Revenue vs. Expenses:**
- Before **Philip IV** (1270-1314): Crown lived off ordinary revenues, occasional extraordinary levies (e.g., ransom Louis IX from Egypt)
- Late 13th century: ordinary revenues stagnated in real terms while military expenses soared
- Philip IV exploited feudal rights, debased currencies, imposed forced loans on bourgeois, manipulated currency
- These methods caused resentment; first half 14th century saw increasing resistance to taxation

**The Binary Contradiction:**
- **Guy Bois**: lord vs. peasant contradiction
- Should be modified to **trinary**: productive class vs. elites vs. state
- State competed with elites for shrinking surplus

**Military Consequences:**
- Lack of consensus on national taxation directly affected ability to wage war vs. English
- **Philip VI** (1328-50) and **John II** (1350-64) unable to collect subsidies except in times of outright conflict
- Constant state of financial distress
- France always unprepared when military operations resumed after truce
- Military disasters **1346** (defeat at **Crécy**) and **1347** (loss of **Calais**) finally persuaded Estates General (1355-56) to authorize taxation
- But attempts to impose taxes met resistance at local level
- Estates unable to produce promised money
- Collapse of royal finances followed by general collapse of French state, triggered by defeat and capture of **John II at Poitiers** (1356)

### Sociopolitical Instability Dynamics

**Assault Statistics (Cerisy, Normandy—Table 4.4):**
- 1314-1346: 1.2 assaults/year, 2% involving weapons
- 1370-1414: 4.9 assaults/year, 25% involving weapons
- 1451-1458: 4.3 assaults/year, 40% involving weapons
- 1471-1486: 0.7 assaults/year, 8% involving weapons

**The Hundred Years' War as Internal Conflict:**
- Not simply dynastic conflict between French and English kings
- Period of great political instability primarily *within* France
- **Braudel**: better called "hundred years of hostilities rather than hundred-year-war"
- Recurrent state collapse and civil war at multiple levels
- Highest-level factions: king, duke of **Guyenne** (also king of England), duke of **Burgundy**, count of **Flanders**
- Conflict descended through magnates (counts/barons), nobility factions, down to knights and peasants
- Multilevel nature illustrated by later medieval **Gascony**: one military frontier in Anglo-French struggle
- Regional level (1290-mid-15th century): feud between houses of **Armagnac** and **Foix** over viscounty of **Béarn**
- Local level: private wars between lesser nobility in Gascony—at least 12 outbreaks of intraelite violence 1290-1327
- Bands of *routiers* and *écorcheurs* robbed and killed indiscriminately—lords and peasants—or ran "protection rackets" from castles

**Crime Wave:**
- Interpersonal violence increased in parallel with organized violence
- Crime wave during 14th century peaked ~1400, subsided by second half of 15th century

### Regional Examples of Breakdown

**Frontier Regions (~1300):**
- First instability signs in frontier regions toward 1300
- **Gascony**: collapse of order from 1290
- Urbanized **Flanders**: social conflicts 1279-1281, again ~1300
- Tension between established urban patriciate and newly enriched bourgeoisie (using proletariat as shock troops)
- Led to fullblown rebellion and **battle of the Spurs** (1302)—Flemish infantry crushed French cavalry
- **1325-26**: Flanders urban communities rose against ruler **Count Louis of Nevers**
- Burghers massacred by French army at field of **Cassel** (1328); Flanders temporarily pacified
- **1337**: Flemish revolted again under leadership of **van Arteveldt of Ghent**, expelled Louis of Nevers
- Flemish rebellion opened northern route for English invasion under **Edward III**

**Central France:**
- North/east France (Picardy, Burgundy): sites of baronial movement against royal taxation during **Louis X** (1314-16)
- County of **Artois**: revolt complicated by internecine fighting between **Robert of Artois** and aunt **Mahaut**
- Robert lost struggle, ended in exile in England, joined voices urging **Edward III** to war vs. France

**Brittany Succession Crisis (1341):**
- **Duke John III of Brittany** died without direct heirs
- Succession disputed between **Blois** and **Monfort** factions
- Civil war: lesser nobles and Celtic west supported Monforts; great lords and French-speaking bourgeois of east rallied to Blois
- English supported Monfort faction via *chevauchée* (mounted raid to lay waste) and besieging Rennes, Vannes, Nantes

**The Évreux/Navarrese Faction (1350s):**
- Led by **Charles the Bad**, king of Navarre
- Son of **Joan of France** (grandson of **Louis X**)—reasonable claim on French throne
- **Philip of Évreux** inherited substantial landholdings in Normandy and Ile-de-France
- Charles the Bad provided leadership to dissident nobilities of these regions
- Disruptive influence 1354 (murdered French Constable) to 1364 (faction suppressed by **Charles V**)

**Thesis: Internal Conflict Primary:**
- Hundred Years' War primarily internal conflict
- International aspect arose because rival factions appealed to kings of England or France
- **Monfort** and **Arteveldt** factions invited **Edward III** to intervene in Brittany and Flanders
- Supposedly **Jan van Arteveldt** suggested Edward III declare himself king of France to legitimize Flemish support
- Resumption of war **1369** resulted from appeal to **Charles V** by count of **Armagnac** against **Black Prince**
- Darkest period for French (1420-36) came from alliance of Burgundians and English

### Paris Rebellion and Jacquerie (1357-58)

**Paris Rebellion:**
- **1357**: dauphin (future **Charles V**) lost Paris after father's capture at Poitiers
- Paris lost to coalition of urban elites led by wealthy cloth-dealer **Etienne Marcel**
- During revolt, Paris mob murdered two royal marshals in front of dauphin
- Marcel faction allied with **Charles the Bad of Navarre**

**The Jacquerie:**
- Winter **1357-58**: gangs of Anglo-Navarrese "day by day conquering and laying waste entire region between Loire and Seine"
- Pillaging by men-at-arms + fiscal demands of monarchy + seigneurial reaction pushed Ile-de-France peasants beyond breaking point
- **Jacquerie** started May 1358, suppressed by nobles June
- Years 1356-60: nadir—shocked by state collapse, nobility consolidated around dauphin, achieved general consensus

### Fiscal Reform and Temporary Recovery (1360s)

**The Ordinance of December 5, 1360:**
- Landmark in French fiscal history, established framework of *ancien régime*
- Imposed salt tax (*gabelle*) and indirect (sales) taxes (*aides*) on consumption
- Estates General agreed to hearth tax (*fouage*, later *taille*)
- Direct taxes (like *fouage*) bore on rural lordships—unpopular with nobility
- Primary purpose: pay military salaries
- Little opposition because need to ransom king was clear (though ransom never paid—money used to build new army)
- Collective realization: action needed or France would be lost
- Contributing factor: massive "pruning" of French nobility by war
- Worst disaster: **Crécy** wiped out 10,000 of "flower of French nobility"
- **Poitiers**: another 2,500 nobles
- Thousands died in naval battle of **Sluys** (1340), local civil wars, at hands of *jacques* of Ile-de-France
- By 1360: many fewer lower thugs to raise trouble—some killed, others inherited slain relatives' property
- Swing in public opinion toward peace evident in literature on warfare and chivalry
- Earlier emphasis: right to private war, pursuit of honor/glory
- Now: questions of discipline and public order
- Consensus lasted two decades (1360s-1370s); when new generation came to power (not experiencing 1356-60 collapse), things fell apart again

**Charles V's Military Success:**
- **1365**: national consolidation resulted in rapid recovery of territory lost to English
- Unlike huge undisciplined throngs of previous reign, army of **Charles V**: only 2,400 men-at-arms and 1,000 crossbowmen (60% mounted)
- Troops permanently employed, regularly paid
- Permanent forces joined by supplementary retinues of men-at-arms during intense activity (total max 5,200)
- First military success: crushing **Évreux faction** in Normandy (led by Charles the Bad) spring 1364
- **1369**: war with English resumed (appeal by count of **Armagnac**)
- Before death of **Charles V** in 1380, almost all French territory regained
- English held only Bordeaux, Bayonne, Brest, Calais, Cherbourg, Valais and surrounding territory

## 4.5 Depression (1365-1450)

### Population Stagnation

- After disasters of mid-14th century reducing population by 1/3 to 1/2, France entered period of *seeming* stagnation until 1450
- Not "steady state"—characterized by short recovery periods followed by new collapses
- Example from **Normandy**: three crisis periods with population minima in 1380, 1420, 1450; interspersed by partial recovery; sustained growth after 1450
- Population decline closely correlated with intensification of internal war (direct demographic effect + indirect consequences for productive infrastructure)
- Regional variations in timing according to fluxes of internal warfare and recurrent epidemics
- Overall change 1300-1450: French population decreased by 1/3 (after mid-14th century catastrophe)
- Overall change 1350-1450: estimated ~50% decline
- Some regions experienced even more catastrophic declines: 70% fall in Normandy
- Earlier historians sometimes too conservative in estimates—later reanalysis showed greater crashes

### Effect of Persistent Warfare

- Sociopolitical instability of Hundred Years' War had enormous impact on productive capacity
- Some areas fought over repeatedly, suffered most
- By 1450: Normandy population fallen to 30% of peak in early 14th century

**Paris Region:**
- Another area of prolonged fighting; rural population may have decreased fourfold
- Region suffered being close to Flanders and Normandy (sources of English *chevauchées*) and being national capital
- **Petrarch** visit to France **1360**: "I could scarcely recognise anything I saw. Most opulent of kingdoms is heap of ashes; not a single house standing except those protected by ramparts of towns and citadels. Where is now Paris that was once such a great city?"
- Two generations later: Paris was battleground between **Armagnacs** and **Burgundians**
- "Vied with each other to prove how far bloodthirstiness could go: murders and massacres never ceased"
- When Burgundians entered Paris May 1418: littered with Armagnac corpses "piled up like pigs in mud"

**South (Southwest):**
- Similarly devastated
- **Philippe de la Boissière** (15th century): "this land of Saintonge, except for towns and fortresses, was deserted and uninhabited.... Where there had once been fine manors, domains, and heritages, towering bushes grew"
- Some areas escaped devastation temporarily
- When English under **Black Prince** marched through **Massif Central** (1356), found "land of Auvergne which they had never before entered...so prosperous and so full of all manner of goods that it was a marvel to see"
- Prosperity of Auvergne did not survive Black Prince and troops

**Famine Endemic:**
- First half of 15th century: crises in food supplies around Paris and Rouen in 1421, 1432, 1433, particularly 1437-39
- These crises not fundamental cause of population stagnation—aggravating circumstance
- Underlying cause: **war**

**Real Wages During War:**
- 14th-century population decline translated into excellent real wages for working people
- First decade of 15th century: building laborer in Paris could buy more than 20 kg of grain with daily wage
- High wages prevailed for several decades after end of Hundred Years' War
- During war decades (1420s-1430s), laborer's wage collapsed to less than 8 kg of grain per day
- Problem not lack of cultivable land or people—fundamental problem: **lack of security**

**Village Abandonment:**
- Peasants abandoned villages for relative safety of fortified towns
- Example around Alsatian towns like Colmar: whole belts of dead villages
- Abandoned land turned fallow or lightly utilized for grazing cattle
- Incessant warfare destroyed infrastructure
- Example in area of **Langle** (modern Pas-de-Calais): drainage system abandoned, land flooded then deserted
- Village abandonment varied by region: south more strongly affected (25-33% of villages abandoned in Provence)
- Many desertions final, affecting 1/4 of localities in mountainous regions
- North by contrast: only 3-10% of villages abandoned forever
- Estimate: minimum 3-4 million ha of land abandoned between 1350 and 1440

### Elite Dynamics

- Incessant fighting affected aristocracy even more than commoner population
- Enormous numbers of French nobles dispossessed during this period
- Population collapse of second half 14th century and ensuing depression resulted in divergent evolution of economic well-being of nobility and peasants
- Declining population pushed down grain prices, drove up real wages
- Small people beneficiaries of new economic conjuncture
- Not "golden age" of peasants—continuously suffered from incessant warfare, periodic epidemics, recurrent famines (largely caused by warfare)
- Nevertheless, usually had enough food to feed family
- Even during bad times (1420-1440 in Paris), wages substantially above peaceful period of 1520-60 when population densities approached carrying capacity ceiling

**Noble Income Crisis:**
- Economic well-being of nobility moved opposite to peasants
- Nobles profited little from cheaper grain (many grew food on own estates; food not large in consumption budget)
- Wages increased: nobles had to pay more for manufactures and services necessary for signaling elite status
- Alternative was quiet slide into ranks of commoners
- Nobles had to spend more to maintain previous living levels
- Noble incomes declined at same time as expenditures increased
- Even before 1350: good half of nobility enjoyed incomes of only 40 livres or less (less than well-to-do farmer with income ~60 livres)
- Review by **Contamine** (1997:107) of noble incomes 1280-1340: great majority of incomes were "modest or even mediocre, and it was quite problematic for them even to maintain their means of existence"
- Severe recession of 14th century + civil wars + peasant uprisings = catastrophe for noble incomes
- All sources of income declined: rents, revenues from justice, profits from seigneurial equipment (mills, baking ovens), tolls from fairs and markets
- Most seigneurs lost 1/2 to 3/4 of their revenues between 1350 and 1450

**Specific Examples:**
- Just 30 years (1335-64): income of **Jeanne de Navarre** from Champaign and Brie properties fell 58% (from 23,000 to 10,000 livres)
- 1340-1400: income of abbey of **Saint-Denis** fell 50% nominal, 2/3 real (from 72,000 to 24,000 *setiers* of grain)
- Buying power in cereals of seigneurial income of **Jeanne de Chalon** in 1420 was only 15-20% of that enjoyed by grandparents in 1340s
- **Dame de Chalon** at least managed to survive modestly, pass on vestiges of patrimony to heirs
- Other less fortunate noble families disappeared: slaughtered in battle, succumbed to plague, ruined and plunged into lower classes
- **1378**: average income of knight in *châtellenie* of **Mello** (in *bailliage* of Senlis, Paris region) was only 45 l.p. (livre parisis)
- Squires had to do with only 25 l.p. (B. Guenée, cited in Contamine 1997:89)
- Peaceful period **1380-1410** saw some restoration of noble incomes
- With resumption of civil war and English invasions, revenues plunged to new lows

**More Income Examples:**
- Castellanies of **Laignes** and **Griselles** (county of Tonnerre): fell from 500 to 238 l.p. between 1405 and 1425
- **1343** before troubles: *prévôté* of **Tonnerre** reported annual revenue of 520 livres
- Revenue fell to 182 livres in 1405, 114 livres in 1425
- Normandy data: income declined nearly 50% between 1400 and 1450
- More extreme collapse: seigneury of **Sully** (Orléans region)—revenue fell from 700 l.p. (1383-84) to 143 l.p. (1455)

**Selection Regime:**
- Severe depression of landed incomes did not affect all noble families uniformly
- Imposed selection regime: weak and unlucky declined and eventually succumbed; strong and lucky held own or got ahead
- Impoverished nobles attempting to maintain consumption levels necessary for preserving status rapidly ran up debt, eventually sold lands
- Majority of *plèbe nobiliare* (families already on brink by 1350) plunged beyond point of no return
- Many magnate families during this period were buying up lands
- Easier for *grand seigneur* to reduce consumption without crossing line between nobility and commonality
- Magnates better positioned to profit from royal patronage (even though volume of flow of favors greatly diminished)
- Land was cheap
- Certain bourgeois profited from economic situation (particularly those providing administrators and legists for state—essentially forerunners of *robe nobility* that merged into ruling class during subsequent centuries)
- Merchants apparently did not do particularly well (unless first became officeholders)—at least true for Paris region

**Elite Dynamics Consequences:**
- Economic difficulties, elevated mortality from conflicts, low replacement rates had measurable effect on rate of noble family and lineage extinction
- Although Forez (south-central France) escaped worst excesses of Hundred Years' War, rate of extinction of noble lineages increased almost twofold
- 13th century: 31% extinction rate
- 14th century: 54%
- 15th century: 55%
- **Bar-sur-Seine**: family extinction rate during 13th century was 58% (35/60)
- This increased to 75% (15/20) during following century
- Note: Forez and Bar-sur-Seine data cannot be directly compared (former relate to lineages potentially comprising several families; latter refer to individual families)
- **Vésubie valley**: 1/3 of all families faded away during first quarter of 15th century
- Implies extinction rate of 80% per century—very high selection pressure

**Who Profited:**
- Severe depression imposed selection regime: weak/unlucky declined; strong/lucky held own or got ahead
- Majority of *plèbe nobiliare* plunged beyond point of no return
- Magnates buying up lands
- Certain bourgeois profited (administrators and legists for state)
- These officeholders were forerunners of *robe nobility* that merged into ruling class during subsequent centuries
- Merchants apparently did not do particularly well (unless first became officeholders)

**Replacement Rates:**
- Few regional studies yield quantitative data on elite dynamics in medieval France
- **Lorcin's** (1981) study of wills registered at officialty of Lyon yielded information on number of living children at time of death of testator
- Numbers incidentally give information about relative dynamics of replacement rate

**Table 4.5 Data (Number of living children per testator having children):**
- 1300-1340: Nobles 4.3, Non-nobles 3.2, Differential +1.1
- 1340-1380: Nobles 3.4, Non-nobles 2.6, Differential +1.2
- 1380-1420: Nobles 3.8, Non-nobles 2.8, Differential +1.0
- 1420-1460: Nobles 4.2, Non-nobles 3.5, Differential +0.7
- 1460-1500: Nobles 3.7, Non-nobles 5.3, Differential -1.6

**Analysis:**
- Family sizes of both noble and non-noble families declined during 14th century, then increased during 15th
- Difference instructive: 14th century noble households tended to have substantially more children than non-noble families
- This differential declined over time, even inverted sign by end of 15th century
- "Noble advantage" of greater family size substantially reduced, if not nullified, by tendency of high proportion of noble daughters to become nuns
- Two-thirds of noblewomen in Lyon entered nunnery during first half of 14th century, only 14% during last half of 15th century
- Compare to just 2.4% of non-nobles choosing religious career during whole period
- Clear noble families shipped to religious houses their "surplus" female progeny
- Proportion of daughters becoming nuns increased from 15% for small families (3 children or less) to 45% for largest families (7+ children)
- Very substantial proportion of males also chose religious career

**Table 4.6 Data (Proportion of children in noble families becoming priests or monks/nuns):**
- 1300-1350: Sons 45%, Daughters 64%
- 1350-1400: Sons 22%, Daughters 40%
- 1400-1450: Sons 22%, Daughters 29%
- 1450-1500: Sons 16%, Daughters 14%

**Sex Ratio Disparity:**
- Final interesting datum from Lorcin study: disparity between sex ratios of noble and non-noble families
- Commoner families: ratio of males to females was 113:100 (expected in preindustrial society with substantial proportion of women dying in childbirth)
- Noble families: sex ratio was astonishing 85 males per 100 females
- In other words, there were 1/4 fewer noble males than expected
- Partly due to high proportion of women entering nunneries (spared dangers of childbirth)
- Most of difference probably due to "hecatombs" of English and civil wars
- Taken together—falling family sizes, high proportion of noblemen and noblewomen choosing religious career, huge male deficit—these data suggest nobility of Lyon region was under substantial degree of pressure
- To point where this period can be appropriately characterized by crisis of nobility
- Pressure gradually relaxed during 15th century
- However, even by 1500 the replacement rates of noble households continued to lag that of commoners

**Supporting Data from Other Regions:**
- Less detailed data from other regions appear to support picture painted by Lorcin study
- Example: Breton family of **Tournemine de la Hunaudaye** produced average 4 children per marriage in 13th century, 2.75 in 14th, 3.25 in 15th
- Urban elites affected similarly
- Data from **Périgueux** indicate high family sizes in second half of 13th century, decline to minimum in 15th century, then increase to 1500
<!-- pp. 161-180 -->

## The Dynamics of Sociopolitical Instability (1365–1450)

### State Collapse and Elite Factionalism

- **France experienced full state collapse during 1356–60**, followed by temporary consolidation that unraveled after **Charles V's** death
- **Charles VI (1380–1422)** reign saw the second state collapse of the Hundred Years' War
- Government dominated by **Charles VI's uncles** — the dukes of **Anjou, Berri, and Burgundy** (the "Princes of the Lilies")
- State policy disrupted by **factionalism**: some taxes permanently annulled (*fouage*), others first annulled then reimposed (*aides*, *gabelles*)
- **Princes of the Lilies** diverted large sums of royal revenues into their appanages — pocketed virtually all taxes collected by the Crown

### The Rise of Impoverished Nobility

- Social situation by 1340s-1350s mirrored earlier crisis phases: **great crowds of impoverished nobles seeking military employment**
- Fall 1386: **10,000 to 20,000 nobles flocked to L'Écluse** for planned invasion of England (never occurred)
- Elite factionalism evolved into **extremely bitter and sanguinary civil war**

### Burgundians vs. Armagnacs

- Main factions coalesced around **Philip the Bold, Duke of Burgundy** (Charles VI's uncle) and opposition faction
- Opposition faction led by **"Marmousets"** — civil officials and military leaders close to previous king **Charles V**
- **Duke of Burgundy's** political program: use France's resources to build independent principality in Burgundy and Low Countries
- **Marmousets' objectives**: relieve burden on taxpayers while building up resources in royal coffers
- Marmousets also supported **Louis of Anjou** and his son **Louis II** (who had dynastic ambitions on Naples throne)
- Another important leader: **Louis of Orléans** (king's brother) who gradually took over Marmousets faction
- Central government weakened by **Charles VI's intermittent insanity**
- Political scene eventually dominated by two factions: **Burgundians** (dukes of Burgundy) and first **Orleanists** (Louis of Orléans), then **Armagnacs** (Count of Armagnac, father-in-law of Charles, new Duke of Orléans after father assassinated in 1407)

### Civil War Escalation (1407–1422)

- **1407**: Assassination of **Louis of Orléans** under orders of **John the Fearless of Burgundy** (son of Philip the Bold)
- France divided into two armed camps
- **Burgundians** drew strength from John's territories in northeast and north, and from **Parisian bourgeoisie and academics**
- **Armagnacs** were party of greater royal officials and high nobility, with large following in south and southwest
- During 1407–14 the two factions battled for the capital, both appealing to English for aid
- By 1414 Armagnacs gained control of most of France including capital, but **John of Burgundy allied with new English king, Henry V**

### English Invasion and French Catastrophe

- **1415**: **Henry V invaded France and won great victory at Agincourt**
- **10,000 French knights** died at Agincourt — terrible blow that reversed balance of forces in favor of Burgundians
- As Count of Armagnac was besieged in Paris by Burgundian forces, **English conquest of Normandy proceeded completely unopposed**
- **1418**: Burgundian supporters rose up in Paris and **killed thousands of Armagnacs** — the occasion of quotation about Armagnacs "lying in the streets like slaughtered pigs"
- The dauphin (future **Charles VII**) and Armagnacs abandoned Paris to Burgundians

> "Veniat Pax" (let there be peace) — cry of early fourteenth-century sermon by **Jean Gerson**

- **1419**: **Rouen surrendered to Henry V** and English conquest of Normandy complete
- Armagnacs revenged themselves by **assassinating John the Fearless** (John of Burgundy) at conference with dauphin at bridge of Montereau
- New duke, **Philip the Good**, vowed vengeance and returned to English alliance
- Working with Burgundians, English overran northern France and installed themselves in Paris
- **Treaty of Troyes**: **Henry V married daughter of Charles VI** and was named heir to French throne, while dauphin (future Charles VII) disinherited
- **1422**: Both **Charles VI and Henry V died**
- Infant **Henry VI of England** recognized as king of France in north, supported by Burgundians, and **crowned in Paris (1436)**

### The Écorcheurs and Population Collapse

- **Dauphinists** (formerly Armagnacs) continued to deteriorate during 1420s
- **1424**: Lost battle of **Verneuil**
- **1428**: English began siege of **Orléans**
- Meanwhile, **Lancastrian France** became wilderness laid waste by garrisons, Dauphinist raiders, deserters, and ***écorcheurs***
- ***Écorcheurs*** or **flayers** were heirs of *routiers* of previous century — took name from custom of stripping victims to skin or even **flaying them alive**
- During this period **populations of Ilede-France, Normandy, and doubtlessly many other regions declined to 25–30 percent of their 1300 peak**

## The Disintegrative Trend Reverses Itself

### The Turning Point (1428–1450)

- **Years 1428–29** were nadir of second French state collapse
- Turning point: **Jeanne d'Arc lifted siege of Orléans**, followed by **coronation of Charles VII in Reims (1429)**
- **1435**: Conclusion of treaty with **Duke of Burgundy** brought civil war to end, French reconquest slowly gathered steam
- Paris recovered **1436**, Gascony (except Bordeaux and Bayonne) reconquered **1442**, Normandy **1450**, Bordeaux fell **1453**

### Restoration of State Finance

- Decade after 1435 saw **permanent establishment of state finance in France**
- **Indirect taxes** (*aides*) restored in **1435** (Languedoil) and **1437** (Languedoc)
- **1439**: Estates of Languedoil granted **direct taxes** (*taille*) for one year to support regularly paid troops
- Restoration of fiscal and political order briefly interrupted by **revolt of princes in 1440** (the Praguerie), but Crown continued collecting *taille* without consulting Estates
- **1445**: **Charles VII established regular cavalry** (*compagnies d'ordonnance*)
- **By 1460 the restored fiscal system was producing 1.8 million l.t. a year**, most from direct taxes, with only 50,000 l.t. from royal domain
- Basic fiscal system first introduced in 1360s **finally implemented in 1440s**
- **Solid fiscal foundation critical factor in ending Hundred Years' War**, but itself was consequence of new-found feeling of **national unity among the elites**

### Social Psychology and National Unity

- Two factors at work in new unity:
- **First**: shift in social psychology — everybody tired of incessant internal and external warfare
- Around 1400 the **yearning for peace was increasingly voiced**
- Although conflict started as civil war, it **gradually transmuted into national war of liberation against the English**

### Numerical Decimation of Nobility

- **Second factor**: nobility was **numerically decimated**, which removed social pressure for elite factionalization that fueled civil conflict
- **Hecatombs inflicted on French nobility during second stage of Hundred Years' War were comparable in magnitude to, if not greater than, those of the first**
- Worst was **Agincourt (1415)** where **10,000 French nobles died** — among fallen were more than ten dukes and counts, 120 barons, 1,500 knights
- Earlier, many thousands of French nobles participated in crusade to free Hungary from Turks, perished in **battle of Nicopolis (1396)**
- **Dauphinist casualties at battle of Verneuil (1424)** about **7,000**, though many were Scots
- **Casualties in large battles probably only minor part of total drain on French nobility** — untold thousands lost lives in civil wars and small-scale military operations against English
- Already referred to **massacre of Armagnacs in Paris (1418)**
- **King Henry V of England** notorious for atrocities: killing prisoners at Agincourt, hanging entire garrison when he took **Armagnac castle of Rougemont (1421)**, drowning defenders who escaped
- Dispossessed nobles in Normandy fighting guerilla warfare called "**brigands**" and hanged when caught
- Other atrocities: butchering of 2,000 men, women, and children in **Caen (1417)**, Henry's refusal to allow 12,000 poor folk driven out of besieged **Rouen** to leave — forced them to stay in ditch where **most died of inclement climate (winter) and starvation**

> The casual attitude to taking life was a population decline in general, but **more specifically the decimation of nobility**

- Because proportion of nobility among general population declined between 1300 and 1450, **numbers of nobility decreased even more than general population**

### Social Mobility During Crisis Phase

- **Crisis phases of secular cycles typically characterized by increased social mobility**, both upward and downward
- Main factor driving turnover: **growing economic inequality** during stagflation and crisis phases
- As conditions of overpopulation develop and large numbers of peasants impoverished, **some favored few nevertheless become richer**
- These families desire to **translate improved economic conditions into social status**
- Under conditions of elite overproduction, majority of aristocrats lose ground and face prospect of **downward mobility**, but few accumulate property and move into magnate stratum
- Pent-up demand for upward social mobility from elite and magnate aspirants during crisis phase provides **cheap alternative for cash-strapped rulers to recompense their supporters**
- **In medieval France, can quantify this process by observing how numbers of letters of ennoblement fluctuated between different reigns**
- Movement of commoners into ranks of nobility **greatly accelerated in early fourteenth century and peaked during its second half** (figure 4.6)
- During rest of fifteenth century, **rate of ennoblement declined to much lower levels**, suggesting greatly decreased upward social mobility
- Because **downward mobility continued unabated during this period**, net result was **substantial decline in elite numbers**

### Elite Replacement: Partial, Not Total

- Data suggests **rate of extinction of noble families went up in fourteenth and fifteenth centuries** compared to preceding period
- When **upward mobility was also choked off** (figure 4.6), it stopped compensating for losses of old families, and **old nobility shrank in number**
- However, **conclusion that old elites were replaced wholesale by new, recently ennobled elites would be too hasty**
- **Many newly ennobled lineages did not manage to establish themselves and went extinct**
- Of old lineages, some lost ground and disappeared, but **others persisted and even managed to increase their lands**

### Case Studies of Noble Survival

- **Sologne (county of Blois)**: contained nine fiefs possessing right of high justice
- **Five of these fiefs remained within hands of old nobility without interruption**
- Of remaining four: one seized by **Duke of Orléans** for debts, one sold to another old noble family, last two acquired by new nobility (**d'Etamps family**, originating in late fourteenth century)
- Total number of old noble families **shrank appreciably**, but there was only one parvenue family to plug resulting gap
- Study by **Maurice Berthe (1976)**: county of **Bigorre** had forty fiefs in 1313 but only eighteen in 1429
- Twelve fiefs disappeared together with villages on which their existence depended, while ten acquired by six of surviving eighteen seigneurs
- Study by **Edouard Baratier (1971)**: In 1377 five villages in **Provence** inhabited by thirty-eight nobles, of whom seventeen not living as nobles (probably couldn't afford it)
- **By 1427–31 number of nobles had declined to twenty-two**
- **1458**: only six nobles left, but after that number increased, and there were twelve nobles in 1474
- **As number of elite families was pruned, those that remained were strengthened**

## 4.6 Conclusion: "A Near Perfect Multi-secular Cycle"

> Quotation from **Braudel (1988:131)**

- Starting date for cycle differs from that of Braudel (who believed 950–1150 was separate secular cycle in Western Europe)
- **If we focus on France between 1150 and 1450, then indeed, the data paint a nearly perfect example of a secular cycle**

### Variables Behaved as Predicted

- **All major variables behaved during this period as postulated by demographic-structural theory**
- Population, prices, and other economic variables went through **high-amplitude oscillation**
- Difference between population minima and maxima was **twofold** (in some regions like Normandy and Ile-de-France, **three- and even fourfold**)
- **Social structure also oscillated** in manner predicted by theory
- **Contamine (1997)** estimates that nobility accounted for 1.8% of total population around 1300 (decreasing to 1.5% in fifteenth century) are **overly conservative**
- Average of eleven regions for which he found data on **noble-commoner proportions is 2.4%**
- After famines and epidemics of first half of fourteenth century, **ratio of nobles to commoners should have increased even further**
- In other words, **decline in elite-commoner ratio between 1350 and 1450 was probably more extreme** than suggested by Contamine's estimates
- **Extreme nature of elite overproduction in fourteenth-century France graphically illustrated by tens of thousands of "surplus nobility" seeking military employment** but finding death or capture on fields of Crécy, Poitiers, and others

### State Strength Followed Grand Cycle

- **Strength of the state followed a grand cycle**: from geopolitical successes during twelfth and thirteenth centuries to persistent fiscal troubles and territorial losses of Hundred Years' War, **reversed only after 1450**
- **Fall and then rise of political instability graphically illustrated by time distribution of coin hoards**

### Reasons for Excellent Match

- **Two main reasons for excellent match between theoretical predictions and empirical patterns:**

**First**: **slow advance of technology**, especially agricultural technology, during period
- Appears that **carrying capacity of France hardly changed between 1150 and 1450**, in contrast to following early modern period

**Second**: During most of period **France was the hegemonic power in Western Europe**
- Its dynamics therefore **primarily determined by endogenous rather than exogenous forces**
- Only major exception: **arrival of Black Death from steppes of Eurasia in 1348**
- But plague epidemic arrived after population had already started to decline, and thus **accelerated and deepened the ongoing decline rather than shifting the endogenously driven trajectory to an entirely different attractor**

<!-- pp. 181-200 -->

### State Finances Collapse (1562)

**Royal finances completely collapsed** with onset of civil war in 1562. Desperate measures (pawning royal jewels, selling church property worth 13+ million livres) insufficient to meet military costs. State debt spiraled: 296 million livres by 1595. **Debt nearly eliminated under Henri IV** but ballooned again during the Fronde, reaching 700 million livres in 1661.

## 5.4 Crisis (1570–1600)

### Population Stagnation

By end of stagflation phase, population recovered to pre-14th-century-collapse levels. Late 16th and all 17th century: **population growth ceased**. Overall French population of 1720 essentially same as 1560. Regional variations existed, but general trajectory: stagnation with temporal fluctuations.

**Le Roy Ladurie** distinguishes three classes of temporal fluctuations:
- **Secular cycles** (centuries-long)
- **Bigenerational cycles** (decades-long)
- **Third-order** fluctuations (annual)

1560–1720 featured three bigenerational fluctuations:
1. Population decline during Wars of Religion → recovery under Henri IV and early Louis XIII
2. Decline during Thirty Years' War and the Fronde → recovery under Fouquet and Colbert
3. Troubles of second half of Louis XIV's reign → sustained population takeoff under successors that finally broke medieval ceiling of 20 million

### The Three Scourges: Plague, Famine, War

**Proximate mechanisms** of population decline: plague, famine, war.

**Plague incidence** increased from minimum of 1,400 communities throughout 15th-16th centuries. Reached pandemic levels:
- Height of Wars of Religion (1580s)
- Peak of Thirty Years' War (1620s-1630s)

**1583 plague**: killed one-third of Angers inhabitants. In Anjou, many parishes lost one-quarter to one-third of dwellers. 17th century Anjou hit repeatedly: 1626–27, 1631–32, 1639. Plague spread via troop movements, affecting Massif Central, Aquitain basin, Armorican Massif, Paris basin in alternating waves.

**Famine widespread** during this period:
- Food shortages from anti-Huguenot Catholic League activities (1590s)
- Subsistence crises: 1630, 1649, 1652, 1661, 1694
- Great grainless winter of 1709

Immediate triggers: very cold/damp winters, unusually wet summers. Famines of 1630 and 1661 occurred during peacetime, entirely due to bad weather. **These caused short-term declines** — Le Roy Ladurie calls them **"third-order fluctuations"** that temporarily disturbed population levels without lasting alteration.

> "These fluctuations temporarily disturbed, without really altering them in a lasting way, the levels and ratios of population and food supplies" — Le Roy Ladurie

During famines of 1590s and 1649–52 (longer-lasting): weather played secondary role. **Civil war** rather than rain killed the grain.

### Third Population Decline (c. 1700)

Third decline (around 1700) occurred when state was strong but engaged in intense external wars. Authors argue: **not demographic-structural mechanisms** but climate worsening and aggressive external policy of Louis XIV.

Issue of third decline falls outside temporal framework of this chapter — focus remains on population dynamics up to 1660.

### Fundamental Mechanism: Sociopolitical Instability

Although proximate factors were epidemics and famine, **troop movements spread epidemics** and **military operations threatened harvests**. **Fundamental mechanism**: sociopolitical instability leading to civil war within kingdom, weakening the state and making it susceptible to foreign invasions.

**Period of heightened political instability** in France: ~1 century, 1562–1675. Two waves:
1. Wars of Religion, culminating ~1590
2. Magnate rebellions, Huguenot insurrections, peasant uprisings → culminating in Fronde of 1648–53 (double peak in figure 5.3)

Two peaks of internal warfare separated by relatively stable two decades under Henri IV and early Louis XIII.

**Wars of Religion**: population loss 20% or more. Orléans reduced by one-third 1561–1597. Rouen reduced by more than a quarter 1562–1594. Second period of sociopolitical instability: demographic losses even greater. **Combined effect of Thirty Years' War and the Fronde**: certain provinces in north and east lost close to half their inhabitants. South of France much less affected (discussed in section 5.6).

## 5.5 A Case Study: The Norman Nobility

Historians **Guy Bois, James Wood, Jonathan Dewald** provide excellent quantitative data on nobility of Normandy. **Normandy particularly interesting**: reasonably representative of northern France. Situated next to core French state but **not heavily distorted by capital** (royal court, central administrative apparatus) unlike Ile-de-France. Norman situation yields testable hypotheses about other French regions and France as a whole.

**Guy Bois** insights from Norman material proved generally valid for northern France, also for whole kingdom when regional variations (north/south social structures) are considered.

### The Dynamics of Elite Numbers

**Peak general population**: achieved by 1560 (essentially approached by 1540). After 1540: baptism numbers in communities stagnated. **Secular decline of northern France population**: 1560–1660 (with shorter-term fluctuations around trend), followed by population growth (interrupted by another decline around 1700). By 1720 population numbers probably regained 1560 level.

**TABLE 5.1** Revolutionary situations in France, 1500–1900:
- 1548: Pitaud insurrection (Guyenne)
- 1562–63: First war of religion
- 1567–68: Second war of religion
- 1568–69: Third war of religion
- 1572–73: Fourth war of religion
- 1574–76: Fifth war of religion
- 1577: Sixth war of religion
- 1578–79: Seventh war of religion
- 1579–80: Eighth war of religion
- 1585–98: Ninth war of religion
- 1594–95: Croquant rebellions (southwest)
- 1614–15: Civil war in Brittany
- 1617, 1619–20: War of Mother and Son
- 1621–22, 1625, 1627–30: Hueguenot wars (English intervention)
- 1629–30, 1635–36, 1637–41: Croquant uprisings
- 1639: Norman rebellion
- 1643–44: Revolt of southwest
- 1648–53: **The Fronde**
- 1655–57: Tardanizat rebellion (Guyenne)
- 1658: Sabotiers rebellion (Sologne)
- 1661–62: Bénauge rebellion (Guyenne)
- 1662–63: Lustucru, Audijos rebellions
- 1663–72: Angelets guerilla warfare (Roussillon)
- 1675: Papier Timbre, Bonnets Rouges (Brittany)
- 1702–06: Camisards rebellions
- 1768–69: Corsican rebellion
- 1789–99: French revolutions and counterrevolutions
- 1815: Hundred Days
- 1830: July Revolution
- 1848: French revolution
- 1851: Louis Napoleon's coup d'état
- 1870: State collapse, occupation, republican revolutions
- 1870–71: Multiple communes

### Noble vs. Commoner Population Dynamics

**Numerical dynamics of nobility**: distinctly different pattern from general (commoner) population.

**Wood (1980)** study of **élection of Bayeux** (Lower Normandy): reconstructed noble household changes 1463–1666.

**Before 1540**: total noble households grew slowly (<0.5% per year). Same period: general Normandy population increased at 1.1% per year (30 to 75 on relative scale). Result: **proportion of nobles to total population declined**.

1713 **élection** of Bayeux: 22,620 hearths. Peak of 1560 very close to this value. By 1540 population perhaps 10% less. Thus by 1540: at least 20,000 households in region, of which 309 were noble, **implying nobility proportion at 1.5%**.

**1540–1600**: general population stagnated (declined 1570–1600), but **numbers of nobility exploded**. Growth rate 1540–98: more than 1% per year. First two-thirds of 17th century: nobility numbers stagnated (even declining 1598–1624). Since general population reached minimum around 1660, **proportion of noble households exceeded 3%** at that time — double its 1540 value.

Summary of noble-to-commoner ratio changes:
- **1460–1540**: nobles increased more slowly than commoners → noble-to-commoner ratio decreased to 1.5%
- **1540–1600**: commoner population stagnated while nobles exploded
- **1600–1660**: both stagnated, noble-to-commoner ratio around 3%

**Bayeux nobility primarily rural**: one-third to one-half sword nobles, only 3-6% officials or professionals. **Robe nobility dynamics**: Dewald (1980) study of magistrates in Parlement of Rouen. Numbers of high officials experienced tremendous growth during 16th century.

**TABLE 5.4** Growth of high officials in Rouen:
- 15th C: 8 total
- 1499: 43 total
- 1554: 81 total
- 1600: 174 total
- Later 17th C: 200 total

Note: Late 15th century before parlement establishment (1499): only one sovereign court (*Cour des Aides*), 8 members. Two hundred years later: late 17th-century **intendant Voysin de La Noiraye** listed 200 members in Rouen's four sovereign courts. Lion's share of growth occurred prior to 1600 — parlement membership increased from 35 to 83 during 16th century.

### Causes Underlying Growth of Elite Numbers

**Numbers of nobility greatly expanded 1450–1660**, bulk of increase during second half of 16th century. Robe nobility particularly spectacular increase, but **sword nobility also increased substantially**. In rural areas (Bayeux region), proportion of sword to robe nobles stayed roughly constant.

**How accomplished?** Change in noble numbers determined by two processes:
1. Natural increase in established noble families
2. Appearance of new nobles (**anoblis**) from upward mobility

**Table 5.3** ("Growth Rate" column): rate of change in established noble families (Bayeux). Most periods: number of established families shrank (failure in male line, emigration, loss of noble status). **Only 1540–98**: natural increase from family splitting (patrimony split between multiple sons) overwhelmed forces reducing old nobility numbers.

Ancient nobility numbers: declined 1463–1540, increased to 1598, thereafter another decline.

**16th century = period of greatest upward mobility**. Majority of "new" nobles in Bayeux were actually **old nobles immigrating from elsewhere**. To study upward mobility: focus on individuals elevated from commoner to noble status — the **anoblis**.

**Main requirement** for obtaining (and preserving) noble status: wealth (land, office, or both). Families entering nobility already part of elite (or elite aspirants).

**Table 5.3**: dynamics of upward mobility largely paralleled established nobility. Rate of appearance of *anoblis* grew throughout 16th century, **reaching peak 1540–98**, then collapsed in 17th century. **Period 1540–98 particularly favorable** to survival and multiplication not only of old noble families but also of upwardly mobile commoners.

### Ennoblements and Civil War

More precise temporal resolution: examining ennoblements registered with **Chambre des Comptes** in Paris (figure 5.5). **Greatest influx of new nobles**: did not occur uniformly during 1540–98 but **toward end of century, during Wars of Religion**. Correlation between civil war and ennoblement rate not spurious — **all three peaks of ennoblement** (1350–1410, 1570–1600, 1640–60) occurred during periods of high sociopolitical instability.

### Elite Incomes and Wealth

**Why did elites do so well during 16th century?** Main requirement for upward mobility: sufficient wealth. Nobility essentially open to any wealthy family willing to play by rules and be patient. Strategies: purchasing land (preferably fief), acquiring offices, marrying well, sending sons to university or military service.

**Economic situation for elites good** during middle 16th century. Main engine of elite prosperity: **growth of income from agriculture**. Landed revenues of Norman seigneuries show similar trajectories:

**County of Tancarville**: collapsed during first half 15th century → mild recovery to 1510 → rapid growth to 1560s. **Roncherolle family**: collapsed during 15th century. 1480–1520: nominal revenue increased. Real value (measured in grain) stagnated but grew in "chicken-equivalents". **1520–1570**: revenues grew rapidly in both grain and poultry terms. 17th century: revenues stagnated.

**Estate of Saussey** (belonging to Maignart family of wealthy Rouen magistrates): similar pattern. Little movement 1480–1520. **Great jump in real revenues 1520–1560** (after 1575 declined due to economic troubles of tenants). Doubling of income in grain-equivalents was unusually good showing.

### Elite Income Growth: The Data

**Magistrates of Rouen** (parlementaires): income grew tenfold 1500–5,000 livres during 16th century. In real terms: more than doubled by 1540s, fell slightly as 1580s approached. Combined income of magistrates as group continued expanding throughout whole period — **interesting pattern**: total income increased substantially 1540s–1570s–1580s, but **number of magistrates doubled**, resulting in **decrease in per capita income**.

Can check by examining **salaries of officeholders in Montpellier**. Data not strictly comparable (Rouen: total income estimates; Montpellier: salaries only). Rouen data for particularly privileged stratum (provincial robe nobility). Nevertheless, **shared patterns**: great expansion of official numbers as well as their cost to society (in real terms). Particularly rapid expansion numbers of officials during third quarter of 16th century — numbers doubled at both Rouen and Montpellier, accompanied by **drop in average income or salary**.

**TABLE 5.7** Salaries of royal officers in Montpellier, 1500–1600:
- 1500: 112 officers, combined 14,885 l.t., adjusted 59,540 l.t., average 532 l.t.
- 1550: 125 officers, combined 33,350 l.t., adjusted 66,700 l.t., average 534 l.t.
- 1575: 253 officers, combined 67,520 l.t., adjusted 67,520 l.t., average 267 l.t.
- 1600: 442 officers, combined 256,791 l.t., adjusted 184,890 l.t., average 418 l.t.

### Marriage Contracts Reveal Income Stratification

Would be interesting to examine income dynamics of other nobility segments, but direct data lacking. **Dewald** obtained glimpse by examining **marriage contracts** (approximate idea of relative wealth of different groups). Between first half 16th century and around 1600: **median dowry in parlementaire marriages increased sixfold**. Recall annual income increased by same order of magnitude (1,000–5,000 livres).

**Dowries of lesser gentry (écuyiers)**: increased only twofold. **Lawyer (avocats) dowries**: growth intermediate between lesser gentry and parlementaires.

**TABLE 5.8** Dowries in Normandy, 1500–1614:

| Social stratum | 1500–1550 | 1568–1614 |
|----------------|-----------|-----------|
| Lesser gentry | 1,320 | 2,625 |
| Lawyers | 1,375 | 5,368 |
| Parlementarians | 4,500 | 27,000 |

During same period wheat prices increased about fourfold. **Lesser gentry losing ground**, parlementaires getting ahead, lawyers breaking even or perhaps slightly slipping.

### Compression of the Elites

**Basic problem facing nobility**: irreconcilable contradiction between two processes:
1. Total production stagnated after 1540 (even declined toward end of century)
2. Numbers of elites grew inexorably

Result: **increasing exploitation of peasants**, but biological limits on how low standard of living of productive class could be driven. Second consequence: **lower real income per elite capita**. Does not mean all elite families suffered equally — basic dynamic (at least for a while): **rich get richer while poor get poorer** (typical of stagflation and crisis phases).

Real income of lesser gentry declining by 1600, while **privileged segment of robe nobility** (parlementaires) **growing more wealthy**.

**Within parlementaire stratum**, same dynamic operating. 1499–1600: numbers of parlementaires grew from 35 to 83. Proximate reason: **Crown's fiscal difficulties** — partially solved by periodic sale of newly established venal offices. But offices also in great demand. As number of venal offices expanded, **price rose at spectacular rate**:

**Councillorship in Parlement of Rouen**: sold for <5,000 livres (1570s), 10,000 l.t. (1580s), 20,000 l.t. (1600s). Doubled again by 1615, yet again by 1634. By 1670s: reached highest point (88,000 l.t.). About half rise due to inflation, most (almost tenfold increase in real terms) driven by **intense intraelite competition**. Economically, increase in office cost did not make sense — offices valued for **extraeconomic value (status)**. Confirmed by growing price of seigneuries (fiefs) — another property sought primarily for extraeconomic value.

**Inflation of fief values** indicated by steadily decreasing returns on seigneurial property: 10% in early 16th century (1507) → 4–4.5% in 1563–97 → 3.3% in 1601 → 2.2% in 1627.

**TABLE 5.9** Average dowries of major governors, 1493–1663:
- 1493–1560: 90,000 l.t.
- 1560–1605: 380,000 l.t.
- 1605–1663: 460,000 l.t.

### Rising Costs and Closure of Magistracy

**Rising costs of office** + **rising costs of education** had dramatic effects on economics of officeholding. Until ~1570: offices sold for prices corresponding to economic returns. After **great office inflation of 1570–1630**: offices bought for prestige or political importance, not economic return. By 1610: chances of creating new fortune from office profits had become very small.

**Officeholding = route for upward mobility during 16th century**, but **magistrate stratum increasingly closed after 1600**. Rouen situation mirrored more general developments in France. Prior to 1600: merchant families often moved into royal courts by buying office or parlement for sons. After 1600: **mechanism for social mobility to highest levels of robe slowed down** — two or three generations of royal office, each more exalted, generally required.

**17th-century slower upward mobility** reflected in social origin of Parlement members. Mid-16th century: only 20% sons of high officials; century later **60% followed in fathers' footsteps**.

**17th century = period of retrenchment** for robe nobility of Rouen. Numbers essentially ceased to grow, upward mobility into ranks practically choked off. **Similar pattern for rural nobility of Bayeux**: rate of appearance of *anoblis* families peaked at 1.3 families per year during 1540–98 → dropped off to 0.7 families per year during first two-thirds of 17th century. Simultaneously, **resistance to accepting nobility of upwardly mobile families intensified**. 16th century: only 1.4–1.6% of families pretending to noble status were condemned (denied); **rose to 3.2% during first half 17th century and to 6% during second half**. Note cyclic return of proportion condemned to previous peak during 15th century (6.2% in 1462) — another period of nobility compression.

**TABLE 5.10** Social origins of parlementaires:

| Time period | High officials | Lawyers/lesser officials | Noblemen | Bourgeois |
|-------------|----------------|--------------------------|----------|-----------|
| 1539–58 | 23 | 18 | 8 | 9 |
| 1559–78 | 22 | 10 | 15 | 7 |
| 1579–88 | 28 | 11 | 6 | 4 |
| 1589–98 | 26 | 2 | 4 | 6 |
| 1599–1618 | 47 | 11 | 13 | 7 |
| 1619–38 | 60 | 5 | 11 | 4 |

### Shrinking Elite Aspirant Pool

As upward mobility into nobility ranks choked off, **size of elite aspirants group apparently shrank**. One route to noble status: acquisition of noble fief (seigneurie). Since proportion of fiefs held by commoners is known, can use it to obtain idea of size of upwardly mobile stratum.

**Proportion of income from fiefs held by commoners**: increased from 8% in 1552 → 13% in 1587 → declined to 2% in 1640. During second half 16th century: **pool of elite aspirants grew**; during first half 17th century: **shrank to almost zero**.

During same period: **proportion of fiefs (by value) held by old nobility** increased from 52% to 72%, while **proportion held by new nobility** declined from 17% to 12%. What apparently happened: **elite aspirants converted holdings of fiefs into noble status during 16th and early 17th century**. As upward mobility dropped off after 1600, descendants of former elite aspirants became first new nobles then old nobles.

**While old nobility proportion among landed elites increased, total numbers of rural nobles in Normandy probably declined toward 1700**. No data for Bayeux, but in another rural *élection* (Gisors): 74 noble households out of 7,500 total in 1703 (~1% of population). In *élection* of Rouen (excluding city): only 154 rural noble families out of population 17,514 (<1%) in 1703. However, regional capital (Rouen) was home of additional 272 noble households + ~160 families of high royal officials.

**TABLE 5.11** Distribution (%) of annual revenues of noble families in Bayeux region, 1639 and 1666:

| Income bracket (l.t.) | 1639 | 1666 |
|----------------------|------|------|
| Less than 1,000 | 67.4 | 49.6 |
| 1,000–10,000 | 29.1 | 48.0 |
| More than 10,000 | 3.5 | 2.4 |

### Rural Nobility Income Distribution

**Second half 16th century**: erosion of rural nobility incomes. Decline stopped, even reversed sometime mid-17th century. Fortunate to have data on income distribution among Bayeux nobility at two points: 1639 and 1666.

**Mean family income**: rose from 1,400 to 1,900 l.t. between dates. Since real value of livre approximately same at these dates, **34% increase in nominal terms represents real increase in noble incomes**. Even more striking change in **median income**: from <400 l.t. to 1,000 l.t. More than two-thirds of nobles disposed of income <1,000 l.t. in 1639, by 1666 proportion declined to less than half. Category including richest nobles also declined slightly — **"middle class" increased**, reflecting declining degree of wealth inequality.

**Purging of nobility of poorest members** continued during first half 18th century. In *élections* of Gisors and rural Rouen (excluding city): proportions of noble families with incomes <1,000 l.t. declined to 46% and 41% in 1703 and to 11% and 16% in 1757. What apparently happening: **poor nobles dropping out of nobility ranks**, while number of better-off nobles (>1,000 l.t. annual income) stabilized. In rural Rouen region: 91 such nobles in 1703 and 88 in 1757; in Gisors: 40 such nobles in both 1702 and 1757.

**Proportion of impoverished nobles** began increasing again during second half of century. On Revolution's eve (1788): proportions of poor nobles increased to 26% and 17% in the two *élections*.

### Conclusions

**General pattern**: numbers of nobility (noble-to-commoner ratio) and per capita wealth existed in state of **dynamical interrelation**. Decline in noble-commoner ratio had beneficial effect on noble incomes. Vice versa: increasing nobles relative to commoner numbers had depressing effect on mean incomes, even greater effect on proportion of poor nobles.

**Incomes enjoyed by noble families affected their numbers** (feedback loop). High incomes promoted family multiplication and upward mobility; low incomes compressed nobility by squeezing out poorest stratum. System not dynamically closed — affected by factors varying with secular cycle phase: commoner numbers, high commoner numbers drive up rents and depress wages (benefiting elites), sociopolitical instability.

**Civil wars**: sped up ennoblement rate of elite aspirants BUT elevated mortality (especially young males). One overall effect: **diminishing pool of elite aspirants** (wealthy commoners + younger sons of nobility) → perhaps temporary relief of social pressure.

## 5.6 Depression (1600–1660)

### Diverging Population Trends between North and South

Different regions of France exhibited significant variations in demographic trajectories. France large and diverse — many regions would not oscillate in synchrony. Main distinction: **French-speaking north** vs. **Occitan-speaking south**. Secondary divisions: ethnic fringes of northern France (Brittany, Flanders, Alsace — more in orbit of German than French politics at that time).

**Tithe records** (Le Roy Ladurie): both south and north followed trajectories of same shape, **shifted with respect to each other by twenty years**:

**South**: 15th century minimum 1400–1430 → expansion 1430–1450. **North**: minimum during 1440s → expansion after 1450 (probably lag in incidence of war-caused disasters). Century later: **south first to achieve maximum (around 1540)** → production stayed flat to 1560. **North**: maximum achieved twenty years later. **South again first affected** by devastation of Wars of Religion.

Documents on church property sale support tithe records: by 1568 a **"red zone" of maximum damage** centered on Poitou, Aquitaine, Languedoc. Where religious conflicts most intense and productive capacity most seriously undermined. By 1583 worst affected zones again in Occitanian heartland (Aquitaine, Gascony, Languedoc, Rouergue, Auvergne, Dauphiné). During 1590s war spread to north (Normandy, Ile-de-France, Picardy, Champagne) — all affected by final convulsions of Catholic League. However, **south also subjected to continuing damage**.

**Tithe records show three bad periods for Languedoc and Mediterranean south**:
1. First production fall (36%) right after war outbreak in 1560
2. Second wave during 1570s (production 43% less than prewar level 1532–50)
3. Third difficult period 1583–1596 (average delivery of tithes 36% below prewar level)

Adjacent regions affected almost as badly: Auvergne lost 35–40% productive capacity, Lyonnais lost 40%.

**French north**: also experienced significant drop, but **did not occur until 1590s** — generally less devastation than south. Overall, regions in north lost 33%, while Paris region lost only 20–25% (25% in tithes, 16% in rents). Eastern France similar to south (depth of fall), but timing of collapse same as north. Burgundy: net product of tithes (in kind) fell spectacularly 1588–1600 — grain tithes during twelve years fell 43% relative to prosperous period 1550–68. Finally, **least affected region in 16th century = Alsace** — net product of tithes fell only 7.3%.

**17th century trajectories**: different regions continued to diverge (parallelism lost). **Mediterranean south** seems to have escaped economic and demographic catastrophes of mid-17th century. Tithes expanded from minimum of 1580s until 1670s, after which declined again. In fact, **south entered period of long-term population decline lasting 1680–1740 or even 1750**.

<!-- pp. 201-220 -->

### Regional Variations in Recovery (France, continued)

- **North vs. South divergence**: North recovered after 1590s catastrophe but short-lived; northeast/north affected by **Thirty Years' War** (1618–48)
- **Alsace collapse**: Grain tithes fell to <10% of peak value; even after peace (1650s), production only 30% of 1620 levels
- **Northern devastation**: Cambrésis devastated by Franco-Spanish military operations; **Fronde** (civil war) particularly destructive in central regions
- **Beaune tithe curve**: Shows lows ~1590 and 1640–1660, rise from 1660–1720
- **South (Languedoc/Mediterranean)**: Tithes in kind recovered early 17th century, curve flattens ~1640
- Tithes never fully regained mid-16th-century levels (perhaps 5% below)
- Regional variations: Auvergne production 15% below 16th-century peak, Burgundy 13% below, Lyonnais stagnated except region around Lyons
- **Paris region exception**: Net product in rents from **Notre-Dame-de-Paris** estates increased 44% above 1580s peak
- Unclear how much from greater production vs. greater exploitation of peasants

### Growth of Top Fortunes

- Largest 17th-century fortune: **Jules Mazarin** (prime minister under **Louis XIV**), ~37 million livres at death (1661)
- Striking achievement: accumulated in 8 years between 1653 (lost most wealth during Fronde 1648–53) and 1661
- Other ministerial fortunes: **Henri IV's minister Sully** (5.2 million livres at death, 1610); **Richelieu** (22 million, 1642)
- **Claude Bullion** (Richelieu's finance minister 1632–40): 7.8 million livres in 8 years
- **Nicolas Foucquet**: assets >15.4 million livres at arrest (1661), though debts equaled assets
- Comparison to noble houses: **Princes de Conti** (close to king): 12 million livres (1655–65), declined to 7.6 million (1670s)
- **Louis Gonzague, duc de Nevers**: 8 million inheritance, but encumbered by 2.5 million debts
- Wealthiest noble house: **Princes de Condé**, ~31 million livres (early 18th century, shared among branches)
- **Colbert's wealth** (under Louis XIV): between 4.95–5.75 million, probably closer to high end
- **Louvois** gained ~8 million over two decades (1672–91)

> "After 1720, ministerial gains from office were small beer indeed compared to the situation before 1661" (Bonney 1999:127)

### Reversal of the Disintegrative Trend

- **1661 turning point**: **Louis XIV's** assumption of personal rule marked shift
- Most dramatic: **consolidation of elites around center**, ending intraelite conflict from preceding hundred years
- Elite energies channeled into **external conquest**
- Internal mechanisms studied by **William Beik** (1985, 2005) using **Languedoc** provincial aristocracy example
- Period of high sociopolitical instability peaked with **Fronde** (1648–53)
- Forced elites to understand need for military/diplomatic/economic protection from center
- 1560–1660: factions fighting in civil wars either entirely composed of elites OR elite-mobilized popular uprisings
- After 1660: elites withdrew leadership; uprisings declined dramatically
- Later popular uprisings (e.g., **Cévennes peasant rebellion 1702–4**) lacked elite support, easily suppressed
- New consensus allowed **unprecedented tax increases** (see figure 5.2)
- **Elites benefited as primary recipients**: comparative tax flow distribution between crown/regional elites (1647 vs. 1677, **Beik** 1985:267) shows regional elites increased share kept in provinces
- Lion's share financed **wars of Louis XIV** = improved employment for sword nobility
- Late 17th century: Louis's external conquest program highly successful

### Economic/Demographic Stagnation Continued

- Consolidation/reestablishment of order did NOT immediately end population stagnation
- Sustained demographic expansion only after 1720
- **Real wages** continued increase until 1680s (figure 5.1), though even at peak <50% of early 15th-century "golden age"
- **Agricultural wages** "exhumed" by **Jacquart**, discussed by **Le Roy Ladurie** (1987:352): followed same pattern around Paris
- **Two decades around 1700**: another substantial population decline
- Determining factor: worsening climate
- Sustained population growth began only after 1719, lasted to 1790
- Although elite/state dynamics turned corner ~1660, **demographic/economic trends showed continuing stagnation to end of century**
- Example: real-life dynamics may not fit neat division of secular cycle into phases

## 5.7 Conclusion

### Valois Cycle as Textbook Example

- **Valois cycle (1450–1660)**: textbook example of demographic-structural mechanisms in action
- Good historical record allows testing mechanisms with concrete/detailed data
- Attracted attention of giants like **Emmanuel Le Roy Ladurie**
- Processes include: **Malthusian effects** of population growth; how population growth/elite overproduction causes state breakdown
- Case study of **Norman nobility** illuminated elite overproduction development and consequences for elites/other social components

### Connection to Goldstone's Work

- **Jack Goldstone** (1991) did not significantly discuss preconditions of **French Wars of Religion**
- Yet France from 1559 onward matches patterns Goldstone discerned in other state collapses:
  - Financial ruin of state
  - Loss of control over military
  - Intensifying intraelite factionalism/competition
  - Finally full-blown civil war
- Also complexities in interplay among variables, mainly demographic/economic dynamics of commoner population during late 17th century
- Though 1660 used as cycle end, argument can be made depression phase lasted to end of century
- Demographicstructural theory = extremely useful tool for understanding historical dynamics
- History is complex; should not expect all to fit into single neat scheme

---

## Chapter 6: Rome: The Republican Cycle (350–30 BCE)

## 6.1 Overview of the Cycle

### A Secular Cycle during the Regal–Early Republic Period?

- Fragmentary sources allow at most hypothetical reconstruction of Rome's economic/social dynamics (regal/early Republic)
- **Between 650–350 BCE**: Roman polity went through complete secular cycle
- **Integrative trend** dominated before 500 BCE; **disintegrative trend** until early 4th century
- Evidence from cyclic dynamics of **public building activity**: first peak ~500 BCE, second during middle Republic (figure 6.1)
- **Fifth century BCE**: Rome went through heightened sociopolitical instability (figure 6.2), typical of disintegrative phases
- Saw-toothed pattern: operation of **"fathers-and-sons" cycles** (Sorokin's time series sampled at quarter-century intervals; fifty-year cycles show alternating high/low values)

### Start of Internal Instability

- Period began in Rome **509 BCE**: overthrow of last king **Tarquinius Superbus**, establishment of Republic
- Most of 5th century/early 4th dominated by **Conflict of the Orders**: struggle between elites/commoners AND between different elite factions
- **Two central issues**:
  1. **Disposition of public land**: 486–367 BCE, twenty-five separate attempts by plebeians to redistribute newly conquered areas as citizen allotments
  2. **Alleviation of debt burden**
- Important: "plebeians" NOT homogeneous group
- Majority motivated by land/debt issues
- Socially prominent/wealthy plebeians aimed at social/political equality with patricians: demanded intermarriage permission, eligibility for magistracies/priesthoods
- **Leaders of plebeian movement**: ambitious individuals from well-to-do families wanting power share
- Used economic concerns of commoners to mobilize political support against patricians
- Conflict ended by **Licino-Sextian laws of 367**: transformed political structure, ending discrimination against plebeians
- Created conditions for merging established/aspirant elites

> "Without major difficulties, therefore, they [plebeians] melted into a new social and political elite, the 'nobility,' already in the second half of the fourth century" (Von Ungern-Sternberg 1986:355)

### Phases of the Republican Cycle

- **Licino-Sextian compromise of 367**: marked shift from disintegrative to integrative trends
- Opened long/highly successful period of Rome's territorial expansion
- By end of 4th century BCE: **Latium and Campania** merged into **Roman-Campanian state** (agreement between Roman/Capuan aristocracies to create shared army)
- Great **Capuan families** welcomed into Roman senate
- Internal unity created conditions for territorial expansion: Rome established control over peninsular Italy, then acquired Mediterranean empire
- **Third century BCE**: Rome fought prolonged/bitter wars (Second Punic War particularly destructive), delivered shocks to population growth
- Growth resumed after end of **War with Hannibal** (218–201); Rome entered stagflation phase ~180 BCE
- No sharp transition between stagflation/crisis phases
- **Between 133–91 BCE**: Roman society slipped into crisis by stages
- First wave of severe instability: civil wars 90–71 BCE
- Relatively peaceful interlude 60s/50s
- Second series of civil wars 50–31 BCE: produced conditions for reversal of disintegrative trend
- **End of civil wars/establishment of Principate by Augustus**: end-point of Roman Republican cycle
- Disintegrative trend (combining crisis/depression phases) dated as century between **130–30 BCE**

### Explaining the Crisis

- **First century BCE**: state/society fundamentally transformed
- Proximate factors leading to Republic collapse/Principate establishment: series of civil wars afflicting Italy during this century
- Why Rome experienced this period of social/political instability lacks widely accepted answer
- Most influential model: **Keith Hopkins** (1978)
- Authors offer demographic-structural theory explanation that partly coincides with Hopkins's model, partly diverges
- Indebted to Hopkins's pioneering effort

## 6.2 Population Dynamics

- Lucky to have **census numbers** for Roman Republican period, transmitted by annalists
- Consensus among Roman historians: numbers can serve as reasonably solid basis for reconstructing Roman population dynamics (**Scheidel** 2001, 2004)
- Numbers refer to **adult free males** only
- Overall trend 350–100 BCE: growth, interrupted by sharp declines (figure 6.3)
- Population decreases correlate with intense warfare: **First and Second Punic Wars** (264–241, 218–201)

### Census Interpretation Debates

- What we want to know: not just Roman citizen changes, but **total population of peninsular Italy**
- Censuses cannot be taken as total population index: increased from demographic growth AND when free inhabitants of annexed territories admitted to Roman citizenship
- Particularly massive enfranchisement of Italian allies following **Social War**
- Italian population swelled by huge slave influxes from outside peninsula (increasingly significant as Rome's Mediterranean conquest proceeded)

### Population Trends (Late Republic–Early Principate)

- Main controversy: interpretation of **Augustan censuses**
- **"High" count proponents** (Frank 1933, Lo Cascio 1994, Morley 2001): census numbers = adult free males
- **"Low" count proponents** (Beloch 1886, Brunt 1971): numbers = all free inhabitants of both sexes, possible exception of infants <1 year old
- Here follow **Walter Scheidel** (2001, 2004, 2007): accepts Beloch-Brunt reconstruction
- **Brunt** (1971): free population of peninsular Italy numbered ~3 million in both 225 and 28 BCE
- Accepted by Scheidel with minor variations
- **Hopkins** (1978:68–69): population of whole Italy (including Cisapline Gaul) declined 225–28 BCE from 4.5 to 4 million
- Hopkins assumed 1.4 million inhabitants in northern Italy (Cisapline Gaul) in 225; **de Ligt** (2004:733) believes this figure is overestimate
- Safer to stick with peninsular Italy numbers alone

### Quantitative Estimates from Brunt's Census Returns

- Apparent stability (same population 225 and 28 BCE) is deceptive
- Estimates anchor **secular population cycle** Italy experienced in last two centuries BCE
- Cycle occurred in three phases
- Following Brunt's corrected census returns (Table VII) as indicators of Italian peninsula population

**First phase (225–200 BCE)**: Population declined from prolonged/destructive Hannibalic war
- Census numbers during this period: 273,000 (225) to 214,000 (203)
- 208 and 194: 137,000 and 144,000 (Brunt argues these low counts = stationing of troops abroad or deficient census procedures)
- Proportionate terms: decline is 22%; total free population declined from 3 to 2.35 million

**Second phase (2nd century BCE)**: Population increased
- Brunt accepts censuses of 168 (313,000) and 124 (395,000) as reliable
- Population growth 203–168 involved because 26,000 **Campanians** (switched sides in Hannibalic war) not counted in 203 but included in 168
- Starting-point population: 214,000 + 26,000 = 240,000
- Ending population: 313,000 + 33,000 (estimated soldiers abroad) = 346,000, or 44% increase
- **Free peninsular population increased from 2.35 to 3.39 million**
- In 124: census = 395,000; Brunt adds 38,500 soldiers = 433,500 total
- Change 168–124 in proportionate terms: 25%
- Free population in 124 was **4.25 million**

**Last phase (1st century BCE)**: Population declined from constant civil wars, high urbanization, later massive state-sponsored population transfers
