- Brunt's calculations based on census of 69: ~3.7 million
- Finally, by 28 BCE: declined further to 3 million, returning to 225 level
- Reconstructed population dynamics depicted in figure 6.4

### Caveats on Population Reconstruction

- Graph in figure 6.4 somewhat deceptive: overly smooth
- Each estimate comes with ≥10% error
- Start of population decline probably occurred in early 1st century, not right after 124
- Census of 114 reported essentially same numbers as 124; population probably stagnated until beginning of **Social War** in 91
- In 85: reported census numbers rose to 463,000, but increase probably reflects partial enumeration of newly enfranchised Italian allies
- All these numbers refer to **free population**
- Dynamics of slave population essentially inestimable, but by cycle end probably reached **1–1.5 million**
- Total population (free and slave) decreased less during 1st century than shown by graph (or even stayed flat)

### Social Structure and Elite Dynamics

**Top stratum (magnates)** during Republican period: **senatorial order** (*ordo senatorius*)
- Senators = governing class: served as government officials, monopolized chief priesthoods, provided army officers
- Came from patrician and plebeian clans (*gentes*), were wealthy landowners

**Equestrians** (*equites*): lesser aristocrats, originally served as cavalry
- Stratum provided businessmen (merchants conducting overseas commerce) and *publicani* (monopolized state contracts)
- Late Republic: jury service reserved for equestrians

**Beginning of cycle (ca. 300 BCE)**: majority of commoner population = small landowners (citizens and families)
- Citizens served as infantry (hoplites) in army, voted in assembly of arms-bearing males (*comitia centuriata*) and other popular assemblies electing public officials
- Divided into five classes according to property value (table 6.1)
- Wealthier citizens (property >100,000 asses) and equestrians had more than half votes, dominated elections
- Also paid bulk of property tax, the *tributum*

**Propertied citizens** (*assidui*): not poor
- Third census class (property ≥50,000 asses) = "medium-sized" landholders
- *As* around 200 BCE weighed two Roman ounces of bronze; value ~0.4 g silver
- During War with Hannibal: senate called for this class to contribute one slave + six months provisions as fleet rower (wealthier citizens expected to contribute more)
- Suggests degree of wealth well above subsistence level

**Lower strata**: landless citizens (*proletarii*), foreigners, freedmen, slaves

**Social structure dynamics**: very dynamic around 300 BCE
- Bulk of population most likely smallholders (*assidui*); landless *proletarii* were minority
- **Brunt** (1971:77): in 218, *proletarii* amounted to half the citizen body
- **Rosenstein** (2004:185): cannot be right
- While *proletarii* did not serve in army, they served as rowers in fleet
- In 214: when no more than 20,000 *proletarii* serving in that capacity, senate forced to recruit slaves as galley rowers
- Brunt calculates in 214 about 100,000 citizens under arms or killed
- Assuming similar military participation ratios: late 3rd century ratio of *assidui* (army service) to *proletarii* (rowers) roughly 10:2
- **Rosenstein** (2002): about 90% of citizenry were *assidui* (half of older married men not drafted to operate farms)

**Third century**: ratio of wealthy landowners (equestrians and senators) to nonelite landholders (*assidui*) just under 1:10
- Suggested by Roman cavalry/infantry numbers in 225 reported by **Polybius**: 23,000 and 250,000
- Same proportion as regal period
- Using this template together with calculation in previous paragraph: breakdown of Roman social structure in mid-Republic:
  - *Equites* (including senators): *assidui*: *proletarii* **1:10:2** (or 1:10:1 if Rosenstein estimate correct)
- Must add unknown numbers of slaves and foreigners

### State Finances

- Basic demographic-structural model developed for states with approximately stationary territory
- Rome's territory expanded from ~5,000 km² (4th century BCE) to >3 Mm² (end of 1st century BCE)
- Such spectacular 600-fold territorial expansion = factor of enormous significance, must be added to basic model described in chapter 1
- Territorial expansion helped solve problem of landless citizens
- Affected how Roman elites and state secured means of livelihood
- Whereas in "typical" agrarian societies elites/state extract surplus from commoner population (rents and taxes), in Republican Rome ruling class largely lived off spoils of conquest
- When **Third Macedonian War** ended (167) with particularly large booty haul: taxes levied on Italian land owned by Roman citizens abolished
- Land tax not reimposed until end of 3rd century CE
- State took cut of booty from conquest and taxed conquered territories at probable rate of 10% of crop on average
- Result of Rome's war success: state revenues more than quadrupled during 3rd century (table 6.2)
- During succeeding century: while population/elite numbers expanded, state revenues stagnated at 55–80 million sesterces
- Main reason: after **Third Punic War** (149–146 BCE), Rome had simply run out of wealthy states to conquer
- Endemic war against Spanish guerrillas consumed men/treasure without yielding significant booty
- Next great leap of revenue expansion came only after first round of civil wars

### Sociopolitical Instability

- Two centuries between last exit of Plebeians (342) and **First Servile War** (135–132 BCE): remarkably free of civil warfare (table 6.3)
- By contrast: century following murder of **Tiberius Gracchus** = almost continuous internecine war

**Coin hoards as instability indicator**: **Michael Crawford** (1974) argued deposition rate of coin hoards = faithful reflection of internal instability conditions
- Plotting Crawford data with internal warfare index: remarkable degree of parallelism (figure 6.5)
- Curves don't coincide in all details (natural when dealing with real data)
- Example: coin hoards trajectory has minor peak in 150s; no significant internal disturbances during this decade
- Crawford thought these hoards could have been buried by legionnaires prior to leaving for/dying in external wars
- Another possibility: peak in 150s = statistical fluctuation (period has only eight hoards)
- **Three major periods of internal war** reflected in both curves:
  1. Hannibalic wars (late 3rd century)
  2. Two civil wars of 1st century
- Second century relatively peaceful: few hoards, no significant internecine infighting during first half
- Rising hoard deposits during second half suggest growing sociopolitical tensions

## 6.2 An Unusually Long Expansion (350–180 BCE)

- After wars with Samnites and Italian Greeks: Roman state ended up with indirect control of peninsular Italy (~20% territory in Ager Romanus)
- Territorial expansion accompanied by growth of citizen body (table 6.4)
- If we can trust census data (consensus among historians: we can, at least from late 4th century onward), then Roman population almost doubled between mid-4th and midthird centuries
- Territory quintupled during this period
- Result: **population density actually declined** (table 6.4)
- Development explains large degree alleviation of land problem plaguing Roman poor since 5th century
- Census numbers reflect not only (perhaps not primarily) demographic growth
- Numbers of citizens increased by enfranchisement of Latins and Campanians
- Estimated 70,000 Romans lost to citizen rolls as result of colonization during period 334–263
- Population growth also undoubtedly slowed by series of intense wars (late 4th century/all of 3rd) against Samnites, Italian Greeks and Pyrrhus, Carthaginians
- Wars fought within central and southern Italy, resulted in much devastation
- Particularly destructive: **Second Punic War** (218–201 BCE)
- Census numbers dropped from high near 300,000 (265 BCE) to close to 200,000 or even less around 200 BCE (censuses of 209 and 194 transmit figure ~140,000; for 204, 214,000)
- Part of drop was artifactual: reflects absence of large numbers of soldiers stationed away from Rome, not counted in census
- Also no question: Roman citizenry suffered huge casualties
- However, logical to assume numbers of females and children did not decline to same extent as males of military age
- Result: census numbers rapidly bounced back to 250,000 level in early 2nd century

### Falling Population Density and Its Consequences

- Falling population density due to war losses and territorial expansion meant internal competition for resources ceased to be factor in Roman politics (until next stagflation phase)
- All efforts of both aristocracy and commoners directed at struggle with external enemies
- Aristocracy also severely affected by war losses
- Example: battle of **Cannae** (216 BCE): eighty senatorial casualties
- Natural attrition and earlier battles: ninety-seven additional vacancies between 219 and 217
- In other words: instead of normal turnover of 10% over these three years, almost 60% of senate obliterated
- Intense wars of 3rd century (aristocracy fighting on front lines) helped prevent elite overproduction
- Once Second Punic War ended (201 BCE): all subsequent Roman wars fought abroad (outside peninsular Italy)
- Rome typically had upper hand in conflicts; casualties lighter
- Result: second century saw period of sustained population growth
- Census numbers increased from 258,000 (189) to 394,000–395,000 (125 and 114 BCE)
- Estimated free population of peninsular Italy increased from 2.35 million to 4.25 million (section 6.1)
- Between 200 and 91 BCE
<!-- pp. 221-240 -->

## 6.3 Stagflation (180-130 BCE)

### Summary of Expansion Phase Demographics

- Second century BCE population nearly doubled between 203 and 124 BCE
- Territory of **Ager Romanus** stayed constant at 55,000 km² (Beloch 1964)
- Massive population growth created demographic-structural pressure through **equal division of property among heirs**
- After 2-3 generations of rapid growth, property fragmentation rendered plots insufficient for subsistence
- Result: decline of small landowners (*assidui*), growth of landless *proletarii* and slaves
- Population growth brought typical Malthusian effects: increased disease frequency and severity in second century

### Demographic Trends in Second Century BCE

**The Conventional Explanation for Late Republican Transformation**

- **Traditional view** (Toynbee, Hopkins, Brunt): long wars undermined agricultural stability of free farmers
- Hannibal's victories inflicted ~110,000 battle casualties (Polybius; Brunt thinks exaggerated)
- Wars fought in peninsular Italy caused widespread destruction of productive infrastructure
- Roman censuses: 292,000 citizens in 264 BCE (before First Punic War), 214,000 in 203 (near end of Second Punic War)
- Returning soldiers found farms ruined, burdened by debts from supporting families
- Many farms seized for debt or taken over by larger neighbors
- Elites invested conquest profits in land, creating **latifundia** (large-scale capital-intensive operations producing for market)

> "The transformation of a subsistence economy which had previously produced only a small surplus into a market economy which produced and consumed a large surplus was achieved by increasing the productivity of agricultural labour on larger farms. Fewer men produced more food. Under-employed small-holders were expelled from their plots and replaced by a smaller number of slaves." (Hopkins 1978:2-5)

**The Revisionist Challenge** (Jongman 2003, Rosenstein 2004, de Ligt 2004, 2007)

- **Nathan Rosenstein** argues Roman soldiers were "willing and often enthusiastic participants" not victims
- Enthusiasm waned only after mid-second century—defeats in Spain frequent after 153 BCE, morale "at rock bottom"
- New plantation agriculture appears in literary/archaeological record mid-second century at earliest
- Widespread only during age of Sulla—several generations after peak war casualties
- Casts doubt on direct causal link between war casualties and rise of *latifundia*

**Rosenstein's Alternative: Demographic-Structural Argument**

- Demographic models show Roman smallholder families could readily spare young males without going under
- Main problem: **rural underemployment** during second century, not labor shortage
- Wars paradoxically contributed to rising population by causing male deaths (218 BCE to late second century)
- As population multiplied after Hannibal's defeat, smaller inheritances created more *proletarii*
- By 133 BCE Roman warfare had produced a body of poor smallholders in lowest census classes
- With end of colonization after 181 BCE, no safety valve for families unable to establish children on farms

### Economic and Social Effects

**End of Second Punic War restored conditions for population growth:**

- Century-long internal peace in peninsular Italy after 201 BCE
- Infrastructure damaged by conflict rapidly restored
- Growth somewhat inhibited by external war casualties and colonization of Po Valley until 181 BCE
- Between 203-124 BCE population almost doubled while territory stayed constant

**Economic consequences of property fragmentation:**

- Some former *assidui* sold land to elites flush with conquest spoils
- Others tried to continue, ran up unsustainable debt, lost land
- Class of small landowners diminished, accompanied by growth of landless *proletarii* and slaves
- Many landless citizens moved to Rome; others rented land from elite landowners
- **Jongman** (2003) argues degree of "latifundization" in late Republic is overestimated

### Economic Trends

**Price and wage inflation 150-50 BCE:**

- Late third century BCE: legionnaire received 3 asses per diem = cost of 1 modius of wheat (Harl 1996)
- Annual pay: 108 denarii = 360 modii = 2.34 tons of wheat (~10 annual rations)
- 141 BCE: denarius revalued at 16 asses; legionnaire pay set at 5 asses per diem
- Annual pay increased to only 112.5 denarii (nominal), staying constant in real terms—mild inflation to this point
- Caesar in 46 BCE raised military pay to 225 denarii per annum (2.5 HS per diem)
- Doubling reflects wheat price increase—Cicero's "fair" price is 2.5 HS per modius
- Between 141-46 BCE: instead of wage increases, soldiers compensated with triumphal donatives and land promises
- **Real pay dynamics**: started high at 2.34 tons grain/year in 141, collapsed by factor of two or more in 90-71, then regained 141 level by 10-9 BCE

**Wage increases:**

- Unskilled laborer: 2 HS per day (Cato, second century), 3 HS per day (Cicero, first century) per Wells 1992

**Urbanization and industry:**

- Rome's population tripled during second century: 150,000 to 450,000 (Chandler 1987)
- Alternative estimate: 150,000 (200 BCE) → 375,000 (100 BCE) → 600,000 (50 BCE) per Scheidel 2004
- Two drivers: (1) conquest wealth enriched elites who employed tradesmen/artisans; (2) landless peasants migrated seeking opportunity
- Urbanization driven by both pull and push forces (parallels English and French cases)
- By end of second century push overwhelmed pull: not enough jobs, high housing demand, rising rents, increasing crime
- Urban poor became increasingly discontented

### Elite Dynamics

**Growth in elite numbers and wealth:**

- Archaeological data confirm simple early Republican elite lifestyles
- After ~300 BCE, elite consumption levels began increasing
- Temple-building activity increased around 300 BCE, peaked in second century
- Monumental tombs in Etruria increased from late fourth century (Barker and Rasmussen 1998)
- **Shatzman** (1975): analysis of 200+ Roman senators shows expenses rose steadily after Second Punic War (201 BCE), peaked after Sulla retired (79 BCE)

> "Rome's wars in the East caused a rise in the standard of living. Once the senators discovered the refined and sophisticated tastes of the Hellenic world, they were not slow to imitate what they found, and to increase their private outlay." (Shatzman 1975:98)

**Scale of private fortunes in first century:**

- Five individuals possessed fortunes >100 million HS: **L. Balbius, M. Crassus, L. Lucullus, Pompey, L. Tarius Rufus**
- 1 HS ≈ 1 g silver → 100 million HS ≈ 100 tons of silver
- "Ordinary" senators: several million sesterces fortunes
- **M. Cicero** had 13 million HS; wrote that rich Roman needed income of 100,000-600,000 HS per year
- Total fortunes/annual incomes in preindustrial societies typically relate in ratio of 20-12 to 1 → fortunes of 1.2-12 million HS
- **L. Aemilius Paullus** (ended Macedon in 167) left only 1.44 million HS at death (Crawford 1993:75)—order of magnitude less than average fortunes a century later

**Increasing impoverishment of lower strata:**

- Capital wealth for first *classis* lowered from 120,000 to 100,000 asses to counteract increasing impoverishment of *assidui*
- Lower boundary for *assidui* (fifth class): once 11,000 asses (Crawford 1993:97) → lowered to 4,000 (first half second century) → 1,500 (by 141) → eventually abolished
- 107 BCE: **Marius** enrolled *capitate censi* (those without property simply listed in census)—the *proletarii*
- Declining proportion of self-supporting freeholder (*assiduus*) manifested in declining Roman citizens vs allies in armies
- By end of second century: ratio of allies to Romans was 2:1

### Intraelite Competition

**Hopkins and Burton study of consular dynamics:**

- Study focused on **consuls**—top stratum of Roman hierarchy, chief elected officials
- Two consuls elected annually; over 30-year generation could be max 60 consulships
- 50-60 men at top of hierarchy at any time provided excellent sample for elite dynamics

**Traditional view challenged:**

- Before **Tiberius Gracchus's** tribunate (133 BCE): ten *gentes* supplied 50% of consuls, another eleven clans supplied 30% (De Sanctis, cited in Toynbee 1965)
- Small proportion—5% of more than 400 *gentes* that produced magistrates—dominated top stratum
- **Hopkins and Burton** argued power concentration was exaggerated
- One-third of consuls elected 250-50 BCE had no direct consular ancestor in previous three generations
- Only a third had consular son, less than quarter transmitted status to grandson
- "There was a continuous movement into and out of the Roman political elite during the last two centuries of the Republic" (Hopkins 1983:32)

**Elite tightening toward Gracchan period (figure 6.6a):**

- Proportion of consuls with consular ascendants increased from 49% (249-220) to 64% (139-110)
- Nobles tightened grip on consular power toward Gracchan period
- Same dynamic with consular fathers: 30-38% before 170 → 58% in 139-110 (figure 6.6b)

**Anisotropy in status transmission suggests interesting dynamics:**

- Strong anisotropy: consular ascendants vs descendants shows consular stratum undergoing change
- Low probability of transmitting consular status to descendants (32% to son, 24% to grandson)
- Data broken down by thirty-year periods (generations) allows examining temporal changes in status transmittal probability

**Key findings (Hopkins 1983: Tables 2.2 and 2.4):**

- Proportion with consular ascendants increased from 49% (249-220) to 64% (139-110)—nobles tightened grip
- Proportion with consular grandson fluctuated ~30% until 169-140, then suddenly dropped to 13% in 139-110
- Proportion with consular sons dropped from 38% to 27% (figure 6.6b, dashed line)
- Proportion with consular great-grandsons dropped from 27% to 10%
- **Paradox**: while nobles tightened grip on consulate, their ability to transmit status to descendants decreased

**Explanation: elite overproduction**

- Pool of aspirants for consular positions steadily expanded during second century
- Increased numbers of nobles contended for same maximum of sixty consular positions per generation
- Intraelite competition increased, probability of losing grew
- First: choking off of upward mobility, growing domination of consulate by nobles
- Second: intensifying competition among descendants of nobles

**Evidence from "inner elite" (table 2.7):**

- "Inner elite" = consuls with both consular fathers and grandfathers
- Second half third century: each inner elite member produced on average 1.25 consular or praetorian sons
- Since >1, inner elite was expanding in numerical terms
- Half century later average dropped to 0.83, then 0.63 in 139-80

**Elite production surplus:**

- 40% of consuls had consular fathers; 32% had consular sons
- In stable situation these should be equal
- Data suggest ~25% surplus elite production per generation
- Extra 20% (0.25/1.25) of consular aspirants frustrated in ambitions, forced into downward mobility as "mere" senators

**Father-son ratio dynamics over time:**

- Until mid-second century, curves fluctuate at roughly same level
- During generation 194-170 (following severe pruning during Hannibalic war), consuls with consular fathers dipped to 30%, consuls with consular sons stayed at 38%—reduced intraelite competition
- During Gracchan period (139-110): consuls with consular fathers shot up to 57%, consuls with consular sons dropped to <27% (figure 6.6b)
- **Result**: rate of elite overproduction peaked right before century-long period of sociopolitical instability
- Peak of intraelite competition fell within next generation (roughly time of Gracchi to Social War)

### State Finances

**Relatively healthy finances 146-91 BCE:**

- 157 BCE: treasury reserve built up to 72 million HS (Harl 1996:44)
- Eve of Social War: 80 million HS (Harl 1996:50)
- Social War rapidly consumed treasury reserves
- Long-term consequence: Social War destroyed fiscal equilibrium of late Republic

**Increasing financial difficulties after 91 BCE:**

- Denarius debased to 95% silver (Harl 1996:50)
- State ceased paying soldiers—treasury empty 89 BCE for first time since 213-12 BCE (darkest days Second Punic War)
- After end of war financial situation so desperate senate in 88 BCE melted sacred treasures to finance Sulla's war in East
- Expedient yielded ~9,000 pounds gold (Harl 1996:50) = 43.2 million HS (Crawford 1974:637)
- Temple treasuries looted by Sulla and allies during civil war 83-82 BCE
- Once Sulla in power and stability reestablished, he got state finances in order
- 81 BCE: Sulla restored denarius to purity (98% silver)

**Sulla's fiscal success depended on Eastern victories:**

- Mithridates indemnity: 2,000 or 3,000 talents
- Fined cities of Asia for five years back taxes: 20,000 talents (Harl 1996:51) = 480 million HS
- Amazingly, these huge treasures immediately spent, money shortages continued throughout 70s
- Sulla instituted new taxes and passed legislation authorizing sale of *ager publicus* (Crawford 1974:638)
- During 70s (peak fighting in Spain against Sertorius): denarius debased to 96-97% silver (Harl 1996:54)

**Pompey's fiscal reorganization:**

- Reorganized Spain and eastern provinces so taxation replaced predatory plundering (Harl 1996:54)
- Result of Pompey's reorganization: Rome's revenues quadrupled (table 6.2)
- Tribute from Gaul (conquered by Caesar) increased total to 380 million HS
- Revenues allowed Republic to amass great reserve including 15,000 bars of gold (we don't know weight)
- Reserve seized and immediately spent by Caesar in 49 BCE
- Year later Caesar short of bullion, forced to lower denarius standard to 95-96% fine (Harl 1996:55)

**Recurring cycles of feast and famine:**

- After Caesar's assassination: 44 BCE treasury reserve 700 million HS, immediately squandered by successors in internecine wars (Harl 1996:56)
- 44-42 BCE: triumvirs debased denarius to 95-96% silver
- By 31 BCE: denarius minted by Mark Antony declined to 92% fineness (Harl 1996:59)
- Meanwhile **Octavian** able to increase pureness of denarius he struck
- Augustan period (after 27 BCE): purity restored to 98%, stayed that way for century thereafter

### Increasing Social Pressures

**Growing misery among lower strata matched by increasing discontent among elites**

- Hopkins and Burton data: intraelite competition for consular positions intensified toward Gracchan period
- Increased numbers of aspirants → even though established nobility strengthened grip on consulships, high proportion of descendants forced down social ladder
- Same dynamic must have affected senatorial class (senate stayed constant at 300 until doubled by Sulla, then doubled again by Caesar)

**Pressure on nonsenatorial elites (equestrian class, *ordo equestris*):**

- Increasingly difficult to achieve senatorial status, almost impossible to break into consular nobility
- Social arrangements under Roman Republic ensured "knights" (*equites*) got their share of Mediterranean conquest profits
- Senators excluded from state contracts (construct public works, operate mines, collect taxes, supply military)
- Contracts undertaken by companies of *publicani* from equestrian class
- During first half second century, large number of knights grew extremely wealthy from *publicani* activities, invested in land, developed senatorial aspirations
- Competition for limited senatorial positions aggravated by increasing aspirants from established families (population growth) and pressure from newly rich knights

**Allied Italian elites in worse situation:**

- Largely excluded from profits of overseas conquests
- After defeat of Hannibal, Romans increasingly treated Italian allies as subjects
- Allies in turn demanded citizenship
- Roman elites obstinately refused to correct this injustice
- Roman ruling class, faced with increasing competition from locally grown elite aspirants, understandably not eager to add Italian elites to citizenship

**Stagnation of state revenues (table 6.2):**

- Mid-second century: Romans ran out of wealthy civilized states to conquer
- After destruction of Carthage and Corinth (146 BCE): no profitable wars until conquests of Pompey and Caesar almost century later
- Rome had to deal with exhausting suppression of primitive tribesmen (Spain), pirates, slave rebellions, internal wars

### 6.4 The Late Republican Crisis (130-30 BCE)

**First symptom: slave revolts beginning 138 BCE**

- Italy: revolt suppressed with crucifixion of 4,500+ slaves at Rome and surrounding towns
- Uprising at slave market of Delos suppressed by force
- Silver mines of Laurium near Athens
- Pergamum: war of **Aristonicus** (bastard son of Eumenes II) leading "Sunstate" against Rome (132-129 BCE)—simply major revolt of slaves, proletarians, soldiers
- Worst: **First Servile War in Sicily** (136-131 BCE)

**First Servile War in Sicily:**

- Normal slave thuggery swelled into full-scale war ~136 BCE under leadership of Syrian slave **Eunus**
- By vomiting fire and uttering oracles, persuaded 70,000 (some say 250,000) followers he was Antiochus, king of Syrians
- After several years hard fighting, murder of many landlords, much property damage, Romans crushed revolt, extinguished last sparks in 131 BCE

**Subsequent slave wars:**

- **Second Servile War** (104-101) in Sicily
- **Third Servile War** (73-71) led by **Spartacus**
- Lesser revolts in Campania and Apulia (Bradley 1989)

**Why slave revolts succeeded:**

- Peasant rebellions rarely succeeded in agrarian societies when confronted by unified elites
- Slave revolts in late Republican Rome no exception
- Much more dangerous threat: when elites become splintered, certain factions mobilize popular support for power quest

**Tiberius Sempronius Gracchus:**

- Politically ambitious young noble from very prominent family
- Father achieved pinnacle of political success: served as consul (twice) and censor
- Nobles of **Tiberius Gracchus generation** faced stiffer competition than fathers
- Natural for one to use swelling popular discontent as engine of political advancement
- Great polarization of wealth (superrich controlling immense fortunes while most citizens landless) patently unfair
- Decline of "middle class" of *assidui* (traditionally provided bulk of recruits to legions) endangering Rome's existence

**Tiberius Gracchus's reform program:**

- Elected tribune of people 133 BCE
- Immediately introduced law to break up large private estates created from public land, divide among landless citizens
- After increasingly bitter struggle, Tiberius and 300 supporters murdered by group of senators and clients in Forum
- Land commission set up to administer Gracchan land law continued functioning after his death
- Next six years allotted land to >75,000 men, achieving partial alleviation of Rome's manpower crisis

**Death of Tiberius Gracchus formalized split into *populares* and *optimates* factions:**

- Struggle between two groups eventually plunged Italy into bitter civil war
- Elite factions NOT true political parties—members of same faction occasionally fought among themselves
- Main conflict between *populares* and *optimates*, but members of same faction on occasion fought each other

**Gaius Gracchus (Tiberius's brother):**

- Elected tribune 123 and 122 BCE
- Continued brother's land distribution program
- Introduced famous *lex frumentaria* (provided grain to Rome citizens at subsidized prices)
- Promoted other popular programs: colonization schemes, public works
- Attempted to procure citizenship for Italian allies, but without success
- During two years of tribunate, Gaius dominated political life of Rome owing to immense popularity among plebs
- Domination short-lived—Gaius's political rivals united against him, engineered defeat in third attempt to run for tribunate
- Gaius died shortly thereafter during street fighting between followers and enemies
- Three thousand partisans killed with him (Le Glay et al. 1997:103)

**Violent deaths of Gracchus brothers: sign Roman political framework unraveling**

### Relative Stability 130s-120s BCE

- Two decades 130s-120s relatively violent period in Roman history
- Characterized by multiple slave revolts, disturbances from Gracchan brothers murders, insurrection of allied town of Fregellae (125 BCE)
- Level of violence nowhere near peak two generations later (figure 6.5)
- Sociopolitical instability waxed and waned in pattern of alternating generations
- Violent Gracchan era followed by two decades relatively free of internal conflict
- Important external conflicts during this period: Jugurthine War in North Africa (111-106), war with Cimbri and Teutones (105-101)
- Side effect of these wars: rise of **Gaius Marius**, became one of most important *popularis* leaders during later civil wars

### Root Cause: Elite Overproduction

- Relative sociopolitical stability did not last long
- Root cause of instability—**competition for status resulting from elite overproduction**—in no way alleviated by developments during Gracchan period
- Began unraveling shortly before 100 BCE, starting with Second Servile War (Sicily 104-101)
- Even more important: intraelite conflict flared up again in Rome

**100 BCE crisis:**

- **Lucius Apuleius Saturninus** elected tribune for second time
- Embarked on full program of social legislation
- When ordinary methods for derailing legislation (vetoes, omens, violence) failed, optimate-dominated senate passed SCU against Saturninus and followers
- Angry mob of nobles and knights murdered Saturninus and supporters (including consul Gaius Servilius Glaucia)
- **Marius** (serving sixth consulship in 100) attempted to steer middle course between optimate and *popularis* factions
- Elite polarization had gone so far apparently no compromise possible, even when espoused by hugely popular figure
- Marius could not prevent murders of *popularis* leaders
- Did not gain any support from optimates but lost confidence of *populares*
- Had to leave Rome in kind of self-imposed exile without fulfilling promise to obtain land for soldiers

**Building pressure during decade after Saturninus murder:**

- Optimate leaders controlling public affairs through dominance at senate continued to deny land to Marius's veterans
- Answered demand of Italian allies for citizenship by expelling any who resided in Rome
- 91 BCE: one last abortive reform attempt by moderate optimate faction led by **Livius Drusus the Younger**
- Again came to nothing as result of Drusus's death at hands of unknown assassin
- Following year majority of Italian allies revolted against Rome (**Social War of 90-88**)

### Intensity of Warfare Measured by Coin Hoard Frequency

- Can be quite accurately measured by frequency of dated coin hoards (figure 6.5)
- After peaceful first half of second century, curve begins to increase around 130 BCE
- **No peak during Gracchan period** (characterized by political infighting and urban crowd riots rather than fullscale warfare)
- Hoard indicator reaches second peak after Hannibalic war during 80s and 90s
- Period of almost continuous civil war: Social War, civil war and victory of Marian faction (87), Marius's "Reign of Terror" (87-86), civil war and victory of Sulla (83-82), Sulla's famous proscriptions (82-81), rebellion of Lepidus (78-77), slave rebellion led by Spartacus (73-71)
- Outside Italy: Marian leader **Sertorius** led rebellion in Spain (82-72)
- Fragile equilibrium achieved 70 BCE under consulship of **Gnaeus Pompeius** (Pompey the Great) and **Marcus Licinius Crassus**
- Pompey and Crassus began careers as optimates (served as officers under Sulla)
- Legislative program moderate (restored tribune powers previously taken by Sulla)
- Consensus between rival elite factions sustained by conflict fatigue, from two decades incessant fighting leaving hundreds of thousands dead

### 60s-50s BCE: Relative Internal Peace

- Characterized by absence of widespread civil war (figure 6.5)
- Period disturbed only by Conspiracy of Catiline (63)—rebellion easily suppressed by consul **Marcus Tullius Cicero**
- Street fighting between factions of *popularis* **Publius Clodius** and optimate **Titus Annius Milo** (52)
- Military power of Rome directed outward
- Two decades saw first significant additions to Roman territory since middle of second century
- **Pompey** conquered Asia Minor and Levant (66-62)
- **Caesar** conquered Gaul (58-51)
- **Crassus** attempting to do same in Parthia, lost army and life (53)
- Additionally, Pompey put end to Mediterranean piracy (68)
- Despite relative internal peace, fiscal health of state remained fragile
- 67 and 63 BCE political crises depleted treasury (Harl 1996:49)

### Final Period of Civil War (49-31 BCE)

- Began 49 BCE when **Caesar** crossed the Rubicon
- Ended 31 BCE with battle of Actium
- Although Caesar began as *popularis* politician while Pompey was optimate, ideological differences became less important
- Nature of conflict became more or less naked power struggle between various elite factions

**Civil War phases:**

1. **49-45 BCE**: Caesar against Pompey and Pompey's partisans after Great One's death in Egypt (48)
2. **After Caesar's assassination** (44 BCE): struggle between Caesar's assassins (**Brutus** and **Cassius**) and Caesar's successors (**Mark Antony**, **Lepidus**, **Octavian**)
3. **41-31 BCE**: confused struggle between Octavian, Mark Antony, and Sextus Pompey (son of Pompey the Great) in various combinations
   - Eventually Sextus Pompey defeated in Sicily, escaped to Greece, executed (36)
   - Mark Antony defeated in battle of Actium (31), committed suicide in Egypt (30)

**Establishment of Principate by Octavian/Augustus (27 BCE) marks end of Republican secular cycle**

## 6.5 The End of the Disintegrative Trend

**Summary: century following tribunate of Tiberius Gracchus can be interpreted as disintegrative phase of secular cycle of Roman Republic**

**Three peaks of violence:**

1. Rather mild one during Gracchan times (133-121 BCE)
2. "Civil War I" (90-71 BCE)
3. "Civil War II" (50-31 BCE)

**Pattern of alternating generations:**

- Violent Gracchan era followed by two decades relatively free of conflict (at least internal kind)
- Important external conflicts: Jugurthine War in North Africa (111-106), war with Cimbri and Teutones (105-101)
- One side effect: rise of Gaius Marius, became one of most important *popularis* leaders during later civil wars

**As usually happens during disintegrative phases of secular cycles:**

- Sociopolitical instability waxed and waned in pattern of alternating generations
- Peak of intraelite competition fell within next generation (roughly from time of Gracchi to Social War)

<!-- pp. 241-260 -->

## Chapter 6: Republican Rome (Conclusion - pp. 241-244)

### Three Effects of Civil Wars on Elite Population

- **Physical liquidation during civil wars (90–71 and 50–31 BCE)**
  - When **Marius** won in 87 BCE, followers killed optimate enemies: "mutilated bodies littered streets, heads dripping blood decorated rostra"
  - **Sulla's reign of terror** (five years later): 15 consular-rank men, 90 senators, 2,600 knights executed
  - Sulla's proscriptions eliminated 90 senators = 30% of the 300-member senate
  - Second triumvirate proscriptions (43 BCE): 300 senators, 2,000 equites executed
  - Battle casualties: 91–82 BCE saw ~200,000 deaths; 49–42 BCE perhaps 100,000
  - Single battle example: 50,000–70,000 died when Sulla vs. opponents (82 BCE)
  - Sulla executed 3,000 of 12,000 prisoners at Campus Martius on November 1
  - 40,000 Romans died at Philippi (42 BCE)
  - First Servile War (104–100 BCE): 100,000 dead
  - Civil wars reduced elite aspirant pool by **at least half**, likely underestimate

- **Reproductive effects — family limitation practices**
  - Romans employed abortion and infanticide during crises
  - **Dio** noted women shortage in 18 BCE — likely female-biased infanticide among elites
  - Conclusion: Romans practiced **female-biased infanticide** during disintegrative phase

- **Inflation of honors during disintegrative phase**
  - Leaders bestowed status cheaply to reward followers
  - **Sulla** doubled senate from 300 to 600 (81 BCE)
  - Sulla expanded quaestors to twenty and increased priests
  - Praetorships increased from 2 to 4 (277 BCE), to 6 (197 BCE), Sulla raised to 10
  - **Caesar** (45 BCE) doubled quaestors to forty, increased praetors to sixteen
  - Caesar raised senate from 600 to 900, filling seats with supporters
  - Over long run, Caesar's reforms would increase senators to 1,200 (each quaestor became senator)
  - By Augustus's reforms: more than 1,000 senators existed
  - After century of Marius/Sulla/Caesar's successors, enough elite aspirants remained to fill 3× the Gracchan-era senate slots
  - Elite reproduction rate **below replacement level** during this period — confirms severe elite overproduction a century earlier
  - Senate expansion was means of "letting off steam" for aspirant elites, but made them vulnerable to civil wars

### Acquiescence to Downward Mobility

- Beyond mortality and depressed reproduction, many potential elites chose to exit politics
- Saw likely consequence = untimely battlefield death or purge
- Chose to be content with modest status and stay away from politics
- **Marcus Aemilius Lepidus** (consul 46 BCE, Second Triumvirate member): fell out with Octavian (36 BCE)
  - Octavian stripped him of power, committed to comfortable retirement in Circeii (Latium)
  - Lepidus lived peacefully for 24 years
  - "Hard to imagine Pompey or Caesar accepting such comfortable retirement"
- **T. Pomponius Atticus** (Cicero's confidant, publisher, banker): equestrian wealthier than many senators
  - Chose to stay away from politics during turbulent years 88–65 BCE
  - Moved to Greece for safety from Rome's political storms
  - After returning to Rome, patronized arts/literature, made contacts protecting him during civil wars
- Lower levels of hierarchy: many such **Lepidi or Attici** who chose estates over war
  - Numbers probably increased as futility of internal war demonstrated itself
- Poet **Tibullus** (probably 32 BCE): "I don't want to die young and for nothing!"
- **Vergil's *Georgics***: longing for peace — "so many wars throughout world…fields going waste in farmer's absence"

### Restoration Under Augustus

- After last civil war period, twenty years of "discordia, non mos, non ius" (discord, no custom, no law) beginning 49 BCE
- Italy exhausted and ready to welcome regime offering peace
- Century of sociopolitical instability dealt with **elite overproduction** problem
- Also induced Romans into powerful longing for peace
- **Augustus's rule** rested on broad popular consensus
- Example: Augustus gave up annual consulship (31 BCE to 23 BCE), people rioted, tried to force him to accept office, fearing diminution of authority
- Secular disintegrative trend reversed itself, new Principate cycle commenced

## Chapter 7: Rome — The Principate Cycle (30 BCE–285 CE)

### 7.1 Overview of the Cycle

- **Principate cycle**: 27 BCE to 285 CE (establishment of Augustan Principate to accession of Diocletian)
- Bulk of territorial expansion accomplished by end of Augustus's reign
- Territorial fluctuations thereafter relatively minor with minor demographic/economic effects

### The Four Phases

**Expansion phase** (Julio-Claudian and Flavian emperors, ~30 BCE to 96 CE):
- Population growth and economic expansion
- Political instability at very top affected mostly ruling class
- Six of ten Augustus successors (Caligula, Nero, Galba, Otho, Vitellius, Domitian) overthrown and met violent ends
- Accomplished by palace coup rather than full-blown civil war
- Most serious instability: civil war following Nero's death (March 68–December 69, less than 2 years)

**Stagflation phase** (96 CE to 165 CE):
- Began with accession of **Nerva** (96 CE), ended with arrival of **Antonine plague** (165 CE)
- Period of high political stability — five "good" emperors (Nerva, Trajan, Hadrian, Antonius Pius, Marcus Aurelius)
- Elites did very well, numbers grew
- Usually considered **Golden Age of Roman Empire**
- Increasing popular misery due to overpopulation and inflation (typical of stagflation phases)
- Peak of state power, territorial extent, economic prosperity for elites achieved
- Social/economic indicators (inscriptions, documents, building activity, marble/brick production) peaked ~130–50 CE

**Crisis phase** (165 CE to ~211 CE):
- Started with first appearance of Antonine plague (165 CE)
- Elite consensus unraveled; by end of period (**Commodus** succeeded) situation developed into full-blown civil war (192–97 CE)

**Depression phase** (211 CE to 285 CE):
- When **Septimus Severus** succeeded by **Caracalla** (211 CE) to 285 CE
- Characterized by incessant intraelite conflict, chronic civil war
- Further population decline (recurring epidemics 250s–260s among other causes)
- Relatively peaceful interludes from sheer exhaustion of warring parties
- Disintegrative trend reversed when **Diocletian** defeated rivals and established Dominate

### 7.1 Population Dynamics

**Augustan censuses and population growth:**
- Three Augustan censuses + one Claudian census of Roman citizen population (Table 7.1)
- Exclude slaves, include unknown proportion of Roman citizens outside Italy
- Decline tendency in first century BCE reversed ~30 BCE
- Population began growing at accelerating rate: 0.2% p.a. (end of 1st century BCE) vs. 0.5–0.7% p.a. (2nd century)
- **Brunt** estimates: given census undercounting + slaves, perhaps 7 million total in Italy at end of Augustan period (14 CE)
- Would imply population in 28 BCE somewhat below 6 million
- Brunt believed increase 28 BCE to 14 CE entirely due to enfranchisements of slaves/provincials (authors find unconvincing)
- Proportion of Roman citizens outside Italy didn't become significant until second half of first century CE
- Substantial part of census increase must be due to population growth in Italy
- Establishment of Principate marked end of destructive civil wars, beginning of long period of **Pax Romana**

**Census data (Table 7.1):**
| Year | Population (mln) | Implied growth rate |
|------|-----------------|---------------------|
| 28 BCE | 4.063 | — |
| 8 BCE | 4.233 | 0.2% p.a. |
| 14 CE | 4.937 | 0.7% p.a. |
| 48 CE | 5.894 | 0.5% p.a. |

**Post-48 CE population:**
- No census data after 48 CE
- Probable that population growth continued throughout first century, then stagnated or increased very slowly during second (until plagues)
- Greatest population growth occurred in provinces of Latin West (not Italy)
- **Frier** (2000) endorsed **McEvedy and Jones** (1978): total population of Latin West increased 25 to 42 million between 14 and 164 CE
- 40% increase in proportional terms
- 29 BCE to 164 CE: population increase of Latin West at least 50%, most likely more
- Initially more populous East increased less (20 to 23 million)
- At peak, total population of Roman Empire: variously estimated as 60 million (Frier) or close to 100 million (Beloch)
- Truth probably lies somewhere in between

**Italian population evidence:**
- One piece suggesting Italian population stagnated or declined during 2nd century: institution of **alimenta** (public assistance for freeborn children) under Trajan (or Nerva)
- Program prompted by perceived population decline of impoverished small farmers (especially central Italy) → reduction in Italian legion recruits
- Diminishing "middle classes" ≠ overall population declining
- Growing economic inequality (characteristic of stagflation) could reduce smallholders while some move up to elites, majority slide to poverty

**Archaeological data — Lewit's survey:**
- **Lewit** (1991): surveyed 201 excavated farm/rural settlement sites in seven western Roman Empire regions
- Time periods: 100–0 BCE, 0–100 CE, 100–200, 200–250, 250–300, 300–350, 350–400, after 400
- Determined proportion of excavated sites occupied during each segment (Figure 7.1)
- **Italy**: occupation index curve begins high (1st century BCE), peaks 1st century CE, declines 2nd century
- **Provinces**: tend to reach maximum during 2nd century (Figure 7.1)
- Minor exceptions: South Gaul (same 1st/2nd centuries), South Spain (peak 1st half 3rd century)


<!-- pp. 261-280 -->

## 7.2 Expansion (27 BCE–96 CE)

### The Commoners

- Early Principate reversed late Republic inequality trend through population decline in 1st century BCE
- Land redistribution created space for landless peasant settlement
- **36 BCE**: **Capua** gave up deserted land for **Octavian's veterans**; acquired funds through selling lands in Crete and aqueduct construction
- **42 BCE**: After **battle of Philippi**, Octavian stripped eighteen towns of land, divided among veterans
- **Cremona** example: 3,000–4,000 veterans received 35 iugera (9 ha) each — substantial allotments
- Land seizures stopped with end of civil wars
- **30 and 14 BCE**: **Augustus** spent 860 million HS purchasing land for veterans (600 million in Italy, 260 million in provinces)
- Peasant emigration to provinces further reduced population pressure on Italian resources
- **Walter Scheidel (2007)**: As many as one in ten free Italian men left Italy for legion service under early Principate; most never returned as surviving veterans resettled in provinces
- This "demographic steam" outlet declined during first century as military participation rates fell

**Slave population dynamics:**
- **Brunt** estimates 2 million slaves in Italy; **Scheidel** revises downward
- Slave numbers probably diminished during first two centuries CE
- Two causes: (1) Diminished territorial conquests after Augustus = fewer war captives; (2) Slaves couldn't reproduce biologically (manumission, lower birth rates)
- As population growth resumed, landless peasants became abundant
- Slave prices increased due to scarcity; real wages for landless peasants declined due to abundance
- More profitable to lease land for rent or hire workers than use slaves

### The Elites

**Wealthiest men in early empire:**
- **Gnaeus Cornelius Lentulus** (consul 14 BCE) and **Claudius's** freedman **Narcissus** each owned property worth 400 million HS
- Both fortunes belonged to early Principate phase
- Largest fortunes declined during first century due to persecution of wealthiest aristocrats
- **Narcissus** poisoned by **Agrippina** (Nero's mother) after Claudius's death
- **Claudius (41–54)** condemned thirty-five senators and many knights to death; fortunes went to imperial treasury
- **Nero** executed six largest landowners in Africa Proconsularis, gaining possession of rich **Bagradas Valley** region
- Starting 65 CE: Nero forced senators and equestrians to commit suicide after plot proliferation (including **Seneca** and poet **Lucan**)

**Elite depletion:**
- Civil war of 68–69 depleted senatorial ranks to ~200 members
- After **Nero** and **Domitian** purges, most old Republican noble families disappeared

**Elite extinction rates:**
- Under **Trajan** and **Hadrian**: Only ~30 senators bore old Republican nobility names
- Of twenty-six families elevated to patrician status by Augustus (27 BCE–14 CE) and Claudius (41–54 CE), only six survived under Trajan (98–117 CE)
- 23% retention rate in less than a century = extinction rate over 30% per twenty-five years, perhaps close to 40%
- **Hammond (1957:75)**: Patricians of Republican ancestry in senate declined from ~16% under Augustus (27 BCE–14) to 4.5% under Nero (54–68), slightly over 2% in 69, only 1% in 96, less than 1% in 117
- This implies 50% extinction rate per twenty-five years
- **Junia Calvina** was only descendant of Augustus alive in 70s

**Elite dynamics resembled Tudor "overmighty subject" downfall:**
- Some large senatorial fortunes lost to state through imperial persecution
- Others joined state when wealthy senators became emperors (e.g., **Titus Aurelius Antoninus** became emperor **Antoninus Pius**)
- First century saw relative power of most powerful/wealthy private individuals decline substantially vs. state fortunes

**Building dedications data:**
- Curves show initial peak under early Principate, declining during first century
- Low reached under **Flavians** and **Hadrian**
- Second peak under **A. Pius** in Italy (Figure 7.8, solid line), **M. Aurelius** in Sabratha, **Commodus** in Thugga, **S. Severus** in Lepcis Magna
- **Severus** was native of Lepcis Magna — his local patronage explains anomaly
- Funding whole buildings only possible for wealthiest elite members

**Power shift:**
- First century: aristocracy dominated by senatorial order
- Second century: senators lost power, persisted as status group
- Power accumulated in hands of provincial governors, military commanders of legions, commanders of **Praetorian Guard** (typically equestrians)
- Senate's gradual loss of status as repository of power elites
- Gravity shifted from senate to imperial bureaucracy, specifically to **Imperial Council**
- Began as **Consilium Principis** (informal conclave of Augustus's friends/advisors), converted to permanent structure by **Hadrian**
- Second century: senate lost real influence on imperial administration
- **Imperial Council** became true successor of old Republican senate after reorganization by **Septimius Severus**

### (De)urbanization

**Rome's population dynamics:**
- Authorities agree city of Rome population peaked late 2nd to late 1st century BCE at 800,000–1,000,000
- Based on free wheat dole recipients (*congiaria*) fluctuating between 150,000–320,000
- 0.8–1 million derived by: starting with 200,000–250,000 recipients, dividing by proportion of males over ten, adding elite and slave strata

**Population fluctuations via corn dole recipients:**
- **P. Clodius** (50s BCE): swelled to 320,000
- **46 BCE**: **Caesar** reduced to 150,000 by organizing emigration to provinces, tightening registration
- **Augustus**: numbers increased to 250,000+, reaching 320,000 peak in 5 BCE
- **Three years later**: sank to just over 200,000
- **Death of Augustus**: corn dole recipients numbered 150,000 (at least from his will)

**Pattern**: Urban population swells following protracted civil war periods (with lag), then gradually reduced during internal peace

**Proposed estimates:**
- Peak of 320,000 recipients implies ~1.2 million total inhabitants
- Trough of 150,000 corresponds to ~0.6 million (±10% uncertainty)
- Under late Republic and early Augustus: Rome population swelled to 1.2 million
- By end of Augustus's reign: declined to 600,000
- During peaks: city horribly overpopulated
- By 14 CE when population halved: quality of life more bearable, even for poorer citizen strata

**First century urban population:**
- Probably didn't grow much during expansion phase (typical rule: urban population proportion tends to decrease)
- **Stagflation phase (96–165)**: urban population should have increased
- Number of *vici* in city increased between **Hadrian** (117–38) and fourth century — almost certainly happened during second century
- By end of third century with start of next Dominate cycle: Rome population likely declined
- Third-century wall marked contraction from regions occupied during Augustan era
- Possible evolution: 0.6 million in first half of first century → well over 1 million at end of second → decline toward end of third

## 7.3 Stagflation (96–165 CE)

### Population Pressure and Economic Change

**Legionnaire wages (Table 7.5):**
- Nominal wage doubled during first and second centuries
- Expressed in silver equivalent: peaked at end of first century, declined during second
- Translated into wheat (using Egyptian prices): soldier wages barely compensated for inflation during first century (9.3 vs. 8.9 quintals difference within uncertainty)

**Egyptian wheat prices (Table 7.6):**

*Private transactions:*
- 18 BCE–47 CE: 7.2 drachmas per artaba (90 g silver/quintal)
- 78–79 CE: 10.6 drachmas (116 g silver)
- 112–135 CE: 10.0 drachmas (105 g silver)
- 150–200 CE: 15.3 drachmas (124 g silver)
- 250–300 CE: 42.9 drachmas (44 g silver)

*Official prices:*
- 13 BCE–65 CE: 3.3 drachmas (42 g silver)
- 99–162 CE: 9.0 drachmas (94 g silver)
- 246–294 CE: 146 drachmas (73 g silver)

- Nominal wheat price doubled or more toward second half of second century
- Not just due to debasement — price increased in silver units too
- Third century: nominal price rose to 200–300 drachmas per artaba, but entirely due to debasement
- In silver equivalents: wheat price actually declined in third century

**Real economic trends in Egypt (**Scheidel 2002**):**
- Reports means and medians of land prices, land rents, wheat and commodity prices, wages in nominal and real (deflated) terms
- Real values important because rapid inflation due to debasement makes nominal values uninformative

**Land prices controversy:**
- **Duncan-Jones (1974)** data (Table 7.7): median first increases then decreases — makes sense in demographic-structural terms
- Third century decrease probably more profound given silver debasement
- However, Duncan-Jones observed both maximum and minimum prices kept increasing throughout period
- Trend throws doubt on validity of median price trend
- **Alston (1995)**: attempted to trace price trends in "low-quality," "average," and "high-quality" land
- **Scheidel (2002)**: used two categories (less or more than 600 drachmas per aroura)
- Both roundly criticized by **Bagnall (2005)**
- Focus instead on **real rents** (also some information on real wages reviewed in section 7.4)

**Real land rents for wheat fields (Table 7.8):**
- Changes consistent among all regions
- Increase from first century toward 165 (eve of plague)
- Decrease in third century
- Post-plague decline on average 37%, suggesting substantial population decrease from epidemics and sociopolitical instability

### Elite Overproduction and Competition

**Consular ascendance/descendance statistics caveats:**
- Republican consuls: pinnacle of status and power pyramids
- Principate consuls: retained status only; power passed to emperors and top administrators recruited mainly from equestrians
- Data much sparser: Hopkins and Burton investigated only every other generation (four data points vs. eight for Republic)
- Under Republic: number elected to consulship stayed roughly constant (two per year)
- Principate: number increased gradually to eight or ten per year under last Antonines
- This affects conclusions drawn from proportions of consuls with consular ascendants vs. descendants

**Hopkins and Barton data (Table 7.9):**
- First century: proportion of consuls with consular fathers dropped dramatically (46% to 18%)
- Proportion with consular ascendant extending three generations back exhibits same qualitative dynamics
- Hereditary nobility's grip on consular status substantially weakened
- Makes sense given elite dynamics: old nobility decimated during Julio-Claudian periods
- Consular stratum size expanded substantially: under August ~2.6 consuls on average appointed per year
- By end of century: eight ordinary and suffect consuls appointed every year
- Assuming thirty-year life expectancy: consular stratum size tripled during first century (80 to 240 individuals)

**Second century trend inversion:**
- Proportion with consular fathers climbed from 18% to 27% to 32%
- Hereditary aristocracy reasserting grip on consular positions
- Never reached same control level as 18–54 CE or second century BCE

**Competition index patterns:**
- Proportion of consuls with consular descendants fluctuated at roughly constant level until mid-second century
- Plunged during 193–235 period (Table 7.9)
- Particularly interesting: difference between consular ascendants and descendants proportions
- During 70–96 and 131–60: index positive, suggesting relaxation of intraelite competition
- By end of second century: revert to pattern of heightened competition and forced downward mobility (similar to Gracchan period of Republic)

**Signs of elite overproduction:**
- Data patterns consistent with narrative in section 7.2
- Top elite stratum fortunes first declined, reaching trough during Flavian period
- Then increased under five good emperors, reaching peak during second half of second century
- Beginning with **Marcus Aurelius** and especially **Commodus**: signs of elite overproduction and increased competition contributing to state breakdown

**Administrative posts growth:**
- **H. G. Pflaum**: 64 equestrian posts in provinces under **Domitian** (81–96)
- 173 posts under **Septimius Severus** (193–211)
- Though second century territory expanded insignificantly (even contracted in places), administrative posts grew almost threefold
- Reflects pressure from surplus elites for government positions

## 7.4 Crisis (165–97 CE)

### Population

**The Antonine Plague:**
- Reached Roman Empire in 165, became known as **Antonine plague**
- Probably smallpox or combination of measles and smallpox
- Brought from eastern Mediterranean by Roman troops returning from **Parthian War**
- **165**: hit **Nisibis** and **Smyrna**
- **Following year**: reached Rome
- **168**: plague raged in Rome and many provinces
- **169**: caused annihilating losses in Egyptian taxpayers (death or flight)
- Reports crop up in 172, 174, 175, 179, 182, and 189
- **Dio**: plague of 189 in Rome was worst he knew, sometimes killing 2,000 people per day
- Roman Empire struck again by catastrophic epidemics in 250s and 260s

**Demographic impact in Egypt:**
- **Fayum area**: tax base dropped 33–47%
- Smaller delta villages: populations declined 70–93%
- Some decline due to flight rather than mortality, but most couldn't escape
- Losses in taxpayer base persisting four years after plague cannot be explained by emigration
- Mortality continued severe during later outbreaks
- Example: one-third of taxpaying population of **village of Socnopaiou Nesos** in Arsinoite died January-February 179
- Egyptian papyrus specifically recorded mortality, not flight

**Empire-wide impact estimates:**
- Outside Egypt: no estimate of population losses from 165–89 epidemics seems possible
- Literary sources report heavy mortality across empire and among soldiers
- **Frier's (2000:815–16)** estimate: up to 10% of empire's total population perished, percentage perhaps twice that in cities and military camps
- Many historians doubted Black Death of fourteenth century could cause catastrophic mortality until modern research demonstrated scale
- Authors' guess: mortality of Antonine plague during 165–89 comparable to **Black Death** in Western Europe 1347–80
- Frier's estimate should be doubled or tripled
- **Scheidel (2002)** points to "massive mortality" of as much as 25% in first ten to fifteen years

**Importance of timing:**
- Plague hit empire when already under enormous demographic-structural stress
- Without such stress, population losses would have been made up in a generation or two
- By 165: social system already near critical point; plagues pushed it beyond
- Analogy with post-Black Death Western Europe suggests Antonine plague imposed higher mortality on productive classes than on elites
- Serious elite overproduction problem already developed by 165
- Differential mortality hastened and exacerbated developing political crisis
- Ensuing century of sociopolitical instability prevented population recovery after epidemics (probably caused further population decline)

**Real wages in Roman Egypt (Table 7.10):**
- 2nd century baseline: index = 1.00
- 250s–260s: daily wages 1.25, monthly wages 1.17
- By 260s: real wages increased ~20% compared to preplague period
- Supports third-century population decline thesis

**Egyptian village evidence:**
- **Village of Theadelphia** in Fayum: 2,500 residents in 130s cultivated 1,600–1,700 ha arable land
- **216**: unknown but obviously diminished number cultivated 990 ha arable land
- Arboricultural land (vineyards and orchards) increased from 140 ha in 158 to 415 ha in 216
- "After plague, fewer people had to be fed, and these villagers enjoyed higher living standard than in 2nd c., either because they could afford to consume more wine and fruit or because they derived profit from selling these products to urban customers"
- Decreased importance of cereals in commoner diet parallels England after Black Death

### Political Crisis

**The "five good emperors" period (96–165):**
- After **Domitian's** murder and **Nerva's** installation by senate in 96: period of remarkable internal stability
- Five good emperors enjoyed goodwill of senatorial elite, propertied classes, and soldier loyalty
- Social and economic pressures rising, but internal peace lasted until 165
- Shattered by arrival of plague

**The "Antonine military crisis" (Greenberg 2003:424):**
- Germanic incursions into Pannonia in 167
- Invasion of Italy by **Marcomanni**, Greece and Balkans by **Costobocci** in 170
- Devastating raids on Spain and N. Africa by **Mauri** in 171
- **Boukoloi** revolt in 172
- Rebellion of **Avidius Cassius** in 175
- Subsequent wave of invasions of Spain and N. Africa in 177
- Protracted campaigning against various German tribes until **Marcus's** death

**Marcus Aurelius's success:**
- Able to hold ruling class together despite pressures
- Collapse occurred under heir **Commodus (180–92)**
- Resulted from "divisions and jealousies among members of Imperial family, military officers, and powerful senators"
- First plot hatched in 182: senators conspired with **Commodus's sister Lucilla** to assassinate him
- Followed by execution of conspirators and later senators close to **Marcus Aurelius**
- Disaffection spread into lower strata
- Declining real wages caused mutinies in provincial armies (Deserters' War)
- Spawned another assassination attempt

**Fiscal bankruptcy:**
- **Commodus** poisoned during end-of-year celebration
- Survived attempt (probably vomiting most poison from overdrinking)
- Assassinated next day in bath, strangled by wrestling partner **Narcissus** who joined plot
- Next two emperors (**Pertinax** and **Julianus**) lasted only eighty-seven and sixty-six days
- Senate called on **Pescenius Niger** (governor of Syria) to seize throne
- Simultaneously: armies of Britain and Danube declared for commanders **Clodius Albinus** and **Septimius Severus**
- **Severus** won race to Rome, became next emperor (193–211)

**Civil war continuation:**
- Continued from 193 to 197
- **Septimius** first moved against **Niger**, defeating and killing him in 194
- Lengthy siege to reduce Niger's base **Byzantium** (fell only in 195)
- Meanwhile: **Parthians** meddled in Roman civil war; Septimius campaigned there in 194 and 195
- **195**: **Albinus** (supported by large senate following) crossed channel into Gaul
- After two more years: Septimius's forces met and defeated Albinus's army near **Lugdunum** (Lyons)
- Septimius allowed troops to burn Lugdunum, then carried out ruthless campaign of extermination against Albinus's adherents in provinces and senate

## 7.5 Depression (197–285 CE)

**Septimius and Caracalla (197–217):**
- Reigns relatively peaceful
- Fit pattern of generation alternation during decentralization phase
- Equilibrium fragile and slow unraveled, beginning during **Caracalla's** reign

**Palace coups and assassinations (beginning 211):**
- Up to 235: sociopolitical instability took form of palace coups
- **211**: **Caracalla** killed brother and co-ruler **Geta**, purged Geta's supporters
- Monotonous litany of emperors assassinated and elevated:
  - **217**: Caracalla assassinated, **Macrinus** installed
  - **218**: Macrinus assassinated, **Elagabalus** installed
  - **222**: Elagabalus assassinated, **Severus Alexander** installed
  - **Alexander** lasted until 235, then also assassinated

**Civil war period (235–285):**
- After Alexander's death: intraelite conflict became general civil war
- Empire simultaneously ruled by several emperors or pretenders
- **Maximinus "Thrax"** proclaimed emperor by Rhine legions after Alexander's murder
- Beat back thrusts of Sarmatians, Dacians, and Goths but opposed by senate
- In Africa: legions proclaimed as emperors **M. Antonius Gordianus** and son **Gordianus II** (238)
- Both perished in war with prefect of Mauretania who supported Maximinus
- Rome senate raised **M. Clodius Pupienus** and **D. Caelius Calvinus Balbinus**
- **Maximinus** slain by own troops besieging **Aquileia** (June 238)
- **Praetorian Guard** murdered Pupienus and Balbinus, forced senate to recognize thirteen-year-old **Gordianus III** as emperor (238–44)
- **Gordianus** murdered by Praetorian prefect **Marcus Julius Philippus** ("the Arab") (244–49)
- **Philippus** killed at **Verona** (249) in battle against commander in Dacia **Decius**
- **Gaius Messius Quintus Traianus Decius (249–51)** slain by Goths in 251
- **Gaius Vibius Trebonianus Gallus (251–53)** proclaimed emperor by army of Moesia
- Fifteen-year plague began during his reign
- When marched against successor in Moesia (**M. Aemilius Aemilianus**), own troops slew him (before October 253)
- **Aemilianus** (253) proclaimed emperor, then murdered by own troops

**Age of Gallienus (253–68):**
- First co-ruler with father **Valerian**, then sole ruler
- Catastrophic external invasions and plague
- Two-thirds of Alexandira population perished; 5,000 died in Rome daily
- Internal warfare peaked, with empire fragmenting and pretenders cropping up everywhere
- "Age of thirty tyrants"
- During **Gallienus's** reign alone: eighteen usurpers attempted to seize throne
- Two-thirds of Roman Empire territory seceded (**Gallic Empire** under **Postumus** and **Palmyrene Empire** under **Odenathus** and **Zenobia**)

**Illyrian soldier emperors (268–285):**
- **268**: staff officers of Gallienus (all Illyrians) assassinated emperor, assumed control
- Cabal of frontier officers produced series of emperors known as "Illyrian soldier emperors"
- Rule interrupted only by short interlude in 275–76 when senatorial candidate sat on throne
- Illyrians began restoring empire
- Job largely accomplished in 285 with **Diocletian's** victory over **Carinus** at **battle of Margus**

## 7.6 Conclusion

**Data quality and theory fit:**
- Database on Roman Empire Principate economic and social dynamics not as complete as desired
- Empirical trends generally agree with demographic-structural theory predictions
- Population trend: up until mid-second century, followed by collapse and stagnation from Antonine plagues, then endemic civil warfare of third century
- Important regional differences: imperial core (Italy) peaked earlier than provinces, may have started declining before plagues

**Economic data:**
- Sparsest part of database
- Quantitative series available only for Egypt; even there data fragmentary with many gaps
- Existing data support Malthusian dynamic of increasing popular immiseration toward mid-second century, declining economic misery after population decrease after 165

**Elite dynamics:**
- Exhibited typical phase shift vs. commoner population
- General population grew during expansion phase (27 BCE–96 CE)
- Elites (especially top stratum, senatorial aristocracy) shrank, lost wealth and power
- Elite numbers and incomes expanded during stagflation phase (96–165 CE)
- One index of elite consumption (monumental buildings expenditure): curious two-humped shape with peaks in early first century and mid-second century (Figure 7.8)

**State finances:**
- Healthy during integrative phase
- Fleeting periods of fiscal strain during first century
- Large budget deficits reported for Caligula and Nero suspect due to hostile sources
- Disintegrative phase financial crisis very real: drastic debasement of Roman coinage in third century (Figure 7.4)

**External relations:**
- Integrative phase: period of external conquests (especially early) and successful defense of *limes* later
- State expenditures on public building reached peak toward end of integrative phase (Figure 7.8)

**Instability patterns:**
- Recurrent instability during first century doesn't fit model
- Most took form of palace coups; only significant civil war (following Nero's overthrow in 68) lasted just eighteen months
- Stagflation phase (96–165): no significant instability events
- Disintegrative phase: characterized by recurrent civil war, barbarian invasions, territory loss

**Overall assessment:**
- Fit between theory and data probably as good as could be in historical applications
- Caveat: data somewhat scanty
- Principate period appears to be another nearly perfect secular cycle
- Probable reason for good match: Roman Empire was gigantic state (only other remotely threatening it was Parthian Empire)
- Rise and fall dynamics primarily governed by endogenous mechanisms

<!-- pp. 281-300 -->

## Chapter 8: Russia: The Muscovy Cycle (1460–1620)

### 8.1 The Fifteenth-Century Crisis

- Analysis starts mid-15th century when **Novgorod scribe books** provide detailed agrarian data
- Before mid-1400s, Russia experienced two secular cycles:
  - **Kievan cycle**: East Slavonic colonization → demographic crisis 1220s–1250s (Novgorod/Pskov lands)
  - Mongol invasion devastated other principalities mid-13th century
  - Population growth resumed 14th century, overpopulation signs by century's end
- Northwest Russia (poor soils, severe climate) hit carrying capacity faster than central regions

**Crisis causes differed regionally**:
- Northwest: famines and epidemics
- Central Region: civil war and Tatar invasions (famine/disease were secondary effects)

**Scale of catastrophe**:
- Archaeological evidence: leather shoe remnants and birch scrolls in Novgorod declined by factor of 2 (implying similar population decline)
- Grain prices halved, real wages surged (daily wage ≈24 kg rye)
- One-fifth of Moscow villages deserted, population losses even higher
- Stone building construction declined by factor 2.5–3
- **Tatar invasion 1408** under **Yedigei** → plague 1418 → internecine war ending 1453

**Financial collapse**:
- Moscow treasury empty, currency devalued
- Ruble lost 60% of value during first half of 15th century
- 1445: **Great Prince Basil II** gathered only 1,500 warriors to repel Tatar raid
- **Battle of Nerli**: Basil II defeated and captured

> "And they began the remnants of the Russian land while quarreling among themselves," wrote the Novgorod chronicler

- Crisis interconnected: economic collapse → weak military → civil war/raids → deeper economic decline → famine/depopulation

### 8.2 Expansion (1460–1530)

- Post-crisis population decline created classic expansion-phase conditions
- Low population density, high land-to-peasant ratios, high real wages, low rents
- Foreigners (e.g., **Barbaro and Contarini**) marveled at forests, abundant grain and livestock

**Wage data**:
- Post-crisis: daily wage >20 kg rye
- Early 1500s: still relatively high
- 1520s: unskilled Moscow worker earned 1.5 dengas/day (0.6 g silver) = 11 kg "grain" (rye+oats mix)
- Comparable to unskilled German workers 1490–1510 during early modern expansion

**Land and production**:
- Typical peasant household: 15 desyatins (16.4 ha)
- **Corvée system**: peasants worked lord's land (2 of 3 fields under three-field rotation)
- Family cultivated 18 desyatins total, paid crops from 3 (one-sixth)
- Large extended families (7–8 members) with horses typical
- Yield ratios: rye 1:3.3, oats 1:3.1
- Per capita consumption: **425 kg/year** (higher than Western Europe due to cold climate demands)

**Population growth**:
- **Ivan III** (1462–1505): "most tranquil and happiest time"
- Famine/pestilence/Tatar attacks abated
- Government documents mention new lands under plow
- **Two censuses** (1480s, 1500): population grew 14% → ~1% annual growth rate
- **A. I. Kopanev**: Russia grew 50% in first half of 16th century to 9–10 million

**Regional expansion**:
- Novgorod Land: population grew faster than cultivated area
- **Derevskaya District**: population +16%, cultivated land +6%
- Peasant-land ratio only 7 desyatins (half of neighboring **Shelonskaya District**)
- Settlement density 1480–1500 higher than any prior period
- Heavy clay soils brought into cultivation
- **Fertilizer use** became widespread

### 8.3 Stagflation (1530–65)

#### Population and Economy

**Chester Dunning** applied demographic-structural theory to 16th-century Russia:
- Population growth beyond subsistence → **price inflation** (confirming **Blum 1956**, **Mironov 1985**)

**Grain price trends**:
- Before 1530: stable around 10 dengas/quarter rye (6 g silver/quintal)
- 1530s: prices began rising
- 1532: rye at **Iosif-Volokolamsky Monastery** 22 dengas/quarter
- 1543–44: crop failure pushed price to 35–40 dengas

**Regional variation**:
- Northwest stagflation appeared before Central Region
- **Novgorod Land** dominated by large landowners extracting heavy rents (up to half the crop)
- Post-annexation by Moscow: in-kind rents → money rents (lightening burden on state peasants)
- **Pomestie** (military service lands): press on peasants changed little or increased
- Gentry-owned lands: peasants paid 10–12 puds (180 kg)/person rent
- After rent deduction: grain production insufficient for minimal consumption (15 puds/250 kg)
- Low consumption → greater mortality risk during crop failures
- Northwest epidemics "particularly severe"

**Population decline evidence**:
- Flight from gentry exactions
- **Vodskaya District**: population declined 17% between 1500–1540
- **Derevskaya Region**: 13% decline
- Some districts (Bezbetskaya, Shelonskaya) still grew 27–45%

**Central regions**:
- Population growth continued → diminished peasant land allotments
- Mid-1500s: 2–3 households sharing standard 15 desyatins
- **Borisovskaya District** (near Vladimir): average 7.5 desyatins/household
- **Belozerskiy District**: only 6 desyatins (insufficient for harvest lasting until next year)

**Peasant indebtedness**:
- Borrowed from monasteries (chief moneylending institution)
- Unable to repay → lost land
- **Russian Orthodox Church** owned ~⅓ of cultivated land by mid-1500s

**Return of crisis**:
- 1548–49: famine in north
- 1552: terrible epidemic in Novgorod and Pskov (30,000 died in Pskov)
- 1556–57: famine in north and Trans-Volga Region
- Peasants migrated south
- By late 1550s: 40% of Dvina river land abandoned

> "A highly unstable equilibrium" — exactly what demographic-structural theory predicts for late stagflation

**Livonian War (1557–82)** often blamed for northern decline, but crisis signs observable well before

**Central Russia contrast**:
- Trans-Moscow Land: population growth continued until 1560
- All cultivable lands already under plow
- 1560–61: Trans-Moscow Land famine
- Grain prices rose from 10 dengas/quarter (30 years earlier) to 50–60 dengas (6→26–31 g silver/quintal)
- Monks blamed land dearth and state exactions

**Real wage collapse**:
- 1520: day laborer earned 11 kg grain equivalent
- 1568: only 3.6 kg/day
- Real wage declined by **factor of three** in half-century
- 3.6 kg appears sufficient but day laborers hired for restricted periods
- At 200 days/year (standard Western Europe assumption): yearly income = 720 kg (insufficient for 3 people in Russia)
- Russian employment season shorter due to severe climate

**Year-round workers**:
- Agricultural laborer pay: 16 puds (262 kg) grain + 80 dengas cash (=118 kg grain)
- Total real pay: **380 kg** (not enough for 2 people)
- 1550s consumption as poor as famine of 1588–89

**Rural underemployment indicators**:
- Flowering of crafts, increased trade, growing urbanization
- **Derevskaya/Vodskaya** peasants couldn't grow enough grain → became traders/artisans
- Specialized settlements for handicrafts/trade emerged
- **Novgorod** by early 1500s: substantial city with 5,500 households, ~30,000 inhabitants (6,000 craftsmen — nearly all adult males)
- **Pskov**: 6,000 households, 30,000 people
- Seventeen churches built 1516–1533 (nearly as many as Moscow)
- Official 1560s census: 41,500 households
- 10% urbanization = upper limit given low agricultural productivity
- Repeated epidemics under **Basil III** (1505–33): 4+ in north, none mentioned in central Russia

#### Elite Dynamics

**Social hierarchy**:
- Top: **appanage princes** (close relatives of Moscow rulers)
- **Basil II, Ivan III, Basil III** reduced relatives' appanages but created new ones for junior sons (perpetuating system)
- Lithuanian victories brought **Lithuanian princes** (Vorotynskys, Odoevskys, Trubetskoys) — equal status to appanage princes
- Second level: **"service princes"** (descendants of Vladimir-Suzdal Land rulers who voluntarily subordinated to Moscow)
- Third level: **"Old Muscovite" boyars** (Morozovs, Zahar'ins, Chelyadins) — closest henchmen when Moscow was small principality
- Boyars held most important government positions (equivalent of modern ministers)

**Magnate stratum**:
- Princes and boyars together = elite
- Below: **"gentry" (dvoryane i deti boyarskie)** — mounted warriors
- Gentry stratified: Moscow-based (middlerank elites) vs provincial (quite poor — 4–5 peasant households)
- 1520s: 20,000 gentry cavalrymen on Oka defensive line (Table 8.2) → at least 20,000 military elite households

**Elite expansion**:
- First half 1500s: Moscow rulers encouraged gentry cavalry expansion
- Number of gentry servicemen grew substantially
- Mid-century: stocks of available land with peasants exhausted
- **Pomestie** land (granted on condition of military service):
  - 1500: 58% of arable land in **Shelonskaya District** (Novgorod Land)
  - 1540: 98% of total
  - **Bezbetskaya District**: 99% by 1544
- Average allotment size declined as numbers increased (Table 8.3)

**TABLE 8.2 Elite dynamics data**:
- 1520s: 20,000 southern frontier army
- 1560s: 100,000–120,000 total Muscovite army
- 1580s: 65,000 southern frontier army
- Total Muscovite army peaked at 80,000
- 1630: 15,000 southern frontier army
- 1700: 23,000 gentry owning peasants
- 1737: 46,000 gentry owning peasants

**TABLE 8.3**: Percent of gentry servitors in Novgorod Land with estates:
- 1500: <150 des. (22%), 150–300 des. (30%), >300 des. (48%)
