- 1540: <150 des. (39%), 150–300 des. (39%), >300 des. (22%)

#### Sociopolitical Instability

**Coin hoard evidence** (Figure 8.1):
- Small peak first half 15th century (probably 15th-century crisis)
- Low numbers fluctuating 0–3/decade until 1540s (orderly conditions)
- First jump: 1540s extending to end of century
- Huge jump during **Time of Troubles**
- Gradual decline interrupted by upsurge around 1700
- Low in mid-1700s

**Political narrative**:
- **Ivan III** (1462–1505) and **Basil III** (1505–33): internal unity, territorial expansion
- **Ivan IV** (Basil III's son): only 3 years old at father's death (1533)
- Minority ruled by mother **Helen Glinsky**, then after 1538 by **boyar duma**
- 1538–47: continuous strife between **Shuiskys** and **Belskys** clans
- Power changed hands repeatedly — imprisonments, exiles, executions, murders

**Kormleniye (feeding) system**:
- Boyars divided governorships, sharply increased demands on population
- Magnates seized service estates land as their own
- Gentry squeezed by large landowners, lay magnates, monasteries
- Litigation between servitors and monasteries common in 1540s

**Precedence litigation** (Figure 8.2):
- **Mestnichestvo**: appointment system based on hierarchical ranking of boyar families/prior service
- Before 1540: 3–5 cases/decade
- 1540s: jumped to 30 (Kollmann database)
- 1580s: huge jump to 200+
- Good indicator of intraelite struggle intensity

**1546–47 crisis**:
- Crop failure 1546 → famine winter 1547
- June 1547: great fire in Moscow → popular uprising (first since Moscow Principality founding)
- Rioters wrecked boyar mansions, killed ruling magnate **Yuri Glinsky**
- Young tsar **Ivan IV** took control
- Repented publicly in Red Square, promised to rule in people's interests
- 1549: convened **zemskii sobor** (Assembly of the Land, similar to Estates General) — normalized situation
- First two decades of Ivan IV's reign: "good" half
- Reforms of military/local government, new law code

**Growth of Taxation** (Figure 8.3):
- Good half of Ivan IV also saw prolonged external warfare
- Eastern front: defeated/annexed **Kazan and Aztrakhan Tatars** (1552–56)
- Western front: **Livonian War** (1557–82) — initial gains, ultimate defeat/territorial loss
- Wars also enriched elites (employment, booty)
- Wars extremely expensive → sharp tax increase
- Peasant consumption already at minimum (15 puds/250 kg) before Livonian War
- Extracting additional 3–4 puds (60 kg) → famine and epidemics
- Example: **Derevskaya District** (AHNWR 1971:II:Table 36)

### 8.4 Crisis (1565–1615)

**Oprichnina (1565–72)**:
- Ivan IV created separate institution **oprichnina** ("apart/beside") dividing state, elites, society
- Separate administrative structure for oprichnina and **zemschina**
- Governed by **boyar duma**
- Two sets of officials
- Countryside divided; many landlords transferred out, lands given to tsar's new servitors (**oprichniki**)
- **Skrynnikov**: 150+ magnates (almost all princely status) removed to Kazan Region
- Initially 1,000 oprichniki, eventually 6,000
- **Oprichniki** instituted terror against boyars, relatives, associates
- Towns (notably **Novgorod**) devastated

> "It looked as if a civil war were raging in the Muscovite state, but a peculiar civil war, for the attackers met no resistance"

- Oprichnina = coup d'état from above
- Ivan IV used one elite segment (and aspirants) to wage civil war against the rest
- Oprichniki later repressed in turn
- 1572: oprichnina abolished

**Demographic disaster triggers**:
- Poor harvest 1567 (specific trigger)
- Increased taxation (figure 8.3) on top of overpopulation-stressed system
- Peasants couldn't keep sufficient grain reserves
- Major famine developed Central Region winter 1567–68
- Grain prices increased 8–10-fold
- 1568: crops failed again, prices remained high, famine worsened
- 1570: famine followed by plague

> "It was one of those terrible epidemics of the Middle Ages that arrive roughly once a century and leave after themselves almost completely depopulated cities and villages" — **E. I. Kolycheva**

- "Great Famine" continued during plague
- Numerous cannibalism reports

**Military weakness from famine/plague/intraelite conflicts**:
- 1571: Crimean khan **Devlet-Girey** invaded with huge host
- Tatars attacked/burned Moscow, killed hundreds of thousands
- Territory around Moscow and south devastated
- 100,000 prisoners carried to Caffa slave market
- Late 1570s: Livonian War final phase → loss of earlier conquests + additional towns to Swedes
- War ended with peace treaties 1582 (Poland) and 1583 (Sweden)

**Catastrophe scale (northwest — best documented)**:
- **Derevskaya District**: ⅓ of peasant allotments (**obezhi**) deserted (famine and plague mortality)
- Other allotments abandoned (peasants unable to fulfill tax obligations)
- **Derevskaya District**: ⅗ allotments deserted (unknown mortality vs emigration proportions)
- **Bezhetskaya District**: lost 40% population to famine and disease

**Monastery estate conditions**:
- **Troitse-Sergiev and Iosifo-Volokolamsky** monasteries around Moscow
- Tatar invasion impact heaviest: 90% previously cultivated land deserted
- **Suzdal District (uezd)**: 60% deserted
- **Murom District**: 36%
- **Iuriev-Pol'sky District**: 18%
- Some desertion from peasants moving elsewhere, but magnitude couldn't be great (Muscovite frontiers unsafe in 1570s)
- Southern frontier: 3 major nomad invasions
- Volga Region: rebellion
- Likely numbers reflect true mortality scale

**Economic recovery signs**:
- Population decline → amelioration for commoners
- Daily wage increased sharply in 1570s
- **Vologda laborers** 1576: 3 dengas/day, grain cost 23 dengas/quarter → real wage **9.3 kg grain/day** (2.5× decade before)
- **Iosifo-Volokolamsky Monastery**: real wage grew 2.5×
- Skilled workers (carpenters, tailors): pay grew twofold

**Land rent decline**:
- **Quitrent (obrok)** on gentry estates fell by factor of 3 (from 10–12 puds to 3–4 puds per "soul")
- State-owned land: rents approximately halved
- Corvée obligations declined 2–3×

**State/elite resource extraction decline**:
- Demographic catastrophe didn't increase peasant exploitation (contrary to some historians)
- Significantly lightened burden
- But also: state/elite ability to extract resources via economic methods declined majorly
- Real taxes paid by households shrank 3-, 4-, or 5-fold (Shelonskaya District around Novgorod)
- Novgorod Land state revenues halved by 1576 — only **one-twelfth** pre-catastrophe level

**Gentry servitors impact**:
- Many estates completely lacked peasants to work land
- Only 7% land cultivated in Moscow District (**uezd**)
- 25% in Kolomna District
- **Derevskaya District**: >⅓ of servitors had no peasants
- Lacking resources: gentry abandoned estates
- Muscovite army (bulk = mounted gentry servitors) lost half its number

**Fedor's reign (1584–98)**:
- Ivan IV died 1584
- Son **Fedor** feeble-minded, government in boyars' hands
- Relatively peaceful interlude between oprichnina (1565–72) and Time of Troubles (1604–13)
- Probably due to exhaustion of warring factions rather than lasting solution to elite overproduction vs declining commoner population

**Root cause of instability**:
- Acute labor shortage, elite economic distress, state financial crisis
- Situation similar to Western Europe after Black Death (chapters 2, 4)
- Decreased labor supply drove wages up, rents down
- Nobility employed extraeconomic/coercive methods to maintain revenues
- England/France: attempts failed
- Poland/Prussia: elites successful in enserfing peasants (section 1.1)
- Two centuries later in Russia: elites also imposed serfdom under similar conditions

**Elite expansion during Fedor's reign**:
- However, enserfment couldn't resolve elite economic problems
- Russian army: 80,000 cavalrymen receiving annual pay (plus service estates)
- Every year 65,000 served on southern frontier against Tatar raids
- Not all gentry, but not all gentry received salary
- By late 1500s: at least 3× as many gentry servitors as under Basil III (when 20,000 cavalrymen served on steppe frontier)
- Reasonable assumption: overall gentry numbers increased by same factor (3+)
- Commoner population roughly same as under Basil III (1570s demographic catastrophe)
- Social pyramid extremely top-heavy by late 1500s
- Russian peasants couldn't support such great gentry numbers even if deprived of all agricultural surplus
- Contradiction solvable only by abating elite overproduction (happened first half 17th century)

**Enserfment process**:
- Not discrete event but process over many decades
- Key period: end of Fedor's reign (1584–98) and **Boris Godunov's** reign (1598–1605)
- Government under gentry pressure issued legislative acts:
  - Restricted peasant movement
  - Extended period during which fugitive serf could be forcibly returned

**Enserfment effects**:
- Gave landowners more power to extract surplus from peasants
- Example: **Iosifo-Voloklamsky Monastery** first attempt to increase corvée 50% met peasant resistance, rescinded
- Subsequent landlord initiatives supported by central authority
- 1601–3: corvée doubled in many monasteries by tsar's edicts

**Servitor estate conditions**:
- Fragmentary data, no quantitative statements
- Petty gentry had very few peasants
- Example: average servitor in **Tula Province** supported by only 4 peasant households
- Had to equip himself for military service yearly
- Majority of servitors compelled to deprive peasants of all surplus (no grain reserves for crop failure)

#### The Time of Troubles

**Early 1600s crisis**:
- Socioeconomic situation similar to 40 years earlier (1560s)
- 1560s: state tax press pushed peasants to brink
- 1600s: elite extraction was the driver
- Trigger: very poor harvest from cold/wet weather 1601
- Grain prices climbed immediately
- Spring 1601 central region: rye 30–32 dengas/quarter
- Following fall: already 60–70 dengas
- February 1602: 1 ruble (200 dengas)/quarter
- 1602: many peasants lacked viable seeds (early frosts 1601 damaged grain before harvest)
- Fall grain prices: 3 rubles/quarter
- 1603: good weather but fields empty, famine deepened

> "Three years of incessant rains" — some authorities proposed

- Reality: bad weather major factor only first famine year
- Climate served as trigger
- Catastrophe length/severity explanation: top-heavy social structure → relentless productive class oppression

**Famine impacts**:
- Far-reaching effects on population, state, elites
- First: vast suffering, enormous mortality shock to general population
- **Avraamii Palitsin**: 127,000 buried in Moscow alone
- Another witness: "⅓ of Muscovite Tsardom perished from famine"
- Starving peasants attacked wealthier peasants and servitor manors
- Fall 1602: banditry outbreaks epidemic in many regions

**State financial collapse**:
- **Boris Godunov** government made extraordinary efforts to alleviate commoner suffering
- Attempted price control: failed
- Used state grain reserves: sold stored surplus at half market price, distributed free bread loaves
- Finally: forced to spend huge amounts giving away coins and bread in Moscow, Smolensk, Novgorod, Pskov
- Example Moscow: government distributed food/money to ~70,000 people daily (large part from surrounding countryside migrants)
- Eventually handouts stopped due to depleted treasury

**Pool of disaffected counterelites**:
- Famine created huge pool of desperate individuals
- Petty gentry hit as badly as peasants
- Many forced to sell themselves into slavery to survive
- 1602: slave sales 9× greater than normal years
- Traded cavalrymen who sold into slavery not employed in agricultural/domestic chores
- Joined armed retinues of magnates as elite military slaves
- As famine lengthened: lords unable to support large retinues, cut military slaves adrift
- These individuals desperate, "armed and dangerous," very numerous
- Contemporary estimate: 20,000 former elite military slaves migrated to southern steppe frontier
- Joined ranks of disaffected cossacks and frontier servitors

**Rebellions and civil wars**:
- Unemployed military slaves, destitute servitors, runaway serfs, frontier cossacks = huge manpower pool for subsequent rebellions
- First outbreak: **Khlopko rebellion** (fall 1603) — large bandit band in Moscow region
- Finally suppressed after defeating government troops, killing commander

**False Dmitrii uprising (1604)**:
- More serious, ultimately successful in toppling state
- Led by impostor claiming to be Prince Dmitrii (Ivan IV's son)
- Started invasion with Polish magnate backing
- Probable: plot initiated/secretly supported by certain boyar factions

<!-- pp. 301-320 -->

## Chapter 8: Conclusion (continued from previous section)

### End of the Time of Troubles

- **Tsar Boris's army composition**: Drew heavily from southern frontier — cossacks, servitors recently joined by former military slaves, destitute servitors, and runaway serfs
- **April 1605**: Tsar Boris died suddenly, magnate coalition collapsed, False Dmitrii entered Moscow in triumph
- **Civil war aftermath**: Dmitrii overthrown and murdered by boyar faction led by **Vassili Shuisky** (1606). Shuisky became tsar but deposed in 1610
- Series of pretenders arose, including another Prince Dmitrii claiming miraculous escape from the boyars
- Popular rebellion led by **Ivan Bolotnikov** and foreign interventions (Poles, Swedes)
- Russian crown offered to Wladyslaw, son of king of Poland, at one point
- 1611: Internal fighting and external invasions triggered powerful unifying response among Russian elites and people
- 1613: **Zemskii sobor** elected **Mikhail Romanov**, scion of prominent boyar clan, to throne — ending Time of Troubles

### Post-Crisis Recovery Patterns

- **Famine ended earlier**: 1604 was good crop year
- Demographic catastrophe had usual positive effect on real wages
- **Servant wages in monasteries** increased 50% compared to prefamine years
- Rye cost 32 dengas per quarter — close to prefamine level
- **Population decline magnitude**: Probably not as great as 1560s-1570s collapse
- Real wages increased by factor of 1.5 (compared to 2.5 after first catastrophe)
- Peasant situation improved, enserfment process de facto rolled back
- **Laws tying peasants to land** continued to exist but were unenforceable in practice
- Very difficult to locate and bring back runaway peasants — beyond most gentry resources, no government agencies to help
- Once situation stabilized, government avoided further agitation among peasants
- On southern frontier, peasants given legal right to leave gentry estates

### Elite Numerical Decline

- **Cavalrymen serving on southern frontier**: 1580s = 65,000 annually; 1630 = only 15,000 elite servitors able to report for duty
- Long and intense civil war shrank elite numbers significantly

## Chapter 9: Russia: The Romanov Cycle (1620-1922)

### 9.1 Expansion (1620-1800)

#### Population and Economy

**Demographic Devastation from Time of Troubles**

- **Novgorod Land population**: 1620 = half that of 1582, one-tenth that of 1500
- **Troitse-Sergiev Monastery estates** (scattered across central Russia): Cultivated area shrank by factor of 10
- **Moscow Region**: Only 1/8 of arable area actually cultivated (1626-29 census); rest left fallow or sprouted forest
- These regions (worst devastated by Livonian War and Time of Troubles) saw total Russian population decline to lesser degree
- **1646 census**: 551,000 peasant households + 31,000 urban households
- Assuming 6 persons/household, correcting for 25% undercount: ~4.5-5 million total
- **Kopanev (1959)**: 1550s population was 9-10 million — intervening crisis reduced population by ~half

**Economic Recovery and Expansion (1620s onward)**

- Gradual economic expansion made possible by building 800-km-long **Belgorod defensive line** — protected southern frontier from Tatar raids
- Further south, fertile **Black Earth Region** colonized in mid-17th century
- Opening of new fertile lands for peasant colonization = factor of huge importance — removed threat of overpopulation until far future (abnormally long expansion phase)
- **Population 1646-1678**: Increased from 4.5-5 million to 8.6 million (within 1678 boundaries)
- Novgorod Land population more than doubled, though still 1/3 lower than 1500 level
- **Black Earth Region population growth** particularly rapid: By 1678 = 1.8 million (compared to 3.5 million in Central Region)
- South became important grain supplier to Central Region
- By end of 1670s: **1 million puds** (16,400 tons) of grain exported from south, government officials repeatedly reported growth of grain supply reducing prices

**Detailed Population Data from "Revisions" (1719 onward)**

- Beginning 1719: Detailed data from "**revisions**" — regular censuses of taxed population
- Important to account for substantial territory growth when interpreting numbers
- Focus on area encompassed by 1719 revision, excluding later gains (Poland, Ukraine, Baltic)
- **Central Region** (Moscow, Vladimir, Yaroslavl, Kostroma, Nizhny Novgorod, Tver, Kaluga provinces): Grew much slower than Black Earth Region (Tula, Ryazan, Kursk, Orlov, Voronezh, Tambov, Penza provinces)

**Population Growth Patterns (Figure 9.1)**

- Growth rates fluctuated — rapid increase punctuated by periods of much slower growth
- Slowdowns result not of overpopulation but of occasional redistribution of resources in state-elite-commoners system
- **Crisis of 1720s-1730s**: Sharp tax increase under Peter I caused famine of 1723-25. ~300,000 people (3% of total population) perished
- **Structural crisis of 1780s**: Caused by abrupt rent increase, led to famine of 1787-88

**Urban Development Remained Minimal**

- Abundance of free land meant peasants not stimulated to pursue crafts or migrate to towns
- Cities grew very slowly during 17th century — served primarily defensive and administrative functions
- **1652 urban population**: 247,000 males = 139,000 servitors + 108,000 *posadskie lyudi* (craftsmen/traders)
- **1678**: 149,000 servitors + 134,000 craftsmen/tradesmen — situation much the same
- Between 1719-1796: Urban population (within 1719 boundaries) grew by only 34%
- In more populated Central Region, urban growth more substantial: 61%
- **Mironov (1990:65)**: 1743-1783 population growth rate = 0.84%/year. Migration from rural areas only 0.04%, bulk of 0.8%/year growth came from natural increase in urban population
- Result: Rural population grew faster, share living in cities/towns shrank from 11.5% (1740s) to 7.1% (1830s)

**Peasant Living Standards in 17th Century**

- Contemporary sources indicate peasants quite well off
- **Yury Krizhanich** (1997:261, 286): Russian peasants "lived much better than in the Greek, Spanish, and other similar lands, where either meat, or fish are very dear, while firewood is sold by weight"
- **Adam Olearius** (1980:329): Great abundance of grain and pasture, large stocks of free cultivable land; in Russia one rarely heard about dearth

**Statistical Evidence of Peasant Prosperity**

- **Gorfunkel (1962:117)**: Post-Time of Troubles period = "golden age" of Kirillo-Belozersky Monastery's peasants
- **Kolesnikov (1976:301)**: Average crops gathered per capita in Totem District (*uezd*) in northern Russia = 460-520 kg
- 1680s-1690s: Average household in certain central Russian monasteries had 2-5 horses
- Peasant households on gentry land owned average of 2.2-2.6 horses and 1-2 cows
- **Starobelsky District** during 1660s: Average household owned 2-3 horses and 4-5 cows
- **Pskovo-Pechorsky Monastery** (1639): Average household = 5-6 people, 3-4 horses, 4 cows
- Even peasants who fled from center to south in 1660s were not paupers: Typical household owned 3 horses and 2 cows

**Real Wages and Consumption**

- **1640-47**: Daily pay of unskilled worker = 9 kg of grain
- **1660s-1670s**: Price inflation reduced it to 6 kg
- End of century: Grain prices fallen, real wage grew to **14 kg**
- Very substantial level, similar to European peasants at end of 15th century
- This favorable consumption level not exceeded in Europe until 19th century

**Anthropometric Evidence: Declining Heights (Figure 9.2)**

- **Boris Mironov (2004)**: Average height of army recruits declined by 4 cm during 18th century
- Decreasing tendency not monotonic
- Severe drop in quality of life during 1710s-1720s, but next 3 decades nutrition ameliorated (probably due to increasing pace of Black Earth Region colonization)
- Second half of century: Standard of living declined again
- Particularly severe decrease during crisis of 1780s

**Grain Prices as Population Growth Indicator (Figure 9.3)**

- During **Peter I's reign**: Prices increased threefold
- End of reign (1723-25): Famine and structural crisis
- After that, prices declined, general conditions more favorable (though another famine in 1733-35)
- **1740s-1750s**: Prices stabilized at low equilibrium due to regular grain flow from Black Earth Region
- New period of inflation began 1760s: Abrupt jump in prices during famine of 1766-68 (made worse by rising rents)
- Finally, increasing demands on peasants by gentry at end of 18th century prevented peasants from building grain stores during good years
- Result: **Structural crisis of 1787-88**

**Grain Price Trends and Monetary Inflation**

- Overall trend of 18th-century price change fits pattern predicted by demographic-structural theory
- Prices grew not monotonically but in spurts — affected not only by population increase but also by Black Earth Region colonization and currency manipulations
- Government introduced copper and paper money; money supply influence very substantial (money circulating per capita increased severalfold during 18th century)
- Can account for monetary inflation by dividing nominal price index by index of currency circulation per capita (Figure 9.4)
- Normalized price index (Figure 9.4) suggests real fall in prices during 1750s much greater than suggested by Figure 9.3
- Grain transportation from Black Earth Region had very real effect on Central Region grain prices
- This effect temporary — by end of 18th century continuing population growth pushed grain prices to new peak

#### The Elites

**Elite Wealth Devastation After Time of Troubles**

- Confiscations of Ivan IV and Time of Troubles delivered heavy blow to *votchina* (allodial) possessions of boyars and gentry
- **Tver District** example: 1548 = 318 *votchinas* averaging 370 desyatins of arable land; 1620s = only 197 *votchinas*, averaging 86.7 desyatins
- Overall area of allodial lands shrank by factor of 4.3
- Central Region: Average *votchina* had only 4.2 peasant households

**Pomestie System Crisis**

- *Pomestie* landowning system (estates held in return for service) also in crisis — not from lack of land but from lack of peasants to work it
- Wealthiest gentry (estates in Moscow) had on average 24 peasant households (including unmarried ones)
- Provinces much less favorable
- **Shelonskaya District** (1626-27): Average gentry servitor possessed 3.8 households with 6.2 male "souls," 35% of estates had no peasants at all
- Combined effect of fewer peasants and 2- to 3-fold decline in quitrents meant gentry incomes only 1/10 the level of century before
- **Swedish diplomat Peter Petreius** reported that right after Time of Troubles, gentrymen could not afford leather shoes and had to wear *lapti* (peasant shoes woven from straw)

**Gentry Numbers Declined**

- Nominal size of gentry cavalry during 1580s = 80,000, of which 65,000 actually served every year on frontier
- Comparable numbers for 1630: 27,000 and 15,000

**Gentry Incomes Remained Low Despite Recovery**

- Socioeconomic balance between strata not yet regained
- Gentry incomes remained very low, servitors insisted on more effective control over peasants (establishing serfdom)
- During 1640s gentry obtained what they wanted
- **Serfdom fully instituted** with *Ulozhenie* (Law Code) of 1649

**Serfdom Led to Growth of Barschina**

- **Tihonov (1974:202)**: Imposition of serfdom immediately led to growth of *barschina* (corvée)
- *Barschina* = one of chief causes of rebellion headed by **Stepan Razin** (1670-71)
- Uprising forced gentry to decrease *barschina*
- Interesting parallelism between events following serfdom imposition in 1590s and 1650s: In both cases, increased gentry pressure on peasants triggered popular uprisings
- Outcome of peasant rebellions very difficult: Unlike during Time of Troubles, elites not divided during 1670s, state relatively strong
- Uprising of cossacks and peasants speedily suppressed by government forces
- Nevertheless, in both cases peasant resistance resulted in at least temporary setback in landowning elites' ability to extract surplus

**Economic Conditions and Surplus Extraction**

- Long and difficult war with Poland (1654-67): Government attempted to solve financial problems by minting large quantities of copper money → inflation and rapid growth in grain prices
- Trade disruption, fall in real value of quitrents (because of inflation), increased expenditures forced gentry to increase *barschina*
- Postwar normalization of economic conditions allowed elites to decrease *barschina* and replace it with quitrent

**Serfdom Did Not Increase Corvée Demands in Real Terms**

- Imposition of serfdom did not lead to heavier corvée demands on peasant labor
- Quitrent also did not increase in nominal terms, though declining grain prices led to increase in real terms to 3.5-4 puds per soul
- Real profit of landowning elites was not from rent increases but from ability to increase number of peasants on their estates (result of new legal barriers preventing peasant escape)
- **Novgorod Land** example: Average number of male peasants per gentry estate increased from 15 (1646) to 26 (1678)

**Single-Holders (Odnodvortsy) Status Change**

- Mid-17th century: Substantial proportion of gentry did not have peasants (*odnodvortsy*, "single-holders")
- Proportion of single-holders among gentry particularly high in southern frontier regions
- Government aggressively recruited servitors among peasants and cossacks beginning late 16th century
- During 1670s-1680s: Southern servitors censused by government agents; in process, single-holders demoted from gentry to soldier rank
- Later, under Peter I, single-holders given status of government peasants (free peasants not assigned to any gentry)

**Gentry Stratum Restructuring**

- 1651: Gentry servitors numbered 39,000
- 1700: 22,000-23,000 gentry landowners (and serf-owners)
- Average gentry landowner had 19 peasant households and 60 male serfs
- Averages hide great degree of inequality in land holdings
- 47% of gentry were smallholders owning 1-5 peasant households; on average they had 2.4 households and 8.8 male souls
- By contrast, wealthiest 464 nobles (2% of total) owned 43% of all serfs, each owning on average 355 households and ~1,300 male souls

**Gentry Numbers Increased (1700-1737)**

- 1700-1737: Number of gentry estate-owners increased from 22,000-23,000 to 46,000
- Number of estates (some gentry owned more than one estate) grew from 29,900 to 63,000
- Population growth slow during this period due to increased state taxes and declining consumption levels
- Number of elites increased faster than general population did
- Average size of gentry holdings decreased from 60 to 40 male souls
- Result: Gentry incomes also decreased

**Nobility Size Relative to General Population Continued to Grow**

- Last half of 18th century: Size of nobility relative to general population continued to grow
- Within constant territory of first revision (census): Proportion of gentry increased from 0.5% (1744) to 0.59% (1762) to 0.68% (1795)
- Number of peasants per noble landowner declined, proportion of impoverished gentrymen increased

**Elite Consumption Levels Rising ("Europeanization")**

- At same time elite numbers grew, so did consumption levels
- Many historians attribute this trend toward luxurious living to spread of European influence, or "Europeanization"
- Total consumption by elite stratum had to increase, either at expense of peasants or at expense of state
- Struggle for resources between elites and state went on during reigns of **Anne** (1730-40) and **Elizabeth** (1741-62)
- **1762**: Nobility won signal victory when **Peter III** abolished gentry's service obligations to state

**"Gentry Revolution" of 1762 and Resource Redistribution**

- "Gentry revolution" of 1762 resulted in significant redirection of resources from state in favor of elites
- Process took form of declining real taxes
- Minting of huge amounts of copper money during **Seven Years' War** (1756-63) resulted in doubling of grain prices
- Government unable to increase tax rate (remained same in nominal terms) → real taxes halved
- Lightening of tax load on peasants gave landlords opportunity to increase quitrents (Figure 9.5)

**State Taxes and Quitrents Diverge (Figure 9.5)**

- By 1794: Inflation eroded state's exactions on peasants to 1/4 the level of first half of 18th century
- Slack apparently taken up by increased quitrents
- Data on growth of quitrent and corvée available for only a few estates; one is fortunately the huge **Yuhotskaya estate of the Sheremetevs**
- Government-imposed quitrent probably increased in parallel with quitrent of gentry-owned peasants (though at lower level)
- Likely that Yuhotskaya estate data reflect typical pattern of quitrent increase for other gentry estates
- Same trend identified by **Mironov** (1992: Table 6)

**Divergence of Tax Curves and Peasant Conditions**

- Until 1750s, curves of per capita taxes due to state and rents due to landowners moved in parallel
- During second half of century curves diverged
- As proportion of peasant surplus going to state decreased, it was not peasant who profited but the lord
- Economic and legal conditions of peasants worsened during this period
- Manifest of 1762 was final step that turned peasant into property of gentry
- End result: **Famine of 1787-88**, which forced landlords to reduce quitrent in real terms (Figure 9.5)

**Paul I's Response and Elite-State Conflict**

- **Paul I** (1796-1801) reacted to worsening peasant conditions by limiting *barschina* to 3 days a week
- Also attempted to reimpose on nobility the obligation to military service
- These initiatives triggered another conflict between state and elites, ended with coup d'état in which Paul was assassinated

#### The State

**State Weakened by Early 17th Century Crisis**

- Crisis of early 17th century weakened state power, led to collapse of tax system
- Reestablishment of government control = one of most important tasks for new dynasty
- Attempt to increase taxes during 1620s ran into resistance from both peasants and gentry (higher state taxes had to come at expense of rent)
- Gentry bombarded government with collective petitions (*chelobitnye*)
- Government had to retreat

**Taxes Remained Low in 17th Century (Figure 9.6)**

- Higher taxes rescinded not only on lands worked by state peasants (mainly in north and on Vyatka river) but also on privately owned serfs
- Overall taxation level mainly determined by tax rate borne by privately owned serfs
- 1670s: This level quite low
- Second half of century: Taxation rate grew, reaching 0.8 puds per soul — still far below burden peasants bore during time of Ivan IV (1.7 puds per capita)
- 17th century: State unable to recover taxation ability it had before 1570s

**Peter I's Reforms Redistributed Resources to State**

- Dramatic redistribution of resources from peasants and elites to state achieved only as result of reforms of **Peter I** (1682-1725)
- Capitation tax (*podushnaia podat*) introduced by Peter I replaced all previous direct levies
- After stabilization of prices in 1730s, capitation and salt taxes added up to 3.9 puds per soul
- Compared to pre-petrine period, taxes increased 5- to 6-fold (Figure 9.6), while military expenditures increased 2.5-fold

**High State Taxes Continued Under Anne and Elizabeth**

- High level of state taxes continued during reigns of **Anne** (1730-40) and **Elizabeth** (1741-62)
- "Gentry revolution" of 1762 redistributed resources in favor of elites (as discussed in previous section)

**Catherine II and Paper Money Inflation**

- Being unable to compensate for losses due to inflation, **Catherine II** (1762-96) kept state finances afloat by printing large amounts of paper money (*assignatsii*)
- Result: Runaway inflation
- During reign of Catherine: Prices increased threefold (compared to price stability under Anne and Elizabeth)
- Inflation cut into real revenues of state budget, forced government to print more money, and so on
- This vicious cycle persisted until reign of **Nicholas I** (1825-55)

**Real State Revenues Declined During Catherine's Reign (Figure 9.7)**

- Real per capita taxes halved during reign of Catherine
- This was compensated somewhat by population growth, which increased by 70% during this period
- Still, real state revenues at beginning of reign were less than at its end

**Paul I's Financial Measures**

- Financial measures undertaken under **Paul I** (1796-1801) attempted to compensate for devaluation of capitation tax
- Tax rate increased by 26%, from 0.9 to 1.2 puds per soul — still only 1/3 the taxation rate under Elizabeth
- Emperor increased quitrents of state peasants by 33-66%, depending on economic development of provinces
- Thus, peasants in Central Region paid to Crown 4.2 puds per soul, while in Black Earth Region the rate was higher: 6.7 puds
- Overall, state peasants paid Crown about 5× as much as privately held serfs
- Under Paul: Real state revenues increased from 2.6 to 5 puds of grain per capita

#### Regional Variations

**Central Region Enters Stagflation First**

- Different parts of huge Russian Empire inevitably developed at dissimilar rates
- Central Region = first one to enter stagflation phase (happened in 1730s-1740s)
- Minimal amount of land necessary to support peasant family during this period = 1-1.2 desyatins per capita
- Data from Crown estates within Moscow Province (*guberniya*) suggest that in many districts (Khatunskaya, Selinskaya, Gzelskaya *volosti*), stocks of land fell to 0.5-0.9 desyatins per capita
- Superintendent of Crown properties **Baron Rosen** wrote that peasants of Moscow Province were short of bread due to insufficient land and poor soils
- Crop failure of 1733 caused famine and flight of peasants
- Between 1732-1735: 1/10 of peasants fled from Crown villages of Moscow Province
- Another famine in 1742-43
- Administration of Crown Estates took steps to solve problem of overpopulation by moving peasants to Voronezh Province (14,000 resettled in 1745)
- Despite these efforts, not enough land for all peasants, and 1/7 of households had neither horses nor cows

**Recurrent Famines in Central Region**

- Recurrent famines and peasant flight from Central Region kept its population at constant level of 4.5 million between 1719-1744
- Population of provinces of Vladimir, Yaroslavl, and Nizhny Novgorod actually declined
- Scarcity of land, recurrent famines, and population stagnation all suggest Central Region was in stagflation phase at this point
- Arrival of stagflation hastened by tax increases under Peter I
- Whereas previously a peasant household could subsist on 1 desyatin per capita, higher taxes tipped it below sustainable level

**Economic Restructuring of Central Region**

- Conditions of stagflation compelled economic restructuring of Central Region
- First, paucity of land resulted in massive substitution of labor obligations (*barschina*, or corvée) with money obligations (*obrok*, or quitrent)
- By 1780s: Great majority of peasants in Central Region (62%) switched from corvée to quitrent
- Transition to cash quitrents gave peasants incentive to practice crafts and trades
- Up to 2/3 of peasants in Moscow District (*uezd*) supplemented agriculture with such pursuits

**Development of "Industrial Villages"**

- Because serfs were bound to land and could not migrate to towns, crafts and trades developed in "industrial villages"
- These rural craftsmen had to buy imported grain to feed families
- Economic system that evolved in Central Region during 18th century was dependent on interregional grain trade
- In other words, continuing existence of population in Central Region would have been impossible without rise of grain plantations in recently colonized Black Earth Region
- **Black Earth Region became granary of Russia**, while Central Region specialized as industrial region
- While population in Central Region stagnated, in Black Earth Region it continued to grow and, by end of century, exceeded that of Central Region

**Southern Landowners Encouraged Immigration**

- Southern landowners encouraged peasant immigration by offering reduced quitrents
- **Prince A. D. Menshikov** required quitrents of only 2-3 puds per capita
- This low level of quitrents persisted in southern parts of Black Earth Region (provinces of Voronezh and Kursk) until 1760s
- Peasants there cultivated large plots of land (~2.5 desyatins per capita), even hired seasonal labor migrants from central provinces

**Interregional Grain Market Formation**

- During 1720s, before industrial specialization of central provinces, overall volume of trade in grains = 2.5 million puds/year
- In 1780s: Grain traded on markets in only 2 Black Earth provinces (Orlov and Kursk) = 24 million puds
- Interregional grain market encompassing Central and Black Earth provinces formed in 1740s-1750s
- Main transport arteries = rivers flowing north
- Evidently, it was grain import from south that caused decline of rye prices in Central Region: Between 1740s-1750s prices fell by 20%

### 9.2 Stagflation (1800-1905)

#### Population and Economy

**Stagflation Timing Varies by Region**

- Stagflation began in central provinces of Russia in mid-18th century
- In Black Earth Region overpopulation developed much later
- Thus, temporal breakpoint proposed here (1800) is even more approximate than usual

**Economic Dynamics Reconstructed from Provincial Governors' Reports**

- Economic dynamics of Russia during first half of 19th century reconstructed from reports of provincial governors
- Complete data available for 38 of 50 provinces of European Russia (table 9.1a)
- These data allow us to estimate average consumption level of Russian population simply by dividing net production of crops per year by number of people
- This approach works until 1850 (grain export negligible in comparison to total production: 2.7 million puds/year in 1841-45, or about 2% of total)

**Population Growth and Declining Per Capita Consumption (Table 9.1)**

- As population increased during first half of 19th century, personal consumption declined from high level near 24 puds to level below 19 puds per capita
- Classic Malthusian mechanism in action — population growth overtaking increases in production, resulting in falling rates of consumption per capita
- Population growth rate also declined: From 0.91%/annum (1795-1833) to 0.58% (1834-50) to 0.49% (1851-57)

**Post-1861 Economic Restructuring**

- During first 2 decades after emancipation of serfs in 1861, economy of Russia entered period of deep restructuring
- One important driver of change: Building of railroads that connected interior provinces with seaports and international trade
- Average export of grain in early 1840s = 27 million puds/annum, but by late 1880s it had become 257 million, or 23% of net grain production in whole of Russia
- Second important trend: Growing agricultural productivity — yield ratios increased from 3.4 to 5.4 between 1850-1910 (table 9.1b)
- Third trend: Continuing population growth (table 9.1b)
- From point of view of personal consumption levels, these 3 trends essentially canceled each other
- As we see in table 9.1b, level of personal consumption slightly declined toward 1880s and then increased
- By early 20th century it was at about same level as in midnineteenth century
- In other words, gains of increased agricultural
<!-- pp. 321-340 -->

### Peasant Living Standards in Late Imperial Russia

**Per capita grain consumption** fell below the minimal sustainable level of 18.7 puds/year during the second half of the 19th century, despite grain exports eating up productivity gains from population growth. **B. N. Mironov** estimated 30% of the peasantry was chronically malnourished around 1900, with this proportion increasing during crop failures.

### The Effect of the Great Reforms on the Peasants

**Emancipation of 1861** drastically reduced demands on peasants—quitrent replaced with smaller redemption payments. However, former serfs lost land: average holdings dropped from 1.54 to 1.28 desyatins per capita in the **Black Earth Region**. Arable land yielded only ~14 puds of grain—insufficient to support one person after redemption payments and taxes.

**Former state peasants** fared better: owned 2.4 desyatins per capita vs. former serfs. But continuing population growth eroded even state peasant holdings: 1877-1905 average household landholdings fell from 15.1 to 12.5 desyatins. Former serfs worse: 8.9 to 6.7 desyatins.

**Social stratification** intensified (Table 9.2): poor peasants (59% by 1896-1900) vs. wealthy minority. Average consumption stayed flat, but growing proportion lived precariously. **Temporal variability in yields** created deadly crises: **famine of 1891** following poor harvest sequence caused 400,000 deaths. Poor harvests 1905-7 triggered revolution.

### Urbanization

Urban population increased from 9.9% (1863) to 14.4% (1914). Industrial provinces urbanized rapidly; agrarian provinces (mostly Black Earth Region) lagged behind.

### Elites

**Nobility numbers** continued rising through 19th century despite efforts to reduce the "noble proletariat." **Nicholas I (1825-55)** reclassified impoverished gentry as single-holders or state peasants, causing petty gentry numbers to decline substantially post-1838.

**Poland-Lithuania** (annexed late 18th century) had different structure: **szlachta** numbered 251,000 vs. 112,000 Russian nobles. Only 14 peasants per noble in Poland-Lithuania vs. 64:1 in Russia. Polish nobility took peasant land on massive scale, "compensating" with barely sufficient grain payments.

**Noble estate size** declined 1858-1897: from 234,000 to 478,000 nobles, but average Black Earth estates shrank from 176 to 104 desyatins.

**Conspicuous consumption** by large landowners intensified post-Napoleonic Wars. Content analysis of memoirs: lifestyle of 4/5 of large landowners described as "conspicuous luxury," "aggressive, unrestrained luxury," "ostentatious splendour," or "extravagance." Poorer gentry attempted to match standards, with disastrous financial consequences.

**Emancipation effects on nobility:** Sharp redistribution of resources from nobility to peasants. Quitrent and corvée replaced by redemption payments and state taxes. Although freed serfs had to rent land, on balance former serfholder revenues suffered.

Many landowners relying on corvée labor failed to adapt. 1862-1877: 11,000 gentry-owned estates contracted (8.4% of total). 12% of noble land lost to other estates (**sosloviya**). Ruined gentry bought by merchants and petty bourgeois.

**Nobility demanded reparations** from the state. One compensation: aristocratic constitution transferring power from monarchy to nobility. **P. A. Valuev** (1862): "until the nobility's aims are to a certain degree of participation in the administration satisfied, there will be neither peace nor truce."

Government created **zemstvo** (self-government) system in provinces (**gubernii**) and districts (**uezdy**). **Zemstvo councils** were elective organs from three classes: landowners, townspeople, peasants. Nobles dominated: 85% of provincial assemblies 1865-67 were nobles and merchants. Zemstvo handled local taxes but law and order remained with provincial governors.

**Education credentials** provided path to government service for impoverished nobles. Gentry youth entered colleges and universities en masse. Half of students in middle schools and universities were children of nobles and government officials. Most students very poor. Combination of abject poverty and exposure to **Western European social ideologies** (Marxism) radicalized students.

1860-1880: middle school students increased from 17,800 to 69,200; university students from 4,100 to 14,100. This expansion created the **intelligentsia** social stratum. **Elite overproduction** was the most important process underlying intelligentsia formation, half with personal roots in noble estate.

State unable to employ all graduates. Government bureaucracy increased only 8% from 119,000 to 129,000. Adding 52,000 new zemstvo positions still left most elite aspirants unemployed. Many found alternative pursuits: revolutionary activity. 61% of 1860s revolutionaries (**nihilists**) were students or recent graduates; 70% were children of nobles or officials.

Decade later (1873-79 arrests): 28.2% nobles, 16.5% clergy, 13.4% military. Only minority peasant origin (13.5%) or petty bourgeois. 38% of radical **"Popular Will"** leadership had noble origins.

**Post-reform noble impoverishment** continued apace. Government offered subsidized credits to slow the process. Despite aid, noble landholdings shrank (Table 9.6). Personal nobles doubled (Table 9.5).

1877-1905: petty gentry increased from 50 to 59% of total. These noblemen owned tiny parcels—on average 30 desyatins, barely larger than well-off peasant holdings. **Special Commission of 1892** noted these estates hardly sufficient to support expected **soslovie** consumption level.

### The State

**Nicholas I (1825-55)** resuscitated policies of **Paul I (1796-1801)**, attempting to reestablish centralized bureaucratic apparatus. **Crown revenues per capita** increased from 5.2 to 7.6 puds in real terms. Government unwilling/unable to increase direct taxes on serfs; relied on indirect taxes and increased rents on state peasants.

1833: Russia most powerful European state, army of 860,000 (1.43% of population) compared to 0.83% under **Catherine II**. However, **Industrial Revolution** transformed European balance of forces. Result: defeat in **Crimean War (1853-56)**, forcing Russia into social reforms.

**Emancipation of serfs** most important task after war resurrected elite fears of peasant uprising. **Grand Duke Constantine**, after reading **De Toqueville's** book on French Revolution: "if we do not carry out a peaceful and complete revolution with our own hands, it will inevitably happen without us and against us." Brother **Alexander II (1855-81)** expressed same sentiment to Moscow nobility: better to abolish serfdom from above than wait until it abolishes itself from below. **Great Reform of 1861** successful in reducing social tensions while redistributing resources from nobility to peasantry and state.

Emancipation allowed state to increase taxes, including **capitation tax** on former serfs—previously impossible due to nobility resistance. 1861-67: capitation tax increased almost twofold. 1863: government introduced real estate tax on urban properties. 1872: started taxing noble land. Although initially not large, introduction marked end of important noble privilege: **freedom from direct taxation**. Despite increases, state revenues stayed flat in real terms due to rapid inflation.

Geopolitical environment required army rearmament and huge railroad investments. State chronically short of financial resources, forced to borrow heavily. 1861-1880: borrowed more than 1.6 billion rubles. Result: 1885-95 interest on loans exceeded 40% of government budget—twice military spending.

Intensifying geopolitical competition forced further revenue increases. 1887-1890: new minister of finances **I. A. Vyshnegradsky** increased customs tolls and indirect taxes, raising tax burden to new level. 1897: **state monopoly on alcohol production** imposed, yielding quarter of revenues during 1900-1903. After 1890: real taxes exceeded and stayed above 10 puds per capita, allowing government to more than double military expenditures from 210 million rubles (1880s) to 490 million rubles (1900-1903).

### Sociopolitical Instability

Growing demands on serfs during first half of 19th century met with increasing peasant resistance. Majority of disturbances occurred on gentry-owned estates, reflecting growing difference between serfs and state peasants. **V. A. Fedorov** data: 59% of Central Region disturbances caused by new impositions (increased quitrent or corvée, land dispossession, harsh punishments). 20% caused by ownership change—often associated with worsening conditions or emancipation hopes. 15% resulted from emancipation rumors.

Average year: only 0.3% of Central Region peasants took part in such actions. However, massive eruptions in 1797, 1812, 1826, and 1848 caused concern among officials afraid accumulated social pressure could find outlet in general peasant uprising.

Main causes of unrest:
- **1796-97:** peasant expectations of serfdom abolition raised by **Paul I** reforms
- **1826:** hopes following beginning of **Nicholas I** reign
- **1848:** famine, cholera pandemic, emancipation rumors triggered by 1847 decree allowing peasants to buy freedom when estates sold for debt, plus European revolutions of 1848

Noble landlords feared disturbances would grow into "Second Pugachev Rising." Another high point: **1848 disturbance peak**.

Increasing pressure from peasant rioting led **Alexander II (1855-81)** to free serfs. 1857 report from **Third Department of His Majesty's Own Chancery** (political police): peasantry in "agitated state" from imminent emancipation rumors; massive unrest likely. This is precisely what happened next year.

Post-reform period: peasant disturbances declined, though unrest peaks toward century's end, usually associated with new emperor ascent raising peasant hopes for land reform.

### The "Fathers-and-Sons" Cycles

Demographic-structural pressures grew steadily, but sociopolitical instability increased not monotonically but in waves—period of roughly two generations (40-60 years). Instability grew during "liberal" reigns of **Alexander I (1801-25)** and **Alexander II (1855-81)** and declined during "conservative" reigns of **Nicholas I (1825-55)** and **Alexander III (1881-94)**, as seen in crime statistics.

Capital punishment in tsarist Russia reserved for most serious political crimes. Number of executions per year provides useful indication of revolutionary activity (and government suppression efforts). **Table 9.7** shows temporal distribution of "named executions"—executions of persons whose names were known to compilers. Underestimate of total, but useful for relative dynamics.

**Revolutionary intensity** showed two peaks during 19th century, followed by another in early 20th:
- **1820s:** Mild upheaval. **Decembrist Rising (1825)**: elite-led military revolt suppressed within single day, accounted for five executions. Six other incidences reflected peasant insurrection in Kiev Province and coup attempt in Siberia (1826).
- **1860s-1880s:** Much more serious period. Outbreak of anarchist campaign of bombings and assassinations culminated in successful assassination of tsar (1881). 1878-79: Russia experienced first significant industrial strikes in St. Petersburg.
- **1890s:** Relatively calm period
- **Early 20th century:** Instability began increasing again. Crime rates (declined late 19th century) started rising again. Student protests and disturbances almost continuous from 1898. Socialist Revolutionaries resumed terror campaign, assassinating officials 1902-5, including two ministers of interior and grand duke. 1905: Russia experienced first revolution.

## 9.3 Crisis (1905-22)

### Elite Fragmentation in the Decades before the Revolution

Elite fragmentation apparent during 1870s when part of impoverished nobility became involved in populist (**narodniki**) movement. **"Going among the people"** program: some 2,000 activists went to villages to become teachers and doctors, hoping to foment peasant revolution to overturn old order and establish socially just future. Campaign met little success among peasant masses; suppressed by police 1877. 1,600 populists arrested. After two mass trials, 200 activists convicted and received various punishments.

Following failure, populists turned to revolutionary conspiracy, assassination, and terrorism. 1878: **Vera Zasulich**, impoverished noble daughter, shot and wounded military governor of St. Petersburg in retaliation for ordering political prisoner flogged. Her trial evoked great resonance among educated classes (intelligentsia); sympathetic jury acquitted her. Trial showed huge legitimacy gap between government and broad elite segments.

Beginning 1879: **"The People's Will"** terrorist organization made seven attempts to assassinate **Alexander II**, last (1881) successful. People's Will consisted of ~80 local organizations in 67 cities with ~500 total membership. Directed by Executive Council, majority very young (most 25-27 years old). Nearly half of Executive Council (13 members) came from noble estate.

Another part of nobility expressed discontent in less radical ways. These elites coalesced into **Zemstvo-Constitutionalist** movement, drawing support from local self-government (zemstvo). Zemstvo-Constitutionalists aimed to replace tsar autocracy (**samoderzhavie**) with constitutional monarchy governing with help of elected representatives of "public," by which constitutionalists understood only upper strata. 1904: constitutionalists with representatives of liberal intelligentsia founded **Union of Liberation**. Liberal program did not propose measures for resolving agrarian question or improving worker lot. Focused entirely on struggle against autocracy and for constitution and political freedoms. Clearly, liberal political movement (at least early period) had no intention of seeking popular support.

Populist successors based strategy on mobilizing popular masses. 1901: several smaller opposition groups merged into **Socialist Revolutionary Party**. Social composition: 45% peasants, 43% workers, 11.2% intelligentsia. Upper echelons, however, 78% intelligentsia, most of noble origin, just as populist organizations of 1870s.

Another opposition group: **Social Democratic Party** (formed 1903). Program mirrored Liberal program in many ways, but added demand to limit working day to eight hours for worker support. Social Democrats originally also dominated by intelligentsia, but able to grow rapidly by attracting workers. 1905: party numbered 9,000 members—62% workers, 5% peasants, 33% intelligentsia.

### The Revolution of 1905

Triggering event: Russian defeat in war with Japan (1904-5), undermining government legitimacy and consolidating opposition forces. September 1904: key Liberal and Socialist Revolutionary leaders met in Paris, agreed to coordinate actions aiming to overthrow government. November: **Union of Liberation** organized zemstvo congress in St. Petersburg, demanding elected representative assembly. "It was an idyll of the liberal nobility leaders—they wanted representation of the propertied classes," **P. N. Miliukov** later wrote.

Zemstvo congress followed by 120 local assemblies in 34 cities bringing together nobility and intelligentsia representatives supporting congress demands. Opposition also attempted to organize popular demonstrations. January 1905 St. Petersburg: massive procession of workers supposed to hand tsar petition demanding constitutive assembly. During demonstration police fired at crowd, killed more than hundred people—incident became known as **"Bloody Sunday."** Massacre further undermined state authority, led to wave of strikes (mainly Polish provinces). Moscow strike lasted about week, involved 20,000 people, but violent altercations avoided. Industrial Vladimir Province: only 8,000 (out of 140,000) factory workers went on strike.

Prime Minister **S. Yu. Witte** remained optimistic about situation, saying 80% of populace not yet affected by revolutionary propaganda. However, Minister **A. S. Ermolov** warned tsar government could not count on nobility for support. Also outside pressure—French bankers recommended tsar make concessions to liberal opposition, warning otherwise difficult to secure further loans. Result: **Nicholas II** agreed to create elected representative assembly (March 1905).

Right wing of liberal movement welcomed concessions; pressure from opposition temporarily relented. Worker protests also declined. March: only one-tenth as many participated in political strikes as January. **P. N. Miliukov** wrote: "the revolutionary movement had not been able to penetrate the masses, instead there was a 'simulation of revolution' by the intelligentsia." Liberals on left wing wanted to keep pressure on tsar. During **Union of Liberation** congress (March 25-28), they added new demand to distribute state land to landless peasants. Where insufficient state land, proposed private land divided among peasants, former owners compensated with cash. Another demand: limit working day to eight hours. For representative assembly promised by tsar, leftwing liberals insisted election basis of universal and equal suffrage.

Imperial manifesto on state duma published August 1905 specified advisory organ elected through multistep indirect process. Liberal opposition directed all efforts to mobilize masses on their side. Chief player: **All-Russian Railroad Union (ARU)**—trade union of engineers, technicians, and managers created by liberals. Union represented middle rank employees recruited from intelligentsia. Practically no workers among 6,000 membership (total railroad workers 700,000). Although ARU members less than 1% of railroad employees, able to bring complete stop practically all railroads in Russia by disrupting technical services (dispatcher or telegraph services).

Grinding stop of all railroads gave powerful impetus to strike movement. Some factories: workers took advantage to present demands to owners. Other factories stopped simply due to lack of raw materials. Mobs of striking workers stormed still-operating factories and forced work stoppage. According to Inspection of Factories data: 519,000 workers on strike in October—about third of all workers in Russia. Thus, although strike initiated by liberal intelligentsia, its further development took life of its own. Workers of St. Petersburg created new organ for coordinating striking activity: **Soviet (council) of Worker Deputies**, imitated in other cities, later became one of most important forms of organizing and directing revolutionary action.

**Nicholas II** and government forced to capitulate. Tsar signed **October Manifesto** essentially transforming samoderzhavie (autocracy) into constitutional monarchy. Manifesto guaranteed civil liberties and provided for elected legislation (**state duma**). Manifesto addressed aspirations of liberal elites but did nothing for workers (who demanded eight-hour working day and increased pay) or growing peasant rebellion.

Railroad strike triggered not only general workers strike, but also mobilized peasants. **V. M. Gohlener** study of peasant movement in Saratov Province: peasant agitation began in villages near railroads then spread into hinterland. Additional factor: **crop failure of 1905**. Black Earth Region seven provinces: net harvest only half previous quinquennium. Volga Region similarly affected. Grain slated for export, stored on noble estates, presented irresistible temptation to millions of peasants without enough food for winter.

Long-term factor of peasant landlessness combined with temporary state power weakening and crop failure caused peasant uprising. Ministry of Internal Affairs data: between October and December 1905 peasants plundered around 2,000 noble estates (one-fifteenth of total). Some localities (Balashovski District of Saratov Province): practically all estates destroyed.

Unrest and disorder increased in cities parallel with rural areas, culminating in workers insurrection in Moscow (December 1905). ARU participated in railroad strike impeding troop operations (remained loyal to government). After several days, military able to get trains going and move reinforcements to Moscow. Ten days after it began: Moscow insurrection suppressed. Rural areas: mass disturbances also began declining after peak in November.

Peasant and worker uprisings served as graphic lesson of revolution dangers; majority of nobility abandoned liberal movement. Liberals expelled from zemstvo assemblies; nobility consolidated on platform of property preservation and against land reform redistributing land from nobles to peasants.

By January 1906: worker and peasant uprisings suppressed; active revolution phase over. Subsequent political conflicts played out within legislature framework (duma). Liberals' attempts to initiate agrarian reform resisted by government (took landlords' side), refused to consider private land alienation even with compensation. Conflict between duma and government led to dissolution of first two dumas. 1907: government able to obtain cooperative legislature by adopting election law giving disproportionate representation to nobility. Same year: public order largely restored.

**Revolution of 1905-7** largely proceeded along lines postulated by demographic-structural theory. During stagflation period elites fragment, resulting in rise of opposition groups. Popular immiseration enables counterelites to mobilize masses in struggle against state. However, counterelite and common people interests not the same. When elite leaders achieve their goals, they abandon revolution or even join government in suppressing popular uprisings, as happened in 1905. Furthermore, revolutionary experience of 1905-7 showed opposition the dangers of uncontrolled popular explosion. Although radicals continued to hope for social revolution, most were killed or driven into exile. Liberal opposition (representing largest elite segments) repudiated revolutionary methods in struggle with government after 1905-7.

### Sociopolitical Instability between the Revolutions

Revolution of 1905-7 forced government to begin reforms whose goal was regaining peasant support. Belated efforts largely ineffective. Suppression of revolution did not mean returning to prerevolutionary conditions. Peasant disturbances subsided gradually; their level during 1908-10 still an order of magnitude greater than before revolution. While rural disorder declined 1912-13, locus of instability shifted to cities, which experienced renewed wave of strikes lasting until World War I outbreak. Significantly, crime wave did not recede after revolution end.

Period of 1906-8 includes two revolutionary years; average criminality level correspondingly high (especially notable: great jump in crimes against private persons and private property after 1900). After revolution, criminality did not decline and for certain crime types actually increased. For example, number of crimes "against the government" (**protiv poryadka upravleniya**) in 1909-13 was greater than during revolutionary level of preceding period. This increase suggests revolution did not end in 1907 but continued in form of chronic and numerous (if small in scale) protest actions. Populace whose aspirations were frustrated during revolution not resigned to continuing state of affairs. Crimes against private property include arsons of landlord estates committed by peasants. 1910-11: number of arsons and small protest actions actually greater than 1907 (last year of revolution).

Government aware of tense social climate and how it could affect Russia's ability to fight war most considered imminent. In end, however, **Nicholas II** decided to enter war, although many contemporaries felt this decision doomed tsarism. Most pessimistic forecast made by **State Council Member P. N. Durnovo**, who (due to his position) had intimate knowledge of country's internal state. If war turns to be victorious, then fine and well, wrote Durnovo.
<!-- pp. 341-360 -->

## Chapter 9: The Romanov Cycle (continued)

### Durnovo's Prediction and War as State Test

**Pyotr Durnovo** (1922) predicted that defeat in war with Germany would trigger social revolution more extreme than the 1905 uprising. His forecast: defeat would lead to blame on government, antigovernment campaign in legislature, socialist mobilization of masses, demands for black repartition (land redistribution from landlords to peasants), and general division of valuables. A demoralized, peasant-influenced army would be unable to maintain order; "hopeless anarchy" would follow.

> "But if there is defeat, then a social revolution, with all its excesses, is unavoidable. All reverses will be blamed on the government." (Durnovo 1922)

- Logic: defeat against Germany would replay 1905 revolution aftermath but more extreme
- February 1917 proved to be a "revolution without revolutionaries"
- Most historians (including **Hobsbawm** 1962) believe Russia could have avoided revolution without WWI
- **Theda Skocpol** (1979) advanced similar argument about social revolutions
- **Jack Goldstone** (1991) countered: early modern states almost constantly at war 1550–1815, yet major wars were rare; state breakdown was sharply cycling with peak during peaceful mid-19th century

War as robustness test: during stagflation phases with fragile social structures, most states failed the test of war. Russia's defeats in **Crimean War** (1853–56), **Russo-Japanese War** (1904–5), and **WWI** were symptomatic of internal fragility.

### Three War Crisis Mechanisms (Nefedov 2005)

Three mechanisms translated war conditions into crisis:

1. **Military insufficiency** — armaments inadequacy led to reverses, undermining state legitimacy
2. **Fiscal collapse** — government financed war by printing paper money during stagflation, causing hyperinflation, market failure, disruption of provisions to cities, hunger strikes
3. **Social fragmentation** — stagflation pressures fragmented society; peasant landlessness and popular immiseration made peasant uprisings or conscript insurrections likely; elites alienated from state

### WWI Military Collapse

Russian army vastly inferior to German and Austrian armies in artillery and weapons. Supply of weapons and ammunition ran out; by beginning of 1915, quarter of new recruits arrived without rifles. Summer 1915: grave defeat when Germans advanced into **Galicia** (Utkin 1976, Riazanovsky 2000).

**Army demoralization statistics:**
- 1915 losses: 2.4 million soldiers (1 million POWs)
- Ratio of killed to surrendered: 1:3 (compared to 1:0.2–1:0.26 in German/French/English armies)
- Readiness to surrender indicated low morale, unwillingness to fight
- By beginning 1917: 1–1.5 million deserters
- "Soldier strikes" — military units refusing to advance against enemy
- Fall 1916: mutinies at **Gomel** and **Kremenchug** logistics centers behind front lines
- By beginning 1917, state lost much ability to control army (Nefedov 2005)

### Economic Collapse and Food Crisis

Third factor: worsening economic conditions. At war start, nobody anticipated bread shortages (**Kondrat'ev** 1991). Grain export forbidden; grain for alcohol production banned. Agricultural production within Russia should have been ample for army and civilian needs. Problem: distribution failure, indirect effect of government fiscal policy.

**Fiscal breakdown:**
- 1914–15 tax revenues: 2.9 billion rubles
- Military expenditures: 2.5 billion (1914) → 9.4 billion (1915) → 15.3 billion (1916)
- Total war expenditures: 30.5 billion rubles
- Internal loans raised 7.5 billion; allied loans 6.3 billion (Sidorov 1960)
- Loans financed less than half expenditures
- Government forced to print paper money to cover deficit → runaway price inflation

**Money supply, price inflation, food riots (Figure 9.12):**
- At first, prices grew slower than money supply
- 1916: prices accelerated and overtook supply, suggesting reduction in goods supply (especially food)
- Mechanism: rural grain producers observe rapid price increase, withhold supplies from markets to get better price later
- Urban dwellers suffer from bread deficit and high prices
- Queues at bakeries/food stores concentrate discontent, triggering riots
- Example: Parisian food riots during French Revolution

Food riots increased with rising prices. Government troops began sympathizing with rioters. First incidence of cossacks refusing to disperse crowds: Orenburg food riots May 1916. Later that year, nine more such cases (Kir'yanov 1993).

**Grain storage collapse:**
- November 1915: grain stores 65 million puds
- Spring/summer 1916: shrank as usual
- Fall 1916: not replenished, continued to decline to below 10 million puds by December
- 1916 harvest worse than 1915; producers withheld grain expecting better prices (Kir'yanov 1993)

Government employed coercive methods and confiscation threats to supply army, leaving almost nothing for civilian population (Nefedov 2005). January–February 1917: urban centers received only 20–30% of planned grain deliveries. Winter 1916–17: provisioning crisis reached catastrophic proportions. Memoirs describe "dearth of bread and huge lines at food stores" (Nefedov 2005).

### The February Revolution

Economic problems intensified as war continued; toward end of 1916, fiscal crisis resulted in disintegration of distribution system. Economic collapse soon followed by political collapse.

**Fall 1916 hunger protests:**
- Rising bread prices caused new wave of food riots and worker strikes in industrial regions
- First major hunger protest: St. Petersburg, October 1916
- Many soldiers joined protesters; rioters could be dispersed only thanks to guard units
- Winter provisioning crisis deepened

**Early 1917 starvation warnings:**
- Moscow Mayor **M. V. Chelnokov** sent four telegraphs to prime minister warning scarcity of food about to cause starvation, followed by protests and agitation among capital inhabitants (Sidorov 1960)

**Army starvation:**
- General Staff meeting December 1916: provisioning of troops about to deteriorate
- **A. A. Brusilov** (2001): "economy was in disarray, but we were told nothing could be done about this calamitous state of affairs"
- Food rations decreased: frontline troops from three to two pounds per day; troops behind lines only 1.5 pounds
- Lack of oats, horses starving → artillery lost mobility, could no longer advance; in retreat, loss of artillery and supply train assured (Brusilov 2001)

**December 1916 soldier protests:**
- Mitava operation: **Seventeenth Infantry Regiment** refused to advance against enemy
- Joined by several other regiments; disturbances spread through three army corps, involving tens of thousands
- Authorities regained control with difficulty
- Hundred most active protesters executed; hundreds more condemned to forced labor (Zayonchkovski 1938)
- Army rapidly running out of provisions; early February, northern front food stores sufficient for only two days
- Western front: flour stores completely exhausted, soldiers fed on canned food and hard biscuits
- Army on brink of mutiny; two infantry regiments on Caucasian front rebelled February 22–23 (Gavrilov 1991)

**February 1917 urban crisis:**
- First two months of 1917: Moscow and Petersburg received only 25% of food needed
- Mid-February press reported food rationing system imminent: adults entitled to one pound bread per day (children half that)
- News caused people to stock up, rapidly turned into panic
- February 14: thousands lining up at grocers and bakers despite freezing weather, hoping to buy loaf of bread (Leyberov 1990)

**February 23 hunger strikes begin:**
- Spontaneous hunger strikes and demonstrations began February 23
- By evening: 60,000 workers on strike, several clashes with police (Leyberov 1990)

**February 24–25 escalation:**
- February 24: 200,000 striking workers
- Police dispersed demonstrators, but they coalesced elsewhere
- February 25: demonstrations became quasi-legal; military stood by irresolutely, many cossacks fraternizing with protesting mobs (Startsev 1984, Leyberov 1990)
- Evening: **General Khabalov** (military commander of St. Petersburg) received telegram from tsar demanding end to turmoil
- Authorities reluctant to use force but compelled to issue fateful order

**Troops join revolution:**
- Clear in retrospect: giving order to unreliable troops would provoke almost inevitable uprising
- Great mass of St. Petersburg soldiers were peasants called up early 1917, waiting to be sent to front
- Former peasants had grievances against ruling order, unwilling to die for it
- Veterans among them (sent back after battlefield injuries) told new recruits about "firestorm of bullets and shells and terrible losses among frontline troops"
- "Soldier masses inspired by only one passionate desire—a miracle that would save them from necessity to go to slaughter" (Oldenburg 1992)

**February 26–27 mutinies:**
- February 26: troops ordered to fire at demonstrating crowds; same day **Pavlovsky Regiment** mutinied
- Mutiny suppressed, but next morning **Volynsky Regiment** mutinied
- Uprising spread to other regiments through chain reaction
- Column of mutineers marched across St. Petersburg barracks to barracks
- Regiments joined uprising one after another, with rejoicing and shooting in air
- February 27 morning: 10,000 mutineers
- Midday: 26,000
- Evening: 66,000
- February 28: 127,000 troops joined
- March 1: 170,000 — practically entire St. Petersburg garrison (Oldenburg 1992)

**Aftermath:**
- Subsequent events well-known: abdication of **Nicholas II**, rule of **Provisional Government** established by duma, violent overthrow by **Bolsheviks** (October Revolution)
- Bolshevik coup triggered general civil war lasting until 1921
- Eventually Communist Party reestablished central control, reconquered most territories of Russian Empire
- Civil war generally over by 1922: **Union of Soviet Socialist Republics** organized
- Exception: Central Asian region, where Soviet authorities suppressed native partisan movement (the "basmachi") only in 1926

## 9.4 Conclusion

### Applicability to Industrializing Russia

Demographic-structural theory formulated for agrarian societies; question arises whether applicable to industrializing states like Russia around 1900. Theory generally applicable because great majority of Russian population—80% in 1913 (Mironov 2000)—were still peasants. Industrialization affected economic and social dynamics and must be taken into account.

**Second half 19th century productivity growth:**
- Russia experienced substantial growth of agricultural productivity — one of most important preconditions of industrial take-off (Rostow 1990)
- Increasing productivity after 1870 (see table 9.1) substantially expanded carrying capacity, ameliorated negative effects of rapid population growth

### Territorial Expansion Impact

Another modifying factor with even more influence: **territorial expansion** and peasant colonization of conquered lands.

**Central Region stagflation postponed:**
- Central Region showed all indications of stagflation by 1730s–1740s
- Stagflation made worse by high tax levels imposed during **Peter I's** reign
- Even after tax rates lowered by **Catherine II**, position of peasants did not improve (increased gentry demands took up slack)
- If no **Black Earth Region** to colonize, Russia would likely have experienced next state collapse during Age of Revolutions, probably same time as France (closely synchronized 1400–1750; Turchin 2003b)
- Crisis postponed by a century
- Formation of interregional grain market between Black Earth and Central Regions allowed former to specialize in food production, latter to develop protoindustrial orientation

**Complex integrative trends 1620–1905:**
- Period 1620–1800 doesn't fit typical expansion phase dynamics
- Better to consider 1620–1905 as two integrative trends following one another:
  - **Expansion I** to 1730, followed by **Stagflation I** in Central Region
  - Meanwhile **Expansion II** continues in Black Earth Region until 1840s
  - General stagflation (**Stagflation II**) sets in
- Doubled-up integrative trends not uncommon in large countries
- Example: China during **Sung era** — carrying capacity doubled from 50–60 million (typical first millennium ceiling) to 110–120 million due to introduction of high-yielding rice varieties and colonization of southern China (Korotayev 2005)

### Crisis Phase Idiosyncrasies

Crisis phase of Romanov cycle had several idiosyncratic features, though beginning followed classic demographicstructural scenario.

**End of stagflation phase (1905):**
- Majority of peasants lacked sufficient land to feed families
- Elite overproduction deeply divided ruling class
- Majority of elites profoundly alienated from state
- All it took to trigger revolution was relatively trivial reverse in **Russo-Japanese War**

**1905 revolution dynamics:**
- Governmental policy demands frustrated by tsar's refusal to implement liberal reforms
- Opposition turned to popular masses for support, succeeded beyond wildest imaginations
- Railroad strike initiated by small liberal-dominated union led to general workers' strike, then peasant uprising
- Peasants' main motivation: acquire land
- Primarily attacked not state but land-owning elites
- Partly satisfied by concessions wrung from tsar; more importantly, frightened by extent and intensity of popular uprising
- Majority of elites abandoned struggle against state, many joined state in suppressing revolution

**Interrevolutionary period (1905–1922):**
- Level of sociopolitical instability continued high—not much lower than during revolution, much higher than before 1905 (figure 9.11)
- Whole period 1905–1922 should be considered continuous crisis period
- 1905 revolution did not resolve either of two main problems: peasant landlessness and elite overproduction
- State suppressed active phase with help of troops from Far East after Russo-Japanese War and liberal segment of elites
- Calm more apparent than real; continuing high level of peasant disturbance and worker strikes indicated temporary weakening of state authority
- Any temporary weakening could have sparked another social crisis
- Stress of **World War I** provided spark

### WWI Crisis Mechanism

Mechanism leading to next crisis involved three intertwined processes:
1. Collapse of state legitimacy from military defeats
2. Runaway inflation and market collapse leading to food riots
3. Increasing unreliability of troops

**Timing element:**
- Food riots in St. Petersburg February 23 triggered revolution to certain degree by happenstance
- Had to happen sooner or later; food riots started previous fall, continued erupting under Provisional Government (which solved provisioning problem no better than tsar's government)

**February food riots significance:**
- Should have been suppressed had government retained control over troops
- Unlike 1905, army decimated by military defeats; government could no longer feed it
- Soldiers who mutinied February 1917 were peasants drafted just months earlier
- These were peasants who demanded land in 1905 and didn't get it
- Many hated landlords and state that took landlord's side
- Confronted with choice: go into war meat grinder and die, or join rebelling workers
- Not surprising they chose latter
