- Symptomatic: whereas workers demonstrated with slogans demanding bread, peasants demanded "Land and Liberty"

**1917 elite fragmentation:**
- Elites continued to be fragmented
- Main opposition party (liberals) did not desire revolution (see Nefedov 2005: Sections 5.2, 5.4)
- Radicals (**Socialist Revolutionaries** and **Social Democrats**) decimated after 1905–7 revolution; those remaining driven into exile
- 1917: radicals completely misread revolutionary situation developing in Russia
- In January 1917 lecture in Zurich, **V. I. Lenin** stated he would never see revolution in Russia (Katkov 1997)
- At beginning of February Revolution, social revolutionaries and **Mensheviks** referred to it derisively as "spontaneous stomach movement"
- Apparently unaware how close this characterization was to **Malthusianism**

*Note: Following practice of Russian historians, all dates in this chapter given in Old Style.

---

## Chapter 10: General Conclusions

Main goal of book: determine how well predictions of **demographic-structural theory** map onto empirically observed patterns in historical societies studied. Focused on four fundamental variables:
1. **Population numbers** (in relation to carrying capacity)
2. **Social structure** (specifically, numbers and consumption levels of elites)
3. **Strength of state** (typically measured by fiscal health)
4. **Sociopolitical instability**

Empirical investigation attempted to measure dynamics of these variables as best as possible. Where possible, looked at data directly demonstrating dynamics; where impossible, searched for proxies. Secular cycles also affect many other aspects of historical societies; where such data available, included in analysis. Empirical investigation looked at eight secular cycles in four countries. This concluding chapter summarizes results and delineates promising future research avenues.

### 10.1 Population Numbers

Match between theoretical predictions about population dynamics and empirical patterns quite good.

**Integrative phases:**
- Characterized by sustained long-term population growth
- Ascending trend could be interrupted and even temporarily reversed when factors not taken into account by theory intruded
- Most conspicuous example: population declines in **Republican Rome** during First and Second Punic Wars
- Where data particularly good (early modern England), observed smaller fluctuations (e.g., 1560s in England)
- General rule: effect of such setbacks during expansion phase is temporary; losses made up quickly

**Disintegrative phases:**
- Generally periods of population decline or stagnation
- Dramatic population declines during crisis phases of **Plantagenet**, **Capetian**, **Principate**, and **Muscovy** cycles
- **Republican cycle** also ended with population decline in Italy (controversial)
- Two cycles (**Tudor** and **Valois**): absolute population numbers declined less dramatically, general trend stagnation rather than collapse
- Tudor case: population pressure on resources (population relative to carrying capacity) exhibited very substantial decline (35% between 1650–1750)
- Would not be surprised to learn comparable decline affected population pressure in Valois cycle
- Examination of **Romanov cycle** stopped at crisis stage; therefore no estimates of how population pressure declined over complete disintegrative phase (future work)

**Economic proxies:**
- Most useful proxy for population dynamics without doubt: **real wage**
- Where data good, inverse relationship between population pressure on resources and real wage very tight (see, for example, figure 3.10)
- Relationship holds only before Industrial Revolution
- After 1800 in England: relationship between population and real wages completely transformed

**Noneconomic proxies:**
- Most useful: **average height of populations** (Komlos 1980, Steckel 2004, Koepke and Baten 2005)
- Basic idea: population pressure on resources results in reduced levels of nutrition
- Inadequate nutrition of growing human beings (infants and juveniles) results in stunted adult stature
- Should be possible to observe population fluctuations indirectly by measuring how average heights changed with time (figure 10.1)

**Tentative chronology:**
- Earlier work (Nefedov 1999, Turchin 2003b) proposed tentative chronology for secular cycles in Western Europe (table 10.1)
- Remarkable degree of congruence between chronology and fluctuations of average heights in Europe (data in figure 10.1 not used in any way to construct chronology)
- Relative height of peaks in graph corresponds well with relative height of population peaks achieved during various secular cycles
- Population peaks during **Roman period** much higher than during Middle Ages
- Drastic population collapse of sixth century particularly well reflected in remarkable increase in average stature
- Population peaks of last medieval (**Capetian**) and first early modern (**Valois**) cycles matched and even exceeded those of Roman times

<!-- pp. 361-380 -->

## Chapter 10: Are There General Laws of Historical Dynamics? (continued)

### Testing Theory Against Empirical Data

- While theory doesn't explain all instances of instability, its prediction of alternating secular trends (stability vs. instability) is supported in all eight empirical case studies
- Theory successfully formalized into mathematical models (Turchin 2003b: Chapter 7)
- Produced specific, quantitative predictions about demographic, economic, social, and political variables
- Not all predictions confirmed, but this is expected in natural sciences too
- Where theory failed (revenue dynamics during disintegrative phases), it failed in interesting ways that suggest improvements
- Observed regularities point to general processes that could inform theory refinement

### The Neo-Malthusian Principle

- **Core mechanism**: During sustained population growth, if agrarian output doesn't keep pace, predictable price trends emerge
  - Rising prices for basic foodstuffs, energy, and land
  - Falling real wages for labor
- Simple consequence of supply and demand laws
- Supply of labor increases; if demand is limited (as in agrarian economies), labor price inevitably decreases
- Pattern observed in all empirical studies with varying clarity depending on data quality
- **Best illustration**: Figure 3.10b showing close relationship between population pressure on resources and real wages in **England, 1150-1800**

### Elite Dynamics and Overproduction

- Principal wealth source in agrarian societies: **land**
- Elite landowners profit from overpopulation in two ways:
  1. **As consumers of labor**: Need peasants for farming, servants for domestic work, craftsmen/artisans for status consumption
  2. **As producers**: Land produces food and commodities (fuel, raw materials) whose demand rises with population
- Items elites consume become cheaper while items they produce become more valuable
- Favorable economic conjuncture means:
  - Elite numbers increase (biological reproduction + upward social mobility)
  - Elites become accustomed to ever-greater consumption levels
- Elite numbers and appetites eventually outgrow their "carrying capacity" (based on commoner labor)
- **Principle of elite overproduction**: Just as overpopulation immiserates common people, elite overproduction immiserates elites (relatively, not absolutely—relative to consumption standards needed to maintain elite status)

### Phase Lag in Elite Overproduction

- **Key temporal pattern**: Rate of elite overproduction lags behind general population growth rate
- Population growth peaks during expansion phase
- Elite overproduction develops during stagflation phase
- Empirical support from case studies in the book

### Three Principal Causes of Sociopolitical Instability

1. **Overpopulation**
2. **Elite overproduction**
3. **Fiscal crisis of the state**

- As discussed in section 10.4, some causal factors more important than others
- **Elite overproduction** appears consistently associated with high instability across all eight cases examined
- **Overpopulation** results in popular immiseration and discontent, but as long as elites remain unified:
  - Peasant insurrections have little chance of success
  - Slave rebellions speedily suppressed
  - Worker uprisings fail
- During disintegrative periods, often substantial lag between population decline and restoration of internal peace
- **Fiscal crisis** usually present but sometimes missing as triggering factor for civil war (see section 10.4)
- Overpopulation and fiscal crisis are important contributing factors, but **elite overproduction plays dominant role** in internal warfare
- Elite overproduction leads to:
  - Intraelite competition
  - Fragmentation
  - Conflict
  - Rise of **counterelites** who mobilize popular masses in struggle against existing order

### Additional Generalizations

- Many more potential generalizations arise from recurrent patterns in historical cases studied
- Example: Shifts in social mood from desire for change to yearning for peace and stability
  - Plays important role in creating peaceful interludes during disintegrative trends (fathers-and-sons cycles)
  - Also in ending disintegrative trend, reversing to start new secular cycle
- Pattern could potentially be quantified by analyzing content of speeches, political writings, newspaper editorials
- Remains a task for future research

### Optimism About History as Science

- Authors believe quantitative empirical estimates possible for many variables needed to test theories of historical dynamics
- Models, particularly **demographic-structural theory**, have matured to point where they can make quantitative, testable predictions
- Many predictions supported by data
- Where predictions failed, often in interesting ways suggesting further theory development
- Historical process is very complex with severe data limitations
- Nevertheless, possible to apply standard scientific approach to study of history
- **Authors are optimistic about future prospects of history as science**

### Three Preliminary Generalizations ("Laws")

> "Perhaps 'laws of history' will never attain the same level of precision as that achieved in some areas of physics; the future will show. But it is possible to formulate some of the general principles of the theory that have received broad empirical support, if in a more qualitative fashion."

**1. The Neo-Malthusian Principle**

- During periods of sustained population growth, if agrarian economy output doesn't keep pace with population:
  - Rising prices for basic foodstuffs, energy, land
  - Falling real wages for labor
- Trends are consequence of supply and demand law
- As labor supply increases with limited demand (agrarian economies), labor price decreases
- Pattern seen in all empirical studies with greater or lesser clarity

**2. The Principle of Elite Overproduction**

- Favorable economic conjuncture for elites during population growth means:
  - Elite numbers increase from biological reproduction and upward social mobility
  - Elites become accustomed to greater consumption levels
- Elite numbers and appetites outgrow carrying capacity
- Results in large segments of elites becoming impoverished (not absolutely, but relative to elite status standards)
- **Key temporal prediction**: Rate of elite overproduction should lag behind general population growth rate
  - Population growth peaks during expansion phase
  - Elite overproduction develops during stagflation phase
- Empirical support provided by case studies in book

**3. The Primacy of Elite Overproduction in Causing Instability**

- Three principal causes of disintegrative trend onset:
  1. Overpopulation
  2. Elite overproduction
  3. Fiscal crisis of state
- Some causal factors relatively more important than others
- **Elite overproduction** always associated with high instability (in all eight examined cases)
- **Overpopulation** results in popular discontent, but unified elites speedily suppress peasant/slave/worker uprisings
- During disintegrative periods, often substantial lag between population density reaching low level and restoration of internal peace
- **Fiscal crisis** usually present but sometimes missing as triggering factor (section 10.4)
- Overpopulation and fiscal crisis are important contributors
- **Dominant role in internal warfare**: Elite overproduction leading to intraelite competition, fragmentation, conflict, and rise of counterelites who mobilize popular masses

### Future Research Directions

- More generalizations possible from recurrent patterns noted in historical cases
- Example: General principle underlying social mood shifts (desire for change ↔ yearning for peace/stability)
  - Creates relatively peaceful interludes during disintegrative trends
  - Ends disintegrative trend, reversing to start new secular cycle
  - Could potentially be quantified through content analysis of speeches, political writings, newspaper editorials

### Concluding Thoughts

- Quantitative empirical estimates obtainable for many variables needed to test theories of historical dynamics
- Models and demographic-structural theory have matured enough for quantitative, testable predictions
- Many predictions supported by data
- Where predictions failed, often in interesting ways suggesting further development
- Historical process is very complex with severe data limitations
- Despite limitations, possible to apply standard scientific approach to study of history
- **Authors express optimism about future prospects of history as science**
